**Proposed Solution: Ideal Parent Figure Protocol**

- Proposed method for reshaping internal working models by creating new, fresh, and positive attachment representations.
- Detailed description in Chapter 8 of the book.

### Attachment and Affective Disorders AFFECTIVE DISORDERS AND SUICIDALITY

**Attachment and Affective Disorders**

**Affective Disorders and Suicidality**

- Mikulincer and Shaver (2007) reviewed studies on attachment quality in adulthood and depressive symptoms:
  - Secure attachment linked to lower levels of depression.
  - Anxious attachment (preoccupied or elevated anxiety) associated with higher levels of anxiety and depression.
  - Avoidant attachment (dismissing or avoidance) linked to about 50% having higher levels of anxiety and depression.
- **Studies on Attachment Dispositions and Depressive Symptoms** (Ein-Dor & Doron, 2015):
  - Over 100 studies examined links between attachment dispositions and depressive symptoms.
  - Dismissing or preoccupied classification on the AAI and self-reported attachment avoidance or anxiety associated with more depressive symptoms.

**Longitudinal Study: Mothers' Attachment Style, Mental Health, and Children’s Emotional Vulnerabilities (Berant et al., 2008)**

- Investigated interplay among mothers’ attachment style, mental health, marital satisfaction, and children’s emotional vulnerabilities.
- No unique contribution of mother's attachment anxiety level or severity of child's CHD to changes in mental health by Time 3.
- Mothers with avoidant attachment at the time of their child’s diagnosis had more symptoms than expected at T3.
- Higher mother's avoidance or anxiety score linked to less positive children's explicit self-concept 7 years later.
- Mother's attachment anxiety seemed to predispose children who suffered from severe CHD to develop a less positive explicit self-concept.

**Studies on Adolescents and Depressive Symptoms (Kobak et al., 1991; Cole-Detke & Kobak, 1996)**

- Insecure attachment in general, and anxious-preoccupied attachment specifically, linked to higher levels of depressive symptoms.
- Depressed teens perceived higher levels of maternal dominance and expressed higher levels of dysfunctional anger during problem-solving episodes.
- Women with primarily hyperactivating (anxious-preoccupied) attachment strategies had significantly higher levels of depressive symptoms than those with deactivating, dismissing strategies.

**Studies on Patients with Borderline Personality Disorder and Dysthymic Disorder (Patrick et al., 1994; Fonagy et al., 1996)**

- Dysthymic patients had a significant representation of preoccupied attachment and unresolved status with respect to trauma compared to borderline patients.
- Patients with major depressive disorder (MDD) were more likely to be secure than either bipolar or dysthymic patients.
- Scores on the AAI Anger scale were significantly higher for the MDD group than for the bipolar and dysthymic groups.

**Studies on Suicidality (DeJong, 1992; Adam et al., 1996; Riggs & Jacobvitz, 2002)**

- Individuals with a history of suicidality had less secure attachment to their parents as measured by the IPPA and compared to nondepressed students.
- Unresolved adults were more likely to report emotional distress, suicidal ideation, and substance abuse than those with resolved status.
- Preoccupied adults reported a significantly higher likelihood of suicidal ideation than those with other attachment classifications.
- Dismissing women had significantly lower levels of depression.
- Unresolved status for trauma or loss reported greater suicidal ideation, emotional distress, and substance abuse than those with resolved status.

### ATTACHMENT AND BIPOLAR DISORDER

**Attachment and Bipolar Disorder**

- Berry, Barrowclough, and Wearden (2007) reviewed studies on attachment status in patients with psychosis, including bipolar disorder.
  - Higher levels of insecure attachment, specifically dismissing attachment, in samples with psychosis compared to controls.
  - Schizophrenia patients had higher levels of insecurity than those with affective diagnoses, such as bipolar disorder and major depression.

**Studies on Attachment and Bipolar Disorder**

- Dozier (1990) gave the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) to 42 psychotic patients, 25 with bipolar disorder:
  - Patients with bipolar disorder had less secure attachment than schizophrenics using Q-sort method.
  - Dismissing attachment associated with externalizing focus in bipolar patients.
  - Psychotic patients with dismissing strategies had more symptoms, while those with hyperactivating strategies reported greater symptom severity.

- Dozier et al. (1991) assessed AAI transcripts of 21 schizophrenics, 11 bipolar patients, and 8 major depressive disorder patients:
  - Narratives of bipolar patients were more likely to be classified as Ds.

**Findings from the Text**

- In a study mentioned earlier, Fonagy et al. (1996):
  - Bipolar disorder patients were more likely to be classified as Ds (38%) than MMD (7%) or dysthymic patients (14%).
  - Narratives of bipolar patients characterized by derogation during hypomanic or manic states.

- In an orphanage study:
  - 14 of 45 subjects had a type II bipolar disorder diagnosis.
  - No bipolar type I or psychotic bipolar subjects in the sample.
  - Bipolar type II subjects were significantly more likely to have insecure than secure attachment on the AAI.
  - Most AAIs were scored as disorganized (CC).
- Previous studies using the AAI identified Ds classification as overrepresented in bipolar (mostly type I) subjects, but this study found an overrepresentation of disorganization (CC) in bipolar type II subjects.

**Attachment and Somatic Symptom Disorders, Factitious Disorders, and Malingering** (Not directly related to the text)
- The text does not provide information on attachment and somatic symptom disorders, factitious disorders, or malingering.

### SOMATIC SYMPTOM DISORDERS

**Somatic Symptom Disorders**

- Waldinger, Schulz, Barsky, and Ahern (2006) investigated the role of insecure attachment in adult somatization.
  - Data set consisted of couples with abuse histories and recent violence.
  - Used Somatic Symptom Inventory (SSI), Relationship Scales Questionnaire (RSQ), and Minnesota Multiphasic Personality Inventory (MMPI) Hypochondriasis scale and the Hopkins Symptom Checklist (HSCL) Somatization scale.
  - 56% of women, 17% of men reported childhood trauma.
  - Women reported more emotional abuse, men reported more physical abuse.
  - Trauma correlated with somatization for both genders.
  - Childhood trauma shapes adult attachment and influences somatization process.

**Assessing Attachment in Medical Patients**

- Maunder and Hunter (2009) reviewed methods for assessing attachment style.
  - Important in understanding symptom presentations in medical patients.
  - Assessing attachment may help adapt medical care to patient's strengths and vulnerabilities.

**Attachment Style and Somatization in Female Primary Care Patients**

- Ciechanowski, Walker, Katon, and Russo (2002) studied female primary care patients.
  - Anxious-preoccupied and fearful attachment styles related to symptom reporting and health care utilization.
  - Anxious-preoccupied women reported more symptoms, had more visits, and higher healthcare costs.
  - Fearfully attached women had least number of visits and lowest healthcare costs.

**Attachment Representations in Patients with Somatoform Disorders**

- Waller, Scheidt, and Hartmann (2004) used the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) to investigate attachment representations in patients with somatoform disorders.
  - Significantly more somatoform patients were classified as insecure on AAI.
  - Dismissing somatoform patients had less frequent physician visits but greater number of hospital admissions.
  - Preoccupied somatoform patients had more physician visits and fewer hospitalizations.
  - Somatization correlated positively with dismissing attachment.

**Attachment in Patients with Somatoform Pain Disorder**

- Neumann, Nowacki, Roland, and Kruse (2011) gave AAI to patients with somatoform pain disorder and control subjects.
  - All somatoform pain patients had insecure attachment.
  - Significantly lower parental loving scores and higher parental rejection scores on AAI parenting scales for somatoform patients.
  - Somatoform patients had significantly higher scores on angry preoccupation on AAI state-of-mind scales.
  - Overall coherence of transcript and mind was significantly lower, unresolved status with respect to trauma was significantly higher in somatoform patients than controls.

**Attachment and Alexithymia in Patients with Idiopathic Spasmodic Torticollis**

- Scheidt et al. (1999) administered AAI and Toronto Alexithymia Scale to patients with idiopathic spasmodic torticollis and control subjects.
  - Dismissing attachment was significantly more prevalent in the torticollis group than in controls.
  - Alexithymia was also significantly more prevalent in the torticollis group than in controls.
  - Conclusion: Alexithymia in adults is significantly interrelated with mental representation of attachment.

**Attachment and Conversion Disorder in Children**

- Kozlowska and Williams (2009) studied attachment in children with conversion disorder using Dynamic-Maturational Model assessment system.
  - Twelve children had inhibitory attachment strategy, twelve had excitatory attachment strategy, four had alternating inhibitory/excitatory attachment strategy.
  - Attachment style related to type of conversion symptom: Negative CD symptoms related to inhibitory attachment style, positive CD symptoms related to excitatory attachment style.

### FACTITIOUS DISORDERS

**Factitious Disorders**

- Originally part of the "hysteria" category in DSM-II (1978)
- Hyler and Spitzer argued for greater conceptual clarity and empirical reliability
- Split hysteria into four categories: (1) histrionic personality disorder, (2) somatoform disorders, (3) dissociative disorders, and (4) factitious disorders

**Factitious Disorders vs. Somatoform Disorders**

- Factitious disorders involve voluntary production of physical or mental symptoms for attention
- Somatoform disorders involve involuntary experience of physical symptoms
- Conceptually separated in DSM-III, but naturally covary for some patients

**Definition and Characteristics of Factitious Disorders**

- Artificial production or simulation of a disease or history
- Involves deliberate deception and intentional self-harm
- Three essential clinical features: pathological lying, recurrent simulated illnesses, assuming sick role
- Compulsive need for attention and caretaking
- Interrelated with attachment disturbance

**Etiology of Factitious Disorders**

- Developmental disturbance
- Early childhood deprivation or difficult relationships with parents
- Abnormal illness behavior arises from problems in attachment, bonding, and caretaking
- Arrested self and self-esteem development
- Insecure attachment and unresolved trauma or loss are likely important factors
- Narcissistic pathology and disorders of self-regulation common

**Consequences of Factitious Disorders**

- Unstable, protean sense of self
- Vulnerability to self-fragmentation
- Impostor identity
- Adaptive attempt to prevent total fragmentation of an unstable sense of self by asserting and reinforcing false identities (some experts' view)

### MALINGERING

**Study by Pianta, Egeland, and Adam (1996): Attachment and Validity Scales on MMPI-2**

* Main purpose: Examine relationship between self-reported psychiatric symptoms on MMPI-2 and attachment status
* Valuable data emerged about attachment's relation to validity scales

**Attachment and Validity Scales**

* Dismissing attachment:
  * Assessed with AAI Q-sort method
  * Reported little psychiatric distress
  * Associated with minimization or "fake" good profile on MMPI-2
* Preoccupied attachment:
  * Highest on a range of psychiatric symptoms
  * Associated with exaggeration or "fake" bad profile on MMPI-2
  * Significantly more likely to score in elevated range on Response Infrequency (F) scale
  * Portray extreme psychological and emotional distress, possibly exaggerating
* All invalid MMPI-2 profiles had insecure attachment on AAI, mostly preoccupied

**Clinical Scale Scores for Preoccupied Group**

* Significantly higher than dismissing and autonomous groups on:
  * Psychopathic Deviation
  * Paranoia
  * Schizophrenia scales
* Indicates signs of impulsivity, insensitivity, suspiciousness, hostility, feelings of persecution, isolation, self-preoccupation, and inferiority

**MMPI-2 Profiles for Dissociative Coping with Trauma**

* Significant elevations on Infrequency (F) scales and Schizophrenia (Sc) scale common in subjects with dissociative coping
* Important to assess raw and T scores on F, F-back, and F-p scales, as well as use other tests for discrimination
* Disorganized (CC) status more representative of significant elevations of F and Sc than preoccupied status on AAI

**Limitations and Additional Findings**

* Pianta et al. did not use five-way classification, many preoccupied subjects may have had a combination of preoccupied and dismissing attachment strategies
* Subjects with preoccupied attachment and unresolved status for trauma or loss had elevation on Sc scale, possibly related to dissociation

**Orphanage Study: Attachment, Malingering Probability Scale (MPS), and Structured Interview of Reported Symptoms (SIRS)**

* Insecure attachment and low coherence of mind significantly related to increased probability of exaggerating or fabricating psychiatric symptoms
* Factitious individuals and certain malingerers have insecure attachment and histories of chronic illnesses, learned to produce symptoms for unmet attachment needs satisfaction from medical system.

### Trauma-Related Disorders

**Trauma-Related Disorders**

* **Posttraumatic Stress Disorder (PTSD)**
  * Pielage, Gerlsma, and Schaap (2000) study on attachment styles and psychopathology:
    * Secure attachment negatively correlated with experienced stressful events and psychological symptoms
    * Fearful and preoccupied attachment positively correlated with stressful events and psychological symptoms
    * No significant correlation in dismissing category
  * Attachment theory suggests that insecure attachment styles increase vulnerability to psychological symptoms, but reverse causality is also possible
* Alexander (1993) study on incest victims:
  * Significant differences in endorsement of attachment styles between victims and normative group
  * Secure attachment associated with least avoidance on Impact of Event Scale's Avoidance scale
* Whiffen, Judd, and Aube (1999) study on childhood sexual abuse and depression:
  * No correlation between depressive symptoms and a history of sexual abuse
  * Women with more severe abuse history had elevated attachment anxiety while maintaining confidence in partners' dependability
* Ein-Dor et al. (2010) study on veterans and their wives:
  * Higher attachment anxiety in veterans and their wives correlated with PTSD and secondary traumatic stress severity
  * Avoidant status of veterans and their wives associated with PTSD and secondary trauma only in couples with a former captive soldier
* Fraley et al. (2006) study on adult attachment style and psychological adaptation after the World Trade Center attacks:
  * Insecure attachment not related to initial or changed levels of PTSD or depression symptoms
  * Secure individuals exhibited modest initial levels of PTSD symptoms that decreased over time, and lower symptoms at both assessments compared to insecure individuals
* Wallis and Steele (2001) study on adolescents hospitalized on psychiatric units:
  * All participants had experienced childhood loss or abuse
  * Attachment was measured using the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI)

**Key Concepts:**

* Secure attachment is negatively correlated with stressful events and psychological symptoms.
* Fearful and preoccupied attachment styles are positively correlated with stressful events and psychological symptoms.
* Insecure attachment may increase vulnerability to psychological symptoms, but reverse causality is also possible.
* Childhood trauma experiences can influence attachment style development.
* Different attachment styles have varying correlations with PTSD and depression symptoms.

### Dismissing attachment and unresolved status with respect to trauma or loss were overrepresented in the sample: F, 10%; Ds, 51%; E, 28%; CC, 10%; and U

**Study Findings on Attachment and Trauma in Adolescents and Adults**

**Adolescent Sample:**
- Dismissing attachment and unresolved status overrepresented: F (10%), Ds (51%), E (28%), CC (10%), Ud (59%).
- High level of loss and trauma in childhood histories.
- Unresolved loss more likely to be resolved for those experiencing it alone compared to those with a combination of loss, physical abuse, and sexual abuse.

**Study by Stovall-McClough et al. (2008):**
- Women aged 18-65 with histories of severe emotional, physical, and sexual abuse.
- Using Attachment Anxious Preoccupied (E) classifications: E1 (passively preoccupied), E2 (angrily preoccupied), E3 (fearfully preoccupied).
- Unresolved regarding loss and/or abuse: 50%, unresolved only for abuse: 40%.
- Dismissive classifications: 12% total, no idealizing type, 21% derogating type, 79% cognitively aware/emotionally restricted type.

**Study by Schuengel et al. (1999):**
- Examined mother–infant dyads to test Main and Hesse's hypothesis on frightening behaviors and disorganized infant attachment.
- Mothers with unresolved loss had highest scores for frightening behavior, while those with a secure representation of attachment had very low scores.
- Frightening caregiver behavior forecasted disorganized infant attachment.

**Bakermans-Kranenburg and van IJzendoorn (2009) Review:**
- Adults with abuse experiences or PTSD were mostly unresolved.

**Stalker and Davies (1995):**
- Insecure attachment status, notably preoccupied attachment, predicts unresolved status for survivors of childhood sexual abuse.

**Maternal Unresolved Attachment and Frightened/Frightening Behavior:**
- Correlation between maternal unresolved attachment classification, maternal frightened behavior, and infant attachment disorganization (Iyengar et al., 2014; Jacobvitz et al., 1997; Lyons-Ruth & Jacobvitz, 1999; Madigan et al., 2006; Thalhuber et al., 1998).

**Clinical Implications:**
- Interventions should address both the individual's traumatic experiences and caregivers' frightened/frightening behaviors.
- Every individual can establish earned secure attachment, regardless of their attachment classification and severity.

### ATTACHMENT AND DISSOCIATIVE DISORDERS

**Attachment and Dissociative Disorders**

* Nilsson, Holmqvist, and Jonson (2011) studied the relationship between attachment style and dissociative symptoms.
* Used a modified version of the Experiences in Close Relationships scale (ECR).
* Two dimensions: avoidance and anxiety.
* Modifications: subjects endorsed items based on closest relationship and were asked about whom they were thinking about.

**Findings:**

* Self-reported attachment style correlated with dissociation more than number of potentially traumatic events.
* Secure attachment might protect against dissociative symptoms.
* Anxious attachment style more prevalent in group with more trauma and dissociative symptoms.

**Coe et al. (1995) Study:**

* Measured four types of dissociative experience: memory disturbance, absorption, isolation, fragmentation.
* Insecure attachment linked to increased levels of dissociation and childhood violence.
* Secure attachment showed fewer dissociative symptoms and lower reports of childhood violence.

**Additional Findings:**

* Fearful attachment style related to memory disturbance, violence in childhood, depersonalization, and derealization.
* Preoccupied attachment style correlated with absorption.
* Dismissing attachment style showed only modest degree of dissociative isolation; infrequent reporting of dissociative absorption predicted dismissing attachment style.

### DEPERSONALIZATION/DEREALIZATION DISORDER (DRD)

**Depersonalization/Derealization Disorder (DRD)**

- Previously known as Depersonalization Disorder (DPD) before DSM-IV
- Defined by persistent or recurrent episodes of depersonalization, derealization, or both

**Depersonalization:**

- Alteration in perception or experience of self
- Feeling detached from mental processes or body
- Characterized by vigilant alertness and profound inhibition of emotional feelings
- Diminished autonomic response to emotional stimuli and blunted pain response
- Symptom categories: emotional numbing, disembodiment, anomalous subjective recall and disruption of self-experience

**Derealization:**

- Alteration in perception or experience of the world and surroundings
- Feeling as if in a fog or dream

**Research on DPD:**

- Demonstrated to be a distinct disorder from other dissociative disorders and psychiatric conditions (Simeon et al., 1997)
- Factor analysis identified symptom categories: emotional numbing, disembodiment, anomalous subjective recall and disruption of self-experience, derealization (Sierra and David, 2011)

**Study on Adults with Childhood Sexual Abuse:**

- 12 participants had clinically significant depersonalization
- 7 participants had clinically significant derealization
- Anxious-Avoidant Attachment Interview (AAI) primary classification of insecure (mostly disorganized) attachment significantly associated with depersonalization and derealization (p < 0.05 and p < 0.01, respectively)
- Secure attachment status protected children from developing depersonalization or derealization in adulthood.

### DISSOCIATIVE IDENTITY DISORDER (DID)

**Dissociative Identity Disorder (DID)**

- Defined by existence of two or more alter personality states, loss of executive control, and amnesia for experiences/behaviors of alter personality states (DSM-5)
- Associated with dissociative amnesia and depersonalization
- 105 somatoform symptoms highly comorbid (Nijenhuis, van der Hart, & Steele, 2002)

**Personality Functioning in DID Patients**

- Apparent normal personality structure (ANP) for everyday functioning at expense of emotional processing
- Emotional personality (EP) states for remembering and processing stressful experiences

**Attachment Interviews with DID Patients**

- Steele (2003): Five female survivors of chronic ritual abuse with suspected DID had unresolved status (Ud) and "cannot classify" (CC) on Adult Attachment Interview (AAI)
- Daniel Brown's study: Over 90% of DID patients had CC and Ud; few remaining subjects had anxious-preoccupied (E) or addressed trauma in psychotherapy

**Dissociative Disorders and Attachment**

- Each alter personality state an expression of specific unmet attachment need
- Switches from one state to another as sequential manifestations of unmet needs

**Study of DID/DDNOS Subjects**

- 3 of 45 subjects met full diagnostic criteria for DID; 17 met partial criteria (dissociative disorder not otherwise specified or DDNOS)
- Trend towards insecure attachment associated with DID/DDNOS diagnosis
- Low coherence of transcript and mind significantly correlated with dissociative disorders

**Farina et al. Study**

- 13 patients with various dissociative disorders, including DID and DDNOS, and 13 healthy control subjects
- Healthy attachment organizes states of mind and brain EEG coherence; dissociative disorders lack this effect
- 8 of 13 patients were CC on AAI, but only 1 met full diagnostic criteria for DID

**Minnesota Longitudinal Study**

- High-risk children studied from infancy through early adulthood over three decades
- Assessments of attachment status using Strange Situation Procedure and Adult Attachment Interview
- Disorganized attachment aggravated by later childhood abuse predicts dissociative disorders in adulthood

**Liotti's Three-Pathway Model**

- Exposure to frightened/frightening parental behavior results in disorganized attachment and mild dissociative symptoms (pathway 1)
- Exposure to frightened/frightening parental behavior followed by later childhood abuse results in DID (pathway 2)
- Limited exposure or offsetting caregivers results in high normal but not pathological dissociative experiences (pathway 3)

### Attachment and Addictions

**Substance Abuse and Attachment**

* Substance abuse is characterized by continued use despite negative impacts on cognitive, behavioral, and physiological systems
* Excessive use creates extreme stimulation of brain reward system, leading to dependency with tolerance, withdrawal symptoms, and compulsive use

**Attachment and Substance Use in College Students (Kassel et al., 2007)**

* Anxious attachment linked to substance use and stress-motivated use of substances: tobacco, alcohol, marijuana
* Insecure attachment associated with lower self-esteem and dysfunctional attitudes

**Attachment and Substance Use in Adolescents (Allen et al., 1996)**

* Insecure attachment linked to increased incidents of hard drug use in later adolescence
* Dismissing attachment linked to conduct disorders and substance abuse

**Attachment and Substance Abuse: Drug-Addicted Mothers (Simonelli & Vizziello, 2002)**

* Majority of drug-addicted mothers had anxious-preoccupied or unresolved attachment
* Most children were insecurely attached

**Attachment and Substance Use: Alcoholics and Drug Abusers (Barone et al., 2000)**

* Overrepresentation of insecure, largely dismissing attachment in alcoholics and drug abusers

**Attachment and Substance Use: Borderline Personality Disorder with Substance Abuse/Dependency (Savov & Atanassov, 2013; Flores, 2004)**

* Drug addicts have affect dysregulation problems and deficits in mentalizing
* Addictions are attempts at self-repair for unmet developmental needs

**Attachment-Oriented Treatment of Addictions (Flores, 2004)**

* Aim is to develop healthy internal working models for attachment and affect regulation
* Substance abuse serves as a defense against processing traumatic childhood events

The studies discussed above suggest that insecure attachment, particularly anxious attachment, increases the likelihood of substance use and stress-motivated use. Insecurely attached individuals may develop dysfunctional attitudes about themselves, leading to low self-esteem and increased drug use. Additionally, unmet developmental needs drive addictions as misguided attempts at self-repair. Attachment-oriented treatment focuses on developing healthy internal working models for attachment and affect regulation to help addicts detach from their objects of addiction.

### EATING DISORDERS

**Eating Disorders and Attachment**

* Illing et al. (2010) investigated the relationship between attachment styles and eating disorder (ED) symptoms/treatment outcomes in women with EDs and a control group:
  * Women with EDs showed higher levels of insecure attachment than controls.
  * Anorexia, binge type, had highest levels of anxious and avoidant attachment.
  * Higher attachment anxiety (Need for Approval) correlated with more severe symptoms before treatment.
  * Women with highest attachment anxiety had poorest treatment outcomes.

**Attachment Styles and Disordered Eating in Athletes**

* Shanmugam et al. (2012) studied the link between attachment styles and disordered eating in young adult college and sports club athletes:
  * Positive association between eating disorder measure and both anxious and avoidant attachment.
  * Self-esteem, depression, and self-critical perfectionism mediated the link between attachment styles and eating psychopathology.

**Attachment Styles and Eating Disorders in College Women**

* Cole-Detke and Kobak (1996) found that:
  * Dismissing attachment was linked to higher eating disorder symptoms when depression was controlled.
  * Anxious-preoccupied attachment was common in women with both eating disorder symptoms and depression.

**Attachment Styles and Eating Disorders in Nonpsychotic Inpatients**

* Fonagy et al. (1996) found that:
  * Dismissing attachment, high idealization of parents, and low reflective functioning were associated with eating disorders.
  * Participants with eating disorders are perfectionists whose disorder may stem from exaggerated standards applied to themselves and parents.

**Attachment Styles and Eating Disorders in Anorexic Patients**

* Ward et al. (2001) found that:
  * Nineteen of nineteen anorexics and ten of twelve mothers were classified as insecure on the AAI.
  * Reflective-functioning scores were very low for both anorexics and their mothers.

**Attachment Styles and Eating Disorders in Anorexic Women**

* Zachrisson and Kulbotten (2006) found that:
  * Insecure attachment and unresolved status with respect to trauma were overrepresented in anorexics.
  * All anorexics were insecure with either dismissing, preoccupied, or disorganized attachment.
  * Anxiety levels were significantly higher in anorexics with dismissing attachment.

**Attachment Styles and Eating Disorders: Clinical Implications**

* Barone and Guiducci (2009) found that:
  * The majority of ED patients had insecure attachment, mostly of the dismissing type.
  * ED patients had significantly greater parental rejection, involving, and neglect on the AAI parenting scales.
  * ED patients had a significantly higher lack of recall regarding attachment experiences and significantly lower coherence of transcript and coherence of mind.
* Mark Schwartz and Mike Rechtien (personal communication with D. Brown, July 2015) found that:
  * Most severe ED patients were given the cannot classify (CC) attachment classification from the five-way AAI classification system and an A/C classification from the DMM scoring system.
  * Using the AAI to set treatment goals led to a decrease in eating disorder behaviors by focusing on repairing the attachment system.

### Attachment and Personality Disorders

**Attachment and Personality Disorders**

- Meyer and Pilkonis (2005) studied the relationship between attachment styles and personality disorders using the Experiences in Close Relationships (ECR) questionnaire.
  - Attachment Anxiety: correlated with avoidant, dependent, obsessive-compulsive, paranoid, schizotypal, histrionic, narcissistic, and borderline personality disorders. Strongest correlations with borderline and dependent. No correlation with schizoid.
  - Attachment Avoidance: correlated with avoidant, paranoid, and schizoid personality disorders.
  - Quadrant findings: histrionic, borderline, and dependent in preoccupied quadrant; paranoid, obsessive-compulsive, narcissistic, and schizotypal at border of preoccupied and fearful; dismissing quadrant contained only schizoid; avoidant types in fearful (disorganized) quadrant.

- Dickinson and Pincus (2003) focused on differentiating narcissistic personality into two subtypes: grandiose and vulnerable.
  - Adult Attachment Questionnaire (AAQ) used to assess attachment styles.
  - Grandiose narcissism: 60% secure, 16% dismissive, 13% fearful, 10% preoccupied.
  - Vulnerable narcissism: 50% fearful, 27% secure, 13% preoccupied, 10% dismissive.
  - Control group: 53% secure, 23% fearful, 17% preoccupied, 7% dismissive.

- Alexander (1993) studied the association of attachment status and personality functioning using the Relationship Questionnaire (RQ) and Millon Clinical Multiaxial Inventory II (MCMI-II).
  - Significant correlations between attachment status and presence of a personality disorder.
  - Fearful attachment accounted for most variance on MCMI-II Avoidance scale.
  - Preoccupied and dismissing attachment associated with Dependent scale.
  - Fearful and preoccupied attachment predicted scores on Masochistic/Self-Defeating scale.
  - Preoccupied attachment most significantly associated with Borderline scale.
  - Attachment status, not abuse characteristics, predicted basic personality structure including dysfunctional manifestations.

### BORDERLINE PERSONALITY DISORDER

**Borderline Personality Disorder (BPD)**

* Devastating behavioral problems: self-injurious behavior (69%-75% prevalence), increased suicide risk (400 times greater than general population)
* Negatively impacts treatment efficacy for Axis I disorders
* Difficult to treat due to rapidly shifting attachment strategies and extreme ambivalence
* Important public health problem, affects 1%-3% of population, high long-term impairment

**Study by Lyons-Ruth et al. (2007)**

* Examined women with BPD and dysthymia using Attachment Anxiety and Avoidance Inventory (AAI)
* Hypothesis: lack of attuned parenting leads to unintegrated idealized and denigrated representations
* Hostile-helpless (HH) states of mind assessment: malevolent representation of attachment figures
* Women with BPD showed more hostile-helpless states than women with dysthymia
* Fearfully preoccupied (E3) states associated with providing caregiving and global devaluation
* Preoccupied (E), dismissing (D), unresolved/disorganized (Ud), and unresolved/preoccupied with anger (Ud/E3) classifications
* BPD patients had more unresolved/disorganized and E3 states than dysthymic patients

**Study by Rosenstein and Horowitz (1996)**

* Identified attachment quality in psychiatrically ill adolescents and their mothers
* Adolescents with borderline personality traits did not have preoccupied classification, but those diagnosed with BPD did
* Striking similarity between adolescents' and mothers' attachment classifications

**Study by Fonagy et al. (1996)**

* Studied patterns of attachment and psychiatric status using AAI four-way classification system and SCID-I and SCID-II
* Specific and robust relationship between BPD and attachment classification

### The BPD patients’ AAI interviews were differentiated by a combination of three characteristics: (1) They showed a higher prevalence of abuse and negle

**Text Summary:**

The following summarizes the key points from the text regarding the relationship between Borderline Personality Disorder (BPD) and Attachment Theory:

**Characteristics of BPD Patients in Attachment Interviews:**

- Higher prevalence of abuse and neglect (89%)
- Lower ratings on Reflective Self-Function (RSF) scale
- Significantly higher scores on Lack of Resolution of Abuse or Loss scales (89%)

**Impact of Parental Attachment on BPD:**

- Individuals with severe maltreatment in childhood may inhibit mentalizing function to defend against realization of harm
- Defensive disruption of capacity to depict feelings and thoughts becomes core symptom in BPD, making resolution of traumatic stress more difficult
- Fonagy et al. found 75% of BPD patients were preoccupied, with 47% fearfully preoccupied (E3) reflecting fear of relationships
- Anxiety disorders significantly associated with E3 subclassification in BPD patients

**Comparison of Nonclinical College Students and Participants with BPD:**

- Barone found major group differences in AAI classifications: unresolved and entangled/preoccupied (E) overrepresented, secure/autonomous (F) underrepresented
- Dismissing (Ds) classifications present in similar proportions in both groups
- Qualitative differences between groups: BPD subjects with F classifications high in anger scores (F5), those with Ds had trend toward angry derogation (Ds2) or idealization (Ds1); BPD subjects with E divided between angry stance (E2) and fearful overwhelmed stance (E3)
- BPD group described fewer loving experiences from parents, more rejecting and neglecting experiences, and higher role-reversing experiences with mothers during childhood
- Clinical group scored significantly lower on metacognition and higher in terms of unresolved trauma
- BPD group scored dramatically lower on ratings of coherence of transcript and coherence of mind

**Attachment Studies and Disorders:**

- Bakermans-Kranenburg and van IJzendoorn: internalizing disorders, particularly BPD, overrepresented by preoccupied and unresolved attachment classifications; externalizing disorders more associated with dismissing attachment
- Agrawal et al.: all studies showed strong association between BPD and insecure attachment; most characteristic types of attachment were unresolved, preoccupied, and fearful

**Attachment Theory and Developmental Psychopathology Perspective on BPD:**

- Levy: temperament-based theory of attachment not supported; intersection between attachment and constitutional factors should be examined more closely; each study found inverse relationship between scores of borderline characteristics and secure attachment; BPD not specifically related to one type of attachment pattern; rates of unresolved attachment in BPD generally around 50%, but can be as low as 35%; newer interview studies suggest many BPD patients could be diagnosed with dismissing attachment.

### An important limitation of Levy’s findings is that most of the studies he reviewed did not use the five-way classification system. We believe that bor

**Limitations of Levy's Findings:**

- Most studies did not use the five-way classification system for Attachment Styles (CC, Ds, F, E, U)
- Use of different attachment classification systems led to inconsistent results

**Study by Crittenden and Newman (2010):**

- Compared attachment functioning of mothers with BPD to mothers without psychiatric disorders using two different AAI methods: Main and Goldwyn, Dynamic-Maturational Model (DMM)
- Using Main and Goldwyn method:
  - No secure (F) mothers with BPD
  - All BPD mothers and 3 normative mothers had unresolved (U) status
  - Eight BPD mothers had dismissing classifications (Ds), seven had preoccupied (E)
- Using DMM method:
  - All BPD mothers were unresolved, all had A/C classification
  - Mothers with BPD recalled more danger, reported more negative effects of danger, and showed more indicators of unresolved psychological trauma tied to danger
  - AAIs of BPD mothers were more complex, extreme, and had more rapid shifts in arousal
- Suggested that women with BPD experienced dangers during childhood beyond their ability to cope, leading to maladaptive adaptations

**Study by Barone, Fossati, and Guiducci (2011):**

- Focused on Axis I comorbidities associated with BPD
- Previous research showed high prevalence and severity of unresolved and preoccupied states in borderline pathology
- First study to systematically compare Axis I comorbidities in relation to attachment categories and infer developmental pathways
- Controlled key variables, used AAI as attachment assessment method
- Found that BPD is nearly always associated with insecure states of mind, particularly E and Ds patterns
- Significant differences in attachment status among four Axis I comorbidity groups:
  - Mood disorders and anxiety disorders: overrepresentation of E and Ud classifications
  - Substance abuse/dependence, alcohol abuse/dependence, eating disorders: overrepresentation of dismissing/derogating attachment style (Ds)
- Nine AAI coding-system dimensions significantly associated with selected Axis I disorders among BPD participants
- Findings suggest usefulness of assessing adult attachment mental states to customize treatment for BPD according to the Axis I disorder

**Our Study:**

- Insecure attachment (mostly disorganized) related to mixed or borderline PD in adulthood
- Past or current trauma not significantly related to mixed or borderline PD diagnosis
- Findings suggest that mixed and borderline PD are more related to early attachment disorganization than to trauma or abuse in later childhood or adulthood.

### CONDUCT DISORDER AND ANTISOCIAL PERSONALITY

**Attachment Styles and Personality Disorders: A Comparison of Studies**

**Timmerman and Emmelkamp (2006) Study:**
- Conducted on Dutch prisoners, forensic inpatients, and normal controls
- Secure attachment less frequent among criminal status subjects
- Fearful attachment style not associated with Cluster B personality disorders or histrionic personality pathology
- Antisocial features correlated to dismissing attachment, borderline pathology to preoccupied style

**Rosenstein and Horowitz (1996) Study:**
- Examined adolescents psychiatrically hospitalized for affective disorder, conduct disorder, or a combined diagnosis
- Dismissing attachment linked to conduct disorders
- Adolescents with dismissing attachment organization minimize distressing thoughts and affects associated with rejection by the attachment figure

**Allen et al. (1996) Study:**
- Insecure attachment at age 25 associated with self-reported criminal behavior in adults
- Dismissing attachment organizations reported more criminal behavior than secure adults
- Young adult criminal behavior linked to male gender, derogation of attachment relationships, and lack of resolution of past trauma

**Fonagy et al. (1997) Study:**
- Prisoners had the lowest scores on Reflective Functioning Scale as compared to other groups
- Crimes committed by individuals with inadequate mentalizing capacities
- Disruptive behaviors adopted by children who receive non-optimal care to maximize parental attention

**Van IJzendoorn et al. (1997) Study:**
- Forensic subjects had a high percentage of disorganized attachment (CC) and lacked secure attachment
- Total number of personality disorder symptoms significantly related to attachment insecurity
- CC subjects, raised in institutional care, showed most personality disorders compared to other attachment groups

**Frodi et al. (2001) Study:**
- Dismissing attachment overrepresented among psychopathic criminal offenders
- No significant association between AAI classifications and degree of psychopathy as determined from Hare results
- Subjects classified as CC made contradictory attachment representations, subjects classified as Ds had high idealization of mother and poor recall of childhood experiences.

### Conclusions About Attachment Status and Psychopathology

**Attachment Status and Psychopathology: Conclusions from Research**

* This summary focuses on conclusions drawn from research using the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) to assess attachment status and its relationship with psychopathology.
* Clinical samples are characterized by a high prevalence of insecure attachment, specifically the unresolved (U) and dismissing (D) categories.
* Disorders with an internalizing dimension (e.g., borderline personality disorders) are associated with more preoccupied and unresolved attachments. Disorders with an externalizing dimension (e.g., antisocial personality disorders) display more dismissing as well as preoccupied attachments.
* Depressive symptomatology is linked to insecurity but not unresolved loss or trauma. Adults with abuse experiences or PTSD are mostly unresolved.
* The failure to use a five-way or even four-way classification model in many studies limits the ability to establish consistent relationships between specific types of insecure attachment and specific psychiatric disorders.
* In extremely disturbed individuals, the quality or type of attachment representation may not be adequately described by current attachment concepts nor established with the AAI coding system.
* The CC (cannot classify) classification is likely the primary classification in forensic and inpatient psychiatric settings. It represents pervasive disorganization across the AAI transcript, not topic-specific disorganation as in unresolved status.
* Research should focus on refining the CC category into a range of subtypes to further differentiate attachment disorganization.
* Challenges when administering the AAI to nonnormative samples include identifying primary attachment figures, probing for unresolved loss, dealing with extreme derogation and idealization, and addressing abuse by caregivers and psychotic processes.
* The AAI offers great promise for understanding attachment dynamics and their relation to wellbeing and psychopathology.
* Use of the AAI and an appropriate scoring system is essential for treating patients with complex disorders like PTSD, borderline personality disorder, dissociative disorders, and treatment-resistant patients.
* Attachment disturbances have significant individual and societal implications.

### The Orphanage Study: Attachment and Complex Trauma

**The Orphanage Study: Attachment and Complex Trauma**

- **Background:** Daniel Brown conducted a large number of forensic evaluations for civil lawsuits against alleged child abusers in the context of the Catholic orphanage in New Orleans (1950s-1960s).
- **Orphanage Abuse:** Allegations involved two priests and five staff members, many with prior abuse accusations. An organized culture of abuse emerged, leading to extensive physical and sexual abuse of numerous orphans.
- **Memory Recovery:** Victims began recalling sexual abuse memories between 2004-2006 due to media exposure. Lawsuits were settled before trials.
- **Assessment:** Between 2005-2006, Brown conducted a detailed forensic psychological assessment of each victim.
- **Unique Features:** Sample represents victims of an entire organized culture of abuse, all abused by the same group during the same time frame.
- **Attachment Interviews:** All subjects underwent Adult Attachment Interview (AAI), revealing 60% insecure attachment (disorganized type).
- **Background Differences:** Two groups: chaotic families with high risk of disorganized attachment vs large, loving Catholic families with secure attachment.
- **Impact:** Despite differences, both groups were abused by the same offenders.
- **Study Significance:** Allows separation of early attachment status and childhood abuse effects on adult psychopathology.

### To increase the statistical power, we also examined a second mixed group of CSA victims. Group A comprised the 17 subjects described above, and Group 

**Study Overview:** The study examined two groups of Child Sexual Abuse (CSA) victims, Group A (17 subjects from an orphanage with same offenders) and Group B (28 adult survivors with different offenders). Both groups underwent similar forensic examinations.

**Group Comparison:**
- Few significant differences between the two groups, leading to a pooled group of 45 subjects.
- Group A had higher mean unresolved score (Ud) for trauma and more dissociative, depressed, and prone to panic attacks.
- Age range: 16-75 years, average age: 44, majority male (89%), varied educational backgrounds, mostly white (87%) and Catholic (84%).

**Assessments:** All subjects were given the same set of instruments to evaluate:
1. Attachment status using Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) and Experience of Close Relationships scale (ECR)
2. Axis I depression and anxiety conditions using SCID-I, Beck Depression Inventory–1, Penn State Worry Scale, and Body Sensations Questionnaire
3. PTSD using Trauma Symptom Inventory and Clinician Administered PTSD Scale
4. Dissociative conditions using SCID-D, Dissociation Questionnaire, and Somatoform Dissociation Questionnaire
5. Axis II conditions using SCID-II and Young Schema Questionnaire–III
6. Other instruments: Index of Self Esteem, Fear of Negative Evaluation scale, and Social Avoidance and Distress Scale

**Findings:**
- Preliminary analyses focused on the relationship between attachment classifications (secure vs. insecure) and various psychiatric diagnoses.
- High coherence of transcript and mind were associated with fewer diagnoses.
- Insecure attachment was significantly related to a mixed PD or borderline PD diagnosis using both SCID-II and YSQ-III.
- Subjects with insecure attachment had more negative, maladaptive schemas.

### A relationship between insecure attachment (coupled with Ud status) and dissociative disorders was also confirmed. Using the SCID-D (Steinberg, 1993),

**Attachment and Dissociative Disorders**

- A study using SCID-D identified 4 subjects with full DID and 17 with DDNOS (distinctive "parts" but not meeting full DID criteria) out of a total of 45 subjects.
- Insecure attachment was related to DID/DDNOS, but the correlation narrowly missed significance level.
- Low coherence of transcript and mind on the AAI were significantly correlated with DID/DDNOS diagnosis.
- Depersonalization and derealization were more prevalent in insecure subjects than secure ones.
- Seven out of seven subjects with a derealization diagnosis had insecure attachment and unresolved status regarding past trauma.
- Ten out of twelve subjects with depersonalization had insecure attachment.

**Attachment and Bipolar Disorder**

- All bipolar subjects (n = 14) had type II bipolar disorder on the SCID-I, eleven with insecure and three with secure attachment on the AAI.
- Insecure attachment was significantly related to bipolar type II diagnosis.

**Attachment and Self-Esteem, Fear of Negative Evaluation, Social Avoidance/Distress**

- Significant Chi-squares were observed when insecure/secure attachment was cross-tabulated with self-esteem, fear of negative evaluation, and social avoidance/distress.
- A logistic regression showed that fear of negative evaluation was sufficient to predict insecurity 73% of the time.

**Attachment and Other Psychiatric Disorders**

- No significant statistical relationships were found between insecure attachment and major depressive disorder, dysthymia, panic disorder, or somatoform disorder.
- Insecure attachment was not significantly related to PTSD based on TSI and CAPS scores.

**Attachment and Substance Abuse**

- No significant statistical relationships were found between insecure status or coherence of discourse on the AAI and alcohol or drug abuse/dependency on the SCID-I.

**Malingering and Attachment**

- Significant Chi-squares were observed when insecure/secure attachment was cross-tabulated with malingering probability scale (MPS) and total malingering score on the Structured Interview of Reported Symptoms (SIRS).
- Subjects with higher malingering probability scores had significantly lower coherence of transcript.

**Implications for Complex Trauma Treatment**

- Complex trauma is often characterized by multiple, comorbid psychiatric diagnoses.
- Early attachment disturbance may account for complex trauma and/or multiple comorbidities independent of or in combination with abuse.
- Recognition of the role of early attachment failure opens the door to reconsideration of its contribution to complex trauma etiology.

### An important exception to trauma bias in the complex trauma literature is Alexander’s research (1993) on women survivors of childhood sexual abuse. In

**Two Pathways to Complex Trauma: Attachment and Abuse**

- *Alexander's research (1993)* on women survivors of childhood sexual abuse identified two separate pathways to complex trauma:
  - Insecure attachment contributing to personality disorders
  - Abuse per se contributing to PTSD and other Axis I anxiety and affective conditions

**Evidence from Prospective Studies**

- Childhood abuse causally related to:
  - Development of conduct and relational problems in adolescence (Widom, 1999)
  - Emergence of psychiatric conditions in early adulthood, such as PTSD, depression, anxiety, somatoform symptoms, and addictions (Silverman et al., 1996; Putnam, 1998; Widom et al., 1995)
- Emergence of multiple comorbid psychiatric conditions in adulthood as a consequence of abuse is common (Brown, 2001; Putnam, 1998)
- Use of dissociative coping style to handle abuse predicts greater severity and range of psychiatric conditions in adulthood (Putnam, 1998)

**Contributions from Attachment Research**

- Early childhood insecure attachment is related to the emergence of significant areas of psychopathology in adolescence and adulthood (Putnam, 1998; Silverman et al., 1996; Widom, 1999)
- Childhood disorganized or resistant insecure attachment status is a risk factor for:
  - Development of personality disorders (Barone, 2003; Fonagy et al., 1996; Patrick et al., 1994; van IJzendoorn et al., 1997)
  - Anxiety disorders (Bosquet & Egeland, 2006; Warren et al., 1997)
  - Addictions in adulthood (Widom et al., 1995)
- Combination of disorganized attachment and later childhood abuse predicts development of dissociative disorders in adulthood (Carlson, 1998; Liotti, 1997; Ogawa et al., 1997)

**Two Lineages of Long-Term Effects of Trauma**

- Simple PTSD:
  - Associated with single or cumulative traumatic events per se
  - Manifests mainly in Axis I psychiatric conditions like PTSD, affective and/or depressive symptoms, and sometimes addictive behaviors
- Complex trauma:
  - Outcome of a combination of early childhood insecure attachment aggravated by abuse or trauma in later childhood or adulthood
  - Manifests as complex trauma-related disorders with somatoform symptoms, personality disorder, major dissociative disorder or multiple dissociative symptoms, and often multiple addictive behaviors
  - Multiple comorbid psychiatric conditions are common, with 5 to 10 discrete DSM diagnoses possible.

### In summary, simple PTSD is associated with single or cumulative specific trauma events, whereas complex trauma is primarily associated with insecure a

**Simple PTSD vs. Complex Trauma**

* Simple PTSD: associated with single or cumulative specific trauma events
* Complex Trauma: primarily associated with insecure attachment aggravated by abuse or trauma in later childhood or adulthood
* Early childhood insecure attachment is a key factor in complex trauma development

**Findings from Orphanage Study**

* Axis I conditions (PTSD, depression, anxiety) related to traumatic acts
* Personality disorders (borderline, dissociative disorders, bipolar type II) linked to insecure attachment and low coherence of discourse on the Attachment Interview (AAI)
* Insecure attachment disrupts developmental lines: affect, self, positive internal working models
* Long-term effects may lead to mixed or borderline personality disorder
* Dissociative symptoms not caused by abuse per se but by early insecure attachment and later trauma

**False Memory Cases**

* Patients initially came for treatment unrelated to abuse
* Developed memories of childhood sexual abuse and dissociative symptoms
* Diagnosed with multiple personality disorder or dissociative identity disorder
* Lawsuits against clinicians for allegedly implanting false memories
* Many plaintiffs coached by attorneys and false-memory proponents
* Defendant clinicians used standard trauma processing methods (trauma-focused, cognitive-behavioral)

**Lessons Learned**

* Trauma processing may be contraindicated for patients with disorganized attachment aggravated by later childhood abuse
* Focus on treating disorganized attachment first using attachment-based methods
* Once high coherence of mind is achieved, trauma processing can be effective without causing further disorganization.

### PART III TREATMENT BACKGROUND AND THE THREE PILLARS MODEL OF COMPREHENSIVE ATTACHMENT REPAIR CHAPTER 6 An Overview of Treatments for Attachment Distur

**Part III: Treatment Background and the Three Pillars Model of Comprehensive Attachment Repair**

**Chapter 6: An Overview of Treatments for Attachment Disturbances**

- This chapter provides an overview of treatment approaches for attachment disturbances, focusing on those representative of the types and forms developed since Bowlby and Ainsworth's pioneering work.
- **Bowlby's Attachment-Based Psychotherapy:**
  - Originated from Bowlby's work in "A Secure Base" (1988).
  - Therapist provides a secure base for exploration of attachment themes, past and present.
  - Focus on the influence of early experiences on the transference relationship.
  - Goal: Develop a more positive internal working model for attachment.
  - Applicable to patients with disturbances in their capacities to make secure attachments due to outright threats or ongoing patterns of parental unresponsiveness or misresponsiveness.
- **Principles:**
  * Therapist as a secure base: Encourages exploration and provides support, encouragement, sympathy, and guidance.
  * Exploration of attachment experiences, feelings, representations, and expectancies about past attachment figures.
  - Reappraising and restructuring internal working models for healthy attachment.
- **Methods:**
  * Secure base: Therapist offers a safe environment for patients to explore their attachment themes.
  * Transference relationship: Focus on the influence of early experiences on the therapeutic alliance.
  * Reconstruction: Exploring memories of the past and deep reluctance to disobey past orders.
- **Limitations:**
  * Time-consuming and resource-intensive.
  * Requires a well-trained therapist with expertise in attachment theory and psychotherapy.

### Psychoanalytic Approaches

**Psychoanalytic Approaches**

- John Bowlby received attention for his work on attachment problems and therapeutic relationship departing from psychoanalytic strictures.
- Lawrence Hedges' method in "Remembering, Repeating, and Working Through Childhood Trauma" (1994):
  - Interpreting resistance to and defenses against attachment.
  - Interpreting transference of organizing period and maternal failure.
  - Allowing the organizing transference to develop.
  - Consolidating sense of self, reflexive mental states, and new template for relatedness.
- Malcolm West and Adrienne Keller's attachment-based approach (1994):
  - Treating patients with personality disorders based on Bowlby's observations on childhood loss and mourning.
  - Understanding loss as experience of what was never present, not just actual separation.
  - Acknowledging the loss to complete delayed mourning process.
  - Focusing on therapist as secure base for patient.
  - Differentiating dismissing vs anxious-preoccupied attachment styles and their exploration targets.
  - Addressing intrapsychic conflict, "desire–fear drama."
  - Interpreting transference to separate desire from fear and provide corrective emotional experiences.

### Attachment-Informed Psychotherapy

**Attachment-Informed Psychotherapy**

- Emerged as a response to the lessening adherence to strict psychoanalytic notions in psychotherapy
- Maintains focus on intrapsychic functioning while emphasizing cognitive, relational, and systemic factors
- Draws from attachment theory and other psychological understandings (Bowlby, Ainsworth, Stern, Trevarthen, Main, Fonagy)

**Attachment, Intimacy, Autonomy (Holmes, 1996)**

- Goals:
  * Attachment system: adult intimacy
  * Exploratory system: autonomy of the self
- Secure base experience with therapist:
  * Foundation for broader security of attachment and healthy self-autonomy
  * Therapist behaves like a responsive, attuned parent-figure
- Therapeutic process:
  * Establish secure base
  * Use it as starting point for exploration of dysfunctional attachment themes
  * Encourage patient to explore transference to therapist
  * Therapeutic stance: interested nonreactivity and capacity for detached reflection
- Dual focus: attachment and autonomy
- Ruptures and failures of empathy in treatment are inevitable, but can lead to new internal working model of attachment
- Development of a healing narrative leads to tolerance of separation anxiety and independence from therapist

**Attachment Patterns**

- Dismissing:
  * First task is to establish emotional contact
- Anxious-preoccupied:
  * Highly dependent
  * Distressed around separation
  * Key issue: expression of appropriate anger at separation
- Disorganized:
  * Will develop borderline personality disorder
  * Treatment entails unhooking role-reversing patterns and moving beyond longing and loss

### CLINICAL PATTERNS OF ATTACHMENT

**Attachment and Adult Psychotherapy: A Bowlby-Based Approach by Pat Sable**

* Sable presents an attachment perspective for treating attachment disorders in adults, not an attachment-based treatment (2000, p. 88)

**Bowlby's Attachment Theory**

* Attachment is about safety and protection
* Seeking proximity and emotional availability of therapist
* Attachment behavior is lifelong and active throughout the life-cycle
* Individuals lack confidence in the availability of affectional figures, leading to dysfunctional internal working models (IWMs) (p. 101)

**Goals of Attachment-Informed Psychotherapy**

* Establish therapist as a stable attachment figure
* Modify dysfunctional IWMs
* Change IWMs through exploration and understanding of past experiences

**Establishing a Secure Base**

* Provide regular appointments, emotional availability, comfort, and safety
* Support development of greater self-reliance (p. 144)

**Exploration Process in Attachment-Informed Psychotherapy**

1. Retrieve defensively excluded memories and feelings
2. Validate emotions associated with early attachment experiences
3. Map out past IWMs of attachment
4. Correct transference distortions and distortion in IWMs
5. Develop a coherent narrative (p. 345)

**Unique Features of Therapy Relationship**

* Safe base for exploration of early dysfunctional attachment experiences
* Trusted companion supporting development of greater self-reliance
* Opportunity to investigate and reexperience past experiences with empathy, support, and guidance (pp. 332–333)

**Benefits of Attachment-Informed Psychotherapy**

* Understanding how working models have been generated and maintained
* Moving away from abusive affectional ties towards healthier relationships
* Leads to greater self-reliance based on confidence in the therapist (pp. 299, 345)

### ATTACHMENT-BASED PSYCHOTHERAPY

**Attachment-Based Psychotherapy (ABP)**

* Developed by Karl Heinz Brisch, based on Bowlby's and Ainsworth's theories
* Therapist acts as a secure attachment figure
* Encourages patients to explore and understand their attachment strategies
* Focuses on recognizing and updating outdated representations of self and objects in current relationships
* Goal: Help patients establish greater awareness of attachment strategies and their appropriateness for current life

**Dynamic-Maturational Model Integrative Treatment (DMM)**

* Developed by Patricia Crittenden
* Attachment behavior reflects organized strategies for coping with danger and seeking protection
* Maladaptive behavior results from exposure to unprotected and uncomforted danger
* Focus on repairing and modifying problematic dynamics within individuals and family systems
* Parents' experiences and coping strategies are crucial in addressing attachment issues
* Goal: Enable individuals to generate and apply self-protective strategies at the right time and context for psychological balance

**Common Themes**

* Attachment patterns play a significant role in understanding and treating mental health issues
* Importance of recognizing and updating outdated representations of self and objects
* Focus on repairing and modifying problematic dynamics within individuals and family systems
* Emphasis on enabling individuals to generate and apply self-protective strategies for psychological balance
* Long-term goal: Change information processing to promote adaptive and functional behavior in current and future circumstances.

### Regarding treatment methods and the process itself, Crittenden emphasizes that “good assessment, good formulation, and good therapeutic relationships”

**Crittenden's DMM Integrated Treatment**

- Essential components: good assessment, formulation, and therapeutic relationships (Crittenden, 2008)

**Assessment** in Crittenden's Dynamic-Maturational Model (DMM):
- Yields information about dispositional representations (DRs) and protective attachment strategies
- DRs: eight types underlie each patient's behavioral tendencies
- Information-processing patterns characterize a patient's protective strategies (A, B, C, and their combinations)

**Formulation**:
- Draws from assessment information
- Understands nature, origins, and perpetuation of intrapersonal, interpersonal, and familial problems
- Generates DR and information-processing strategy formulations for diagnostic conditions (e.g., eating disorders, PTSD, factitious illness by proxy)

**Therapeutic Relationship**:
- Important for successful treatment
- Critical of manualized treatments and limiting techniques to specific schools of therapy
- Use whatever techniques best fit the goals of treatment

**General Principles of Treatment**:
1. **Danger and safety**:
   - Address patient's experience of danger in past, present, and future
   - Establish sense of safety
   - Consider potential dangers associated with therapy itself or modification of information-processing strategies
2. **Affect regulation**:
   - Help patients manage emotions
   - Develop skills for emotional self-regulation
3. **Interpersonal communication**:
   - Improve communication between family members
   - Encourage effective problem-solving and conflict resolution
4. **Cognitive restructuring**:
   - Challenge maladaptive thoughts and beliefs
   - Replace with adaptive ones
5. **Behavioral change**:
   - Teach new skills and behaviors
   - Reinforce desired behaviors
6. **Relationship building**:
   - Strengthen therapeutic relationship
   - Encourage attachment security and bonding between family members

**Techniques**:
- Elicit discovery, foster exploration, encourage practice
- May include medication, individual sessions, group sessions, inpatient hospitalization, or social service interventions

**Importance of Assessment and Formulation**:
- Individualized treatment based on unique needs of the patient
- Prevent missing the patient's needs or causing harm with misfit techniques
- Consistent with parenting based on attunement to child's characteristics and developmental stage.

### 2. Therapists as transitional attachment figures.

**Therapists as Transitional Attachment Figures:**

- Therapists can function as transitional attachment figures by making treatment tolerable and safe for individuals with distorted information processing strategies (Crittenden & Landini, 2011; Crittenden et al., 2014).
- More problematic adaptive strategies require stronger therapist-patient relationships, which may resemble naturally occurring attachment relationships (Crittenden, 2008).
- Qualities that increase the likelihood of a patient experiencing the therapist as a transitional attachment figure include compassion, acceptance, attunement, sensitive responsiveness, and genuine feeling, particularly comfort (Crittenden, 2008; Crittenden et al., 2014).
- The connection between the therapist and patient is the therapist's willingness to be a secure base and the patient's desire for one (Crittenden, 2008).
- The progress of the relationship is both the process and a goal of the treatment (Crittenden, 2008).

**Regulating Arousal:**

- Helping patients regulate arousal supports access to motivation, stability, and essential mental processes for therapy.
- Maintaining an appropriate level of arousal is crucial for effective therapy.

### 4. Quieting traumatic responses to current conditions. “Unresolved traumas and losses require enough exploration to make them explicit and open to dis

**Crittenden's Attachment-Based Therapy: Components and Key Concepts**

**1. Establishing a Secure Base:**
- Building a therapeutic alliance
- Creating a safe and predictable environment
- Promoting emotional regulation

**2. Quieting traumatic responses to current conditions:**
- Exploring unresolved traumas and losses
  * Unresolved loss: 12 forms (Crittenden & Landini, 2011, pp. 236–253)
  * Unresolved trauma: 14 forms (Crittenden & Landini, 2011, pp. 386–387)
- Tailoring focus and intervention based on identified forms
- Identifying maladaptive coping strategies
- Functional formulation for understanding past behavior and treatment possibilities

**3. Regulating arousal:**
- Protecting against large fluctuations in patient's arousal
- Supporting effective therapy process
- Highlighting maladaptive strategies in current circumstances

**4. Increasing family members’ repertoire of protective strategies:**
- Acquiring additional adaptive strategies
- Preventing recurrence of problems when new strategies are needed
- Functional formulation for understanding past behavior and treatment possibilities
- Identifying patient's typical existing strategies
- Selecting appropriate interventions based on over- or underrepresentation of strategies
- Considering effects of Type A and Type C strategies on treatment process

**5. Reaching coherence and resilience:**
- Promoting further resilience in current and unfamiliar circumstances
- Iterative integrative process for understanding developmental progress
- Gaining compassionate understanding of limitations of important others
- Optimism for facing new adversities while protecting oneself and loved ones.

### As Crittenden emphasizes the importance of goal-setting and working within the patient’s zone of proximal development (e.g., Crittenden et al., 2014, 

**Crittenden's Treatment Model:**

- Emphasizes goal-setting and working within the patient's zone of proximal development.
- Integrative step involves complex cortical processing, not fully possible before late adolescence.
- Reflective approaches to therapy recommended for integrative step.
  * Crittenden praises Fonagy's work on reflective function and mentalization.
  * Assumes mentalization-based methods are appropriate.
- Importance of metaprocessing with the patient during later phases of treatment (Fosha).
- Tailors interventions to individual's developmental needs and capacities.
- Examples: parents using verbal episodes, toddlers working through play enactments.
- School-aged children connecting preconscious DRs with verbal forms of representation.

**Attachment Representations and Internal Working Models:**

- Bowlby's recognition of internal working models central to attachment theories and treatment methods.
- Several approaches work directly with patients' mental representations related to attachment.

### WORKING WITH ATTACHMENT REPRESENTATIONS INDEPENDENT OF TRANSFERENCE

**Sperling and Lyons' Approach to Attachment Disorders: Focusing on Attachment Representations**

- Psychodynamically based approach to treating attachment disorders without focusing on transference reenactment (Sperling & Lyons, 1994)
- Informed by Mary Main et al.'s definition of Internal Working Models of Attachment (IWMs): a set of rules for organizing and accessing attachment-related information (Main, Kaplan, & Cassidy, 1985)

**Identifying and Modifying Attachment Representations**

- Focus on facilitating change in mental representations of attachment (p. 331)
- Interpersonal but non-enactive transferential mode with regard to the therapeutic relationship (p. 338)
- Vigilantly attend to patient's reports of current and past attachment experiences to discern representational patterns (p. 340)

**Identifying Active Representational Patterns**

- Attend to patient's reports of current and past attachment experiences
- Discerning emergent representational patterns
- Use these patterns to give meaning to current events and anticipation of future events

**Modifying Internal Working Models (IWMs)**

- Guiding the patient in constructing defining relational narratives
- Allowing for a more conscious awareness of idiosyncratic rules and expectations governing representations
- Using these markers against which new experiences can be compared and evaluated (pp. 340–341)

**Interpretations in Sperling and Lyons' Method**

- Interpretations focus on patterns of experience rather than specifically on transference with the therapist
- The interpretive process takes on a psychoeducational quality (p. 341)
- Developing new, healthy attachment relational narratives or IWMs

### IMAGERY OF THE GOOD-ENOUGH THERAPIST TOGETHER WITH THE PATIENT

**Baker's Approach to Enhancing Attachment in Psychotic and Borderline Patients**

* Elgan Baker introduced a method for enhancing relatedness in psychotic and borderline patients with attachment disturbances (1981)
* Based on British object relations theory, focusing on internalization and organization of interpersonal relationships
* Primitive forms of psychopathology conceptualized as structural deficits in ego formation due to difficulties in developmental tasks

**Fostering Attachment Bond**

* More active and nurturing role for the therapist
* Enhances attachment by gratifying dependency strivings
* Maintains connectedness during regression
* Therapist becomes a "real object" who can "hold" boundaries of reality
* Patient forms stable internal representation (cathexis) of the "good-enough" therapist

**Advantages of Hypnotic Imagery**

* Heightened state of attentiveness
* Greater inward orientation and less orientation to outside reality
* Greater access to internal emotions and memories
* More vivid and readily available imagery
* Highly structured relationship with therapist control

**Seven Steps of Treatment**

1. Patient imagines self involved in pleasant activity, evoking a stable positive representation of self
2. Patient opens eyes to see therapist focused on state, reassuring presence
3. Patient repeatedly imagines therapist as benign and supportive presence
4. Patient imagines himself/herself and therapist together with proximity, preserving clear boundaries
5. Patient imagines coordinated interactions, internalizing therapist as "good object"
6. Patient externalizes malignant internal voices or representations
7. Dynamic exploration of dysfunctional patterns and integrating positive and negative images

**Unique Contributions**

* Co-creating attachment imagery spontaneously during sessions
* Refusing canned scripts for what to say during the imagery process

### New Forms of Attachment-Informed Treatment

**Schema Therapy for Patients with Attachment Disturbances**

* Schema Therapy is a new approach to treating patients with personality disorders and attachment disturbances, developed by Jeffrey Young, Janet Klosko, and Marjorie Weishaar.
* Based on emotion-focused or experiential methods, it focuses on healing Early Maladaptive Schemas (EMS).

**Concept of Maladaptive Schemas**

* EMS are self-defeating emotional and cognitive patterns that begin in childhood and repeat throughout life.
* Each schema revolves around a childhood theme and is relationally based.
* Result from unmet core emotional needs.
* Composed of memories, emotions, cognitions, and body sensations.
* Healing involves diminishing all aspects of the schema.

**Schema Domains**

1. Disconnection and Rejection: Abandonment, mistrust-abuse, emotional deprivation, defectiveness, social isolation.
2. Impaired Autonomy and Performance: Dependency, vulnerability, enmeshment, failure.
3. Impaired Limits: Entitlement, insufficient self-control.
4. Other-Directedness: Subjugation, self-sacrifice, approval-seeking.
5. Overvigilance and Inhibition: Negativity, emotional inhibition, unrelenting standards, punitiveness.

**Schema Therapy Goals**

1. Identify salient maladaptive schemas.
2. Increase conscious control over schemas.
3. Understand schema origins in childhood.
4. Heal schemas through limited reparenting and experiential techniques.

**Limited Reparenting**

* Therapist serves as an ally to fight against maladaptive schemas.
* Provides limited reparenting, supplying missed emotional experiences within ethical and professional boundaries.
* Goal: Patient internalizes a Healthy Adult mode.

**Experiential Techniques**

* Safe-place imagery.
* Evoke negative affect associated with early childhood experiences.
* Reparenting through therapist entering patient's image as a Healthy Adult.
* Teach patients there are other ways parents might have treated them.

**Overall Aim**

* Patient develops an internalized healthy adult representation, modeled after the therapist.

### [T]he therapist enters the images to take care of the child. The therapist does whatever a good parent would have done: removes the child from the sce

**Schema Therapy**

- Therapist enters patient's images to care for traumatized inner child.
  * Removes child from scene, confronts perpetrator, etc.
  * Patient eventually takes over as Healthy Adult.

**Experiential Techniques**

- Imagery work with dialogues and schema mode dialogues.
  * Safe place imagery.
  * Dialogue between patient and parent figure.
  * Encourage desired changes in parent figure.

**Schema Modes**

- Moment-to-moment emotional states and coping responses.
- Grouped into parts of the self.
  * Child modes: Vulnerable, Angry, Impulsive, Happy.
  * Maladaptive coping modes: Compliant Surrender, Detached Protector, Overcompensator.
  * Dysfunctional parent modes: Punitive, Demanding.
  * Healthy Adult mode.
- Goal: Access schema modes and initiate dialogue to resolve conflicts.

**Schema Mode Therapy**

- Behavioral homework assignments for practicing adaptive responses.
- Target self-defeating coping strategies.
- Identify problematic behaviors, coping strategies, and life patterns.
- Replace maladaptive schemas with healthy patterns.

**Attachment Connection**

- Therapist as attachment figure.
- Provides stable base for identity and self-acceptance.
- Patient internalizes therapist behaviors as Healthy Adult mode.

**Effectiveness**

- Schema therapy shown to be more effective than transference-focused treatment in treating borderline personality disorder (Geisen-Bloo et al., 2006).

### ACCELERATED EXPERIENTIAL DYNAMIC PSYCHOTHERAPY (AEDP)

**Accelerated Experiential Dynamic Psychotherapy (AEDP)**

* Innovative attachment-informed treatment developed by Diana Fosha
* Focus on facilitating affect experience and transformation through a good attachment relationship
* Central concept: core affect

**Core Affect**

* Vital, spontaneous emotional responses when defenses are not in operation
* Defining feature: has the power to engender healing state transformations
* Goal of AEDP: increase availability and stability of core affect

**Treatment Process**

* Establish therapeutic holding environment
	+ Secure, emotionally facilitating dyadic relationship
	+ Qualities of therapist's ways of being with the patient
		- Empathic attunement
		- Recognition, affirmation, validation, and valuing of patient's experience
		- Expressing care, compassion, concern
		- Authenticity and active use of therapist's own affective experience
		- Offering encouragement and being helpful
		- Expressing delight in the patient
* Facilitate core affect to promote emergence of core states
	+ Altered condition of openness and contact with self and relational experience

**Therapist Role**

* Attentively track moment-by-moment fluctuations in patient's affective state
* Maintain accurate attunement and match/amplify patient's ongoing affective displays
* Facilitate mutual, coordinated expression of facial displays of affect
* Highlight the importance of the relationship itself as a focus of therapeutic work

**Dyadic Interaction Processes**

* Ongoing regulations: how moment-to-moment experience is received and responded to
* Heightened affective moments: intense emotional experiences in relational context
* Disruption and repair of ongoing regulations: handling disruptions within the relational dyad

### In addition to these processes of dyadic interaction and the qualities of the therapist’s ways of being with the patient, AEDP places emphasis on the 

**Accelerated Experiential Dynamic Psychotherapy (AEDP)**

* Focuses on dyadic interaction, therapist's ways of being, patient's somatic experience, and metaprocessing
* Emphasizes transformative processes and attachment security

**Principles and Practices**

* Dyadic attunement to patient's vitality affects
* Reducing defenses and inhibiting affects
* Holding environment for transformation
