**Emotion-based Transformational Process**

**State One:**

* Patient comes with distress, symptoms, dysregulated affects, inhibiting affects, defenses, and transformative strivings
* Therapist's dyadic attunement supports transformation from first state
* Signs of transformation: green signal affects, heralding affects, transitional affects
* First state transformation: full experience of core affective phenomenon

**State Two:**

* Patient feels safe in therapeutic dyad
* Processing emotional experiences
* Appearance of categorical emotions
* Relief, clarity, hope, strength, adaptive action tendencies
* Second state transformation: relief from inhibited emotions

**State Three:**

* Processing transformational experience
* Mastery affects, emotional pain, tremulous affects, healing affects, realization affects
* Shifting from core affect to core state
* Third state transformation: calm, clarity, openness, aliveness, vitality, sense of ease and relaxation

**State Four:**

* Transformation into core state
* Core state prominent and available
* Self and relational characteristics consistent with attachment security
* Defining qualities of core state: wisdom, compassion, generosity, vibrant wellbeing, equanimity, confidence, creativity, naturalness, enhanced initiative and agency, sense of the sacred, truth sense

**Unique Contribution:**

* Thorough affect transformation in dyadic patient–therapist attachment relationship
* All affective states are seeds for deeply transformative experience and development

### Intersubjectivity-Based Treatment

**Intersubjectivity-Based Treatment**

- Influenced by Beebe and Lachmann's work on intersubjectivity in the field of attachment
- Integrated into Accelerated Experiential Dynamic Psychotherapy (AEDP)
- Based on research on intersubjectivity in infant-mother interactions

**Intersubjectivity and Adult Treatment**

*Beebe and Lachmann's Model for Treating Adults with Attachment Disturbance*

- Secure dyads: self-regulation and interactive regulation, disruption and repair, positive mutual engagement
- Primary intersubjectivity emerges from these principles
- Collaborative vs. contradictory dialogues: significant difference in nonverbal exchange
- Contradictory interactions lead to failed self-agency, expectation of misregulation, and internalization of dysregulated interaction

**Beebe and Lachmann's Approach to Psychotherapy**

- Emphasizes attunement to nonverbal exchange between patient and therapist
- Careful observation of nonverbal behavior: head orientation, body posture, gaze, facial affect display, rhythm, pitch, tone, timing of verbal responses
- Therapist matches and rematches patient's nonverbal behavior
- Addresses patterns of distancing or hyperengaging nonverbal behavior in the patient

**Methods of Treating Nonverbal Intersubjectivity Failures**

1. Sustained collaborative, noncontradictory nonverbal responsiveness from therapist
2. Videotaping and reviewing sessions to bring attention to intersubjective dynamic

**Recommendation**

- Awareness of moment-by-moment nonverbal exchange between patient and therapist essential for treating attachment disorders in adults
- Familiarity with intersubjective principles and practices recommended for therapists treating patients with insecure attachment.

### EXPLORATORY PSYCHOTHERAPY

**Exploratory Psychotherapy and Attachment Theory**

- Dorothy Heard, Brian Lake, and Una McCluskey extended Bowlby's attachment theory with a new paradigm focusing on intersubjectivity and various behavioral systems (Heard et al., 2009).

**Behavioral Systems**

- Extension to Bowlby's thinking: collaborative interest-sharing, affectional sexuality, system of personal defense, and external lifestyle system.

**Intersubjectivity**

- Manifests through intricate rhythmic patterns in dyadic exchange.
- Three aspects: communion, joint attention, and reflective and recursive communication.
- Caregiver's affect attunement vs. purposeful misattunement.

**Self Development**

- Self grows and develops both within and apart from attachment relationship.
- Withdrawal into subjectivity for self-reflection and creativity.

**Exploratory Psychotherapy**

- Restorative therapy: patient seeks care, therapist as secure base.
- First task: attenuate fear system, activate attachment system.
- Secure base enables exploration of self across behavioral systems.

**Quality of Dyadic Exchange**

- Empathic recognition and emotional communication essential for a secure base.
- Video recordings used to train therapists in nonverbal attunement.

**Patient Care-Seeking Behaviors**

- Openness, avoidance, tangled discussion, dismissal, and overwhelm.

**Therapist Caregiving Responses**

- Careful attunement, misattunement with repair, defection, withdrawal, and disorganization.

**Interaction Patterns**

- 25 potential patterns based on patient behaviors and therapist responses.

**Common Misattunements by Therapists**

- Interrupting, failing to regulate state of mind, minimal response, avoiding affect, discrepancy between verbal and nonverbal, ignoring fear activation, missing immediate affect.

**Learning Attunement**

- Through training and experience, therapists learn to genuinely attune themselves to patients, restoring wellbeing and enabling exploration.

### Metacognitive Attachment-Informed Psychotherapies

**Metacognitive Attachment-Informed Psychotherapies**

* Metacognitive functioning plays a crucial role in attachment and its disturbances.
* Mentalization-Based Treatment (MBT) focuses on mentalization as a metacognitive skill.

**Mentalization-Based Treatment (MBT)**

* Developed by Peter Fonagy, Anthony Bateman, Jon Allen, and their colleagues.
* Central concept: mentalization.
* Mentalization is making sense of actions based on intentional mental states.
* Develops in secure attachment relationships during childhood.
* Stages in development: contingent responsiveness, marking, representation of emotions, second-order representations.
* Allows for reflective capacity and affect regulation.

**Mentalization Development**

* Security of attachment and caregiver's holding the child's mind in mind facilitate mentalization.
* Individuals with insecure attachment are at risk for failure to develop mentalization capacity.
* Reflective Functioning Scale (RF-S) assesses reflective functioning in adults.
* PD and DD patients lack reflective capacity.

**MBT Approaches**

* Mentalizing is a consistent therapeutic stance.
* Therapist enables patient to discover their mind in the therapist's mind.
* Tips for therapists to influence mentalizing: create a safe environment, use clear language, explore emotions, reflect on thoughts and feelings, maintain a mentalizing stance, avoid blame or criticism, encourage perspective-taking, and provide empathy and understanding.

### Promoting mentalizing • Maintaining an inquisitive, curious, not-knowing stance • Offering interventions that are simple and to the point • Promoting 

**Mentalizing-Based Therapy (MBT)**

**Promoting mentalizing:**
- Maintain curious, not-knowing stance
- Offer simple interventions
- Emotional engagement: neither too hot nor too cold
- Secure base experience
- Balance exploration of self and others' mental states
- Mirroring process: reflect patient's mental state back to them
- Judicious self-disclosure
- Validate patient's experience before offering alternative perspectives
- Multiple perspectives
- Let patient correct therapist's distorted mentalizing
- Acknowledge mistakes and misunderstandings

**Undermining mentalizing:**
- Striving to be clever or insightful
- Complicated, lengthy interventions
- Presenting ideas with certainty
- Attributing mental states based on theoretical preconceptions
- "Psychobabble"
- Prolonged silences
- Generalizing patient's experiences
- Intense reactive emotion

**Bateman's approach to MBT:**
1. Enhancing mentalization:
   - Adopt mentalizing stance
   - Use it for emotional states
2. Bridging the gap between affects and their representation:
   - Identify affect
   - Interpret moment-by-moment changes
   - Contingent mirroring
   - Understand antecedents
   - Clarify meaning
   - Improve emotional control
   - Place feelings in context
   - Learn to express feelings appropriately
3. Working mostly with current mental states
4. Keeping in mind the patient's deficits

**Mentalized affectivity:**
- Capacity to connect consciousness of an affective state to its experience at a constitutional level
- Establish congruent connections between primary and secondary affect-representational structures

**Working with cognitive states:**
1. Identify maladaptive beliefs
2. Reflect on beliefs about these beliefs and their fears/wishes (aboutness)
3. Identify ruptures in the here-and-now process
4. Mentalize the transference: highlight underlying motives
5. Keep focus on patient's deficits: encourage mentalizing, not expected or demanded

**Long-term effects:**
- Coherent sense of self
- Capacity for second-order regulation of emotional states
- Capacity for secure intimacy

### THE MODULAR APPROACH TO METACOGNITIVE DEVELOPMENT

**Modular Approach to Metacognitive Development (Rome School)**

- Conceptualizes metacognition as a modular function with discrete components or subfunctions
- Identified six specific metacognitive skills, or modules:
  - **Identification**: recognizing and defining inner states (emotions, cognitions)
  - **Differentiation**: recognizing mental events as subjective and distinguishing fantasy from reality
  - **Relating variables**: establishing relations among components of mental states and between mental states and behavior
  - **Integration**: developing coherent descriptions of mental states and showing consistency
  - **Decentration**: comprehending another individual's mental state from a non-egocentric perspective
  - **Mastery**: working through representations and mental states to implement effective action strategies
- Different psychiatric diagnoses manifest specific metacognitive deficits:
  - Borderline Personality Disorder (BPD): poor at differentiation, integration, and mastery
  - Narcissistic Personality Disorder (NPD): good at metacognitive mastery but poor at identifying states of mind in self and others
  - Major Dissociative Disorders: poor at metacognitive identification and integration
- Treatment approach focuses on improving specific deficient metacognitive subfunctions using validation, therapist self-disclosure, and encouraging patient reflection on problematic states.
- Liotti's theory for BPD and DID patients:
  - Result of early childhood disorganized attachment and later abuse
  - Characterized by multiple, fragmented, and incoherent Internal Working Models (IWMs)
  - Destabilized, shifting mental states oscillating between persecutor, rescuer, and victim roles
  - Poor metacognitive capacity, severe relational deficits, deficits in self-integration, emotional dysregulation, and dissociative experiences/behaviors
- Expanded Bowlby's concept of behavioral systems: fear system, attachment system, cooperation and collaboration system, social rank system, and sexual behavioral system.
- Patients with PDs or DDs typically activate non-attachment behavioral systems in response to threat.
- Intervention designed to activate therapeutic behavioral systems within the therapeutic relationship.

### An extraordinarily important point for therapists treating patients with attachment disturbances to remember is Liotti’s discovery that the activation

**Liotti's Discovery and Its Implications for Attachment-Based Therapy**

* Activation of attachment behavioral system in PD and DD patients early in treatment decreases mentalization capacity due to disorganization and overwhelm (Liotti, Prunetti et al., 2008)
* Validation interventions can activate emotional closeness and disorganize the patient's internal working model (IWM), hindering metacognitive abilities (Prunetti et al., 2008)
* Shifting to a collaborative behavioral system when disorganization occurs increases mentalization capacity (Liotti & Gilbert, 2011)
* Metacognitive shifts are motivation-and context-specific in PD and DD patients (Liotti & Gilbert, 2011)

**Collaborative Behavioral System: Conceptual Groundwork**

* The cooperative behavioral system is an innate motivational system necessary for group survival (Cortina & Liotti, 2010; Liotti, 1999)
* Superior cooperative and communicative abilities helped early human groups compete favorably (Cortina & Liotti, 2010)
* PD and DD patients rarely experienced collaboration in their family of origin (Liotti, 2005)
* Attachment-based therapists explicitly introduce collaboration at the onset and throughout treatment (Liotti, 1999)

**Implications for Therapeutic Strategies**

* Shift to collaborative behavioral system when attachment system activation leads to disorganization and decreased mentalization capacity
* Explicitly establish goals and rules of therapeutic work to promote collaboration between patient and therapist (Liotti, 1999)

### A therapist’s openness regarding his or her thinking about the assessment and the treatment plan 151 establishes the cooperative system and the founda

**Collaborative Foundation in Therapy**

- Therapist's openness about assessment and treatment plan establishes cooperative system (Dimaggio et al., 2007)
  * Ongoing transparency about symptom changes and need for alterations
  * Written treatment contract for mutual understanding and agreement
  * Written session summaries after each session
- Collaboration fosters mutual understanding and secure base within the treatment relationship (Liotti, 2005)
  * Sharing, working towards common goal, appreciation, and pleasure
  * Cooperative motives involve feelings of safety and trust

**Parallel Coordinated Treatments for Severe Cases**

- Combination of individual and group therapies with two primary individual therapists (Liotti et al., 2008)
- Second therapist provides support during ruptures, promoting metacognitive reflection
  * Leads to modulation of attachment, improving metacognition, and increasing security

**Cognitive Evolutionary Therapy (CET)**

- Developed by Liotti for inpatient and outpatient treatment of personality disorders, dissociative disorders, and attachment disturbances
- Key goals: helping patients reflect more skillfully on interactions and reaching a cooperative stance (Prunetti et al., 2013)

**Beyond Mentalizing: Mindfulness and Transcendence of Self**

- David Wallin extends mentalizing into the domain of mindfulness (Wallin, 2007)
- Starting point is implicit, nonverbal experience
- Therapy for insecurely attached patients focuses on mindfulness and intersubjectivity.

### Because our first relational experiences are mainly lived outside the domain of language, our crucial internalizations of early relationships register

**Early Relationships and Internal Representations**

- Our first relational experiences are mainly nonverbal and unconsciously internalized as representations, rules, and models (Wallin, 2007).
- These hard-to-reach representations can be modified only by accessing them experientially.
- In therapy, these representations may become accessible through other-than-verbal channels (Wallin, 2007).

**The Unthought Known**

- Wallin calls this nonverbal domain of implicit knowledge the "unthought known" (2007).
- Nonverbal states of mind are reenacted in the transference, evoked in the therapist, and embodied as felt experience.
- Enactment, evocation, and embodiment are primary means for patients to communicate what they know but have not thought (Wallin, 2007).

**Secure Base and Collaborative Communication**

- Wallin's crucible for transformation of experience and the self is the provision of a secure base by the therapist.
- Secure base is promoted through collaborative communication, which involves:
  - Inclusive dialogue of all domains of experience
  - Active identification and repair of ruptures
  - Upgrading dialogue to higher levels of awareness
  - Providing a real relationship with full engagement (Wallin, 2007).

**Engaging Ongoing Experience in Insecurely Attached Patients**

- Three stages of engaging ongoing experience in insecurely attached patients:
  1. Embeddedness: patient is deeply embedded in vicissitudes of ongoing experience with much implicit.
  2. Mentalizing: patient reflects on mental states rather than simply experiencing them.
  3. Mindfulness: directing awareness to moment-by-moment process of experiencing (Wallin, 2007).

**Transition from Mentalizing to Mindfulness**

- Difference between mentalizing and mindfulness: mentalizing makes sense of experience vs. mindfulness being fully present to experience.
- Through mindfulness practice, there is a transition from mentalizing focus to identification with awareness itself (Wallin, 2007).
- Mindfulness offers awareness of reflective self and the difference between reflecting upon experience and being fully present to it.
- Shift in subjectivity from representations of self to awareness itself leads to transcendence of the self (Wallin, 2007).

### A Consensus-Based Model for Attachment Treatment

**Consensus-Based Model for Attachment Treatment**

* Mallinckrodt and associates developed a model of attachment-based treatment based on expert consensus from interviews with therapists specializing in adult attachment issues (Mallinckrodt, Daly, & Wang, 2009).
* Adult attachment theory offers a useful framework for understanding and treating relational problems in adults.
* Attachment system is activated when adults perceive significant threats, making an attachment-based approach suitable for such patients.

**Types of Problematic Attachment Problems in Adults**

* Two main types: deactivating and hyperactivating strategies.

**Deactivating Strategies**

* Inflated self-esteem
* Suppressed attachment-related thoughts and feelings
* Avoidant attachment
* Reduced physiological arousal
* Project unwanted self-traits onto others
* Alexithymia regarding distress

**Hyperactivating Strategies**

* Negative beliefs about coping with distress
* Expect inconsistent responsiveness and availability from others
* Anxious attachment
* Exaggerated appraisal of perceived threats
* Fears of abandonment
* Unable to suppress painful thoughts of separation
* More alert to self-perceived weakness

**Attachment-Based Treatment Phases**

* Engagement phase:
  * Establishing a secure therapeutic relationship as a safe base for exploration.
  * Helping the client form a secure attachment.
  * Regulating closeness and distance in the therapeutic relationship based on patient's attachment style.
  * Encouraging patients with deactivating strategies to focus on their longing for connection.
  * Patient-therapist match should be complementary in terms of attachment styles.
* Working phase:
* Termination phase:

**Engagement Phase Indicators of Successful Engagement**

* Client makes a commitment to change
* Reports behavioral change outside sessions
* Develops tolerance for deeper and more intense affect in sessions
* Shows capacity to "do the work" of therapy

These indicators suggest that a secure therapeutic attachment is forming.

### The main goal of the working phase is to develop a secure attachment in which clients are able to meet their needs consistently through security-based

**Comprehension Notes: Comprehensive Treatment of Attachment Disturbances**

**Goals of Working Phase:**
- Develop a secure attachment with clients
- Reduce hyperactivated patients' need for reassurance
- Reduce deactivated patients' tendency to cut off emotional experience
- Shift from insecure to secure attachment style

**Core Feature:**
- Clients rely on security-based strategies to regulate affect and meet interpersonal needs
- Secure attachment to therapist provides corrective emotional experience

**Termination Phase:**
- Major separation, reactivation of old dysfunctional behaviors expected
- Therapists should be aware of hyperactivating and deactivating strategies during termination

**Consensus Model:**
- Integrates many principles of good attachment treatment
- Valuable contributions from intersubjective and metacognitive fields not explicitly included

**Three Pillars of Attachment Treatment:**
- Detailed in the following chapters
- Combines important published material and clinical experience
- Efficient at creating comprehensive attachment repair

### CHAPTER 7 Introduction to the Three Pillars of Comprehensive Attachment Treatment

**Chapter 7: Three Pillars of Comprehensive Attachment Treatment**

**Introduction:**

- Approximately half of mental health seekers present with relational disturbances instead of psychiatric conditions.
- Understanding relational disturbance and its subtypes is crucial due to its significant impact on psychotherapy.

**Three Types of Relational Disturbance:**

1. **Attachment Disturbance:**
   - Develops between 12-20 months, forming internal working models (IWM) and attachment patterns.
   - Two memory systems develop: behavioral and narrative.
   - Behavioral memory system is present from birth, narrative memory begins at 18-24 months.
   - Attachment disturbance in adults expressed primarily as reenactments in relationships, including transference.
   - Interpretation of transference reenactments may not lead to insight due to the developmental timing of memory systems.

2. **Core Conflictual Relationship Theme (CCRT):**
   - Develops between third and fourth year of life.
   - Characterized by the maturation of narrative memory, complex emotional ideas, stable beliefs, schemas, wishes, needs, fantasies, and defenses.
   - A second layer of relational representation independent of attachment representation.

3. **Object Relations:**
   - Develops during late childhood and adolescence.
   - Involves the development of a sense of self and others as distinct entities with internal thoughts, feelings, and motivations.
   - Object relations are organized into mental structures called "internalized objects."

**Conclusion:**

- Comprehensive attachment treatment addresses all three types of relational disturbance.
- Understanding the developmental stages and memory systems associated with each type is essential for effective treatment.

### The CCRT is a relatively fixed and repeating pattern of a person’s relational expectations and experiences. Based on a patient’s account of his or her

**CCRT (Cycle of Relational Patterns and Transactions)**

- A relatively fixed and repeating pattern of relational expectations and experiences based on past and present relationships
- Consists of wishes, needs, intentions (W), responses from others (RO), and responses from self (RS)
- Formed during childhood due to functioning narrative memory
- More complex and diverse than attachment maps
- Identification and conscious recognition of dysfunctional CCRT patterns can contribute to diminishing their effect

**CCRT Formulation Example:**

- You go into relationships wanting to be appreciated for who you are (W)
- Others commonly respond by never satisfying your needs (RO)
- You then feel not seen, discouraged, and depressed (RS)

**CCRT vs Attachment Maps**

- CCRT maps are more complex and diverse than attachment maps
- CCRTs can be present whether or not a person has attachment disturbance
- Problematic and clinically significant CCRTs can occur in securely attached individuals

**Trauma Bonding**

- Occurs in relationships with significant power differential and intermittent experiences of fright and caring behavior
- Can develop in childhood or adulthood
- Reflects a reactivation of early attachment disturbance, acquired in adulthood, or both
- Shows a pattern of relational disturbance similar to fearful (disorganized) or anxious-preoccupied attachment

**Determining Underlying Causes of Relational Disturbance**

- Accurate determination of underlying causes is essential before treatment
- Assess attachment type using AAI or other measures
- Collect history of relational episodes to develop a CCRT formulation
- Determine if trauma bonding is present
- Decide together whether the relational disturbance is related primarily to attachment, CCRT, or trauma
- Develop a specific treatment plan based on determination

**Treatment Focus**

- If secure attachment with problematic CCRTs or trauma bonding: focus on latter issues
- Significant insecure attachment of one type or another and/or problematic CCRTs or trauma bonding: initially focus on attachment patterns
- Mild-to-moderate anxious or dismissing attachment with CCRT conflict or trauma bonding: clinical judgment needed as to which issue to address first based on interference with relationships

**Resources for Treatment**

- Luborsky, 1984; Strupp and Binder, 1984; Luborsky and Critt-Christoph, 1998; Book, 1998 (CCRT treatment)
- J. G. Allen, 2001; Hassan, 2000, 2009; Landenburger, 1989; van der Kolk, 1989 (Trauma bonding treatment)

### Treating Attachment Disturbance: The Three Pillars

**Treating Attachment Disturbance: The Three Pillars**

* Developed over last 20 years for adult attachment disturbances
* Based on Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) protocol, metacognitive skills development, and collaborative behavior in treatment

**The First Pillar: The Ideal Parent Figure Protocol**

* Focuses on developing new, positive internal representations of attachment
* Assumptions:
  + Attachment behavior develops after 12-20 months, with representations peaking around 18 months
  + Representation of attachment, not behavior, is the main focus for treatment intervention
  + Interpreting dysfunctional attachment patterns does not lead to change
  + Direct introduction of positive attachment imagery creates new, positive attachment map
* Utilizes mental imagery as an efficient way to develop new internal representations

**Background and Core Assumptions**

* Attachment behavior starts in first weeks of life, but specific patterns and underlying representations develop between 12-20 months
* Representation of attachment is the main focus for treatment intervention
* Interpreting dysfunctional attachment patterns does not lead to change
* Direct introduction of positive attachment imagery creates new, positive attachment map

**The Second Pillar: Fostering Metacognitive Skills**

* Addresses limitations in metacognitive capacity
* Helps patients understand their thoughts and emotions related to attachment

**The Third Pillar: Promoting Collaborative Behavior in the Treatment Relationship**

* Encourages nonverbal and verbal collaboration between patient and therapist
* Enhances therapeutic alliance and treatment effectiveness

**Mutually Enhancing Interrelationship of the Three Pillars**

* Each pillar benefits its particular domain of focus
* The application of each one reinforces and enhances the others

### The Five Primary Conditions That Promote Secure Attachment

**Five Primary Conditions That Promote Secure Attachment**

1. **A Sense of Felt Safety:**
   - Child experience: Consistent and reliable protection from danger and threat.
   - Parental behavior: Providing consistent and reliable protection.
   - Benefits: Emotion regulation, key self-functioning capacities.

2. **A Sense of Being Seen and Known:**
   - Child experience: Consistently sensing that parents see and understand them.
   - Parental behavior: Consistent, reliable, and accurate attunement to the child.
   - Benefits: Self-esteem, metacognitive development, key self-functioning capacities.

3. **Experience of Felt Comfort:**
   - Child experience: Receiving consistent, reliable, and timely soothing and reassurance.
   - Parental behavior: Providing consistent, reliable, and timely comfort.
   - Benefits: Key self-functioning capacities.

4. **A Sense of Being Valued:**
   - Child experience: Feeling consistently valued by parents.
   - Parental behavior: Consistent, reliable, and clear expressed delight in the child.
   - Benefits: Self-development, metacognitive development, key self-functioning capacities.

5. **A Sense of Support for Being and Becoming One's Unique, Best Self:**
   - Child experience: Receiving consistent, reliable, unconditional support and encouragement for exploration.
   - Parental behavior: Providing consistent, reliable, unconditional support and encouragement.
   - Benefits: Self-development, metacognitive development, key self-functioning capacities.

**General Factors:**
- Consistency and reliability are common to all parental behavior categories.
- Parental presence and interest are also important factors.

**Benefits of Secure Attachment:**
- Soothing and reassurance: Emotion regulation.
- Attunement and expressed delight: Self-esteem, metacognitive development.
- Attunement and support for exploration: Self-development, metacognitive development.
- Foundation of protection: Supports all self-functions.

### 1. Felt Safety/Protection

**Bowlby's Attachment Theory:**

* Primary function of attachment: protection of species from predators
* Humans: maternal protectiveness leads to felt sense of safety in world and relationship with mother
* Failure of protection can lead to devastating ruptures, harder to heal than abuse memories
* Parental protectiveness not always present or effective
* Secure base provides protection but not overprotection
* IPF protocol introduces protective ideal parent figures for attachment repair

**Bowlby's Attachment Theory (continued):**

* Protection failures lead to insecure attachment patterns
* Therapist co-creates ideal parent figures using IPF protocol
* Ideal parents deeply protective, responsive when harm occurs
* Positive opposites of parents with protection failures.

### 2. Feeling Seen and Known/Attunement

**Attunement and Secure Attachment**

- Attuned parents are carefully attuned to their child's behavior, inner state, and developmental range
- Demonstrate immediate responsiveness, contingent matching, and voice recognition of emotions, needs, motivations, and ideas
- Support present capacities while introducing challenges for optimal development
- Help children find the right skill-to-challenge ratio for optimal functioning and development
- Example: Developmentally attuned parents understand potty training developmental stages and respond accordingly
- Non-attuned parents may engage in power struggles, humiliation, or personal failure experiences, limiting secure attachment formation

**Components of Attunement**

- **Immediate behavior:** Parent's response to the child's current actions
- **Inner state of mind:** Understanding and responding to the child's emotions and thoughts
- **Developmental range:** Adjusting parenting style based on the child's developmental stage

**Effects of Attunement**

- Feeling deeply known and seen by the parent
- Enables attachment repair for adults lacking secure childhood experiences
- Incorporation of consistent attunement and contingent responsiveness into a positive map of secure attachment through imagined ideal parent figures.

### 3. Felt Comfort/Soothing and Reassurance In addition to promoting attachment security, consistent parental soothing and reassurance contributes over t

**Felt Comfort and Soothing**

- Contributes to the development of internal affect regulation (Marian Tolpin, 1971)
- Results from cumulative internalization of repeated soothing behavior by parents
- Parental response includes physical comfort, emotional soothing, and verbal reassurance
- Child experiences "felt comfort" when parent responds
- Repeated experience leads to internal representation of soothing response
- As child develops representational thinking, they can evoke internal representation for self-soothing

**Impact on Insecure Attachment**

- Adult patients with insecure attachment show clinically significant affect-regulation deficits
- Emotion repair and development is done through ideal parent figures (IPF)
- IPF provide physical comfort, emotional soothing, and verbal reassurance during emotional upset
- Affect-regulatory goal: Incorporation into new positive map of secure attachment as soothing-related representations.

**Key Concepts:**

- Felt comfort
- Internal affect regulation
- Cumulative internalization
- Ideal parent figures (IPF)
- Emotion repair and development
- Representation thinking
- Soothing-related representations.

### 4. Feeling Valued/Expressed Delight Consistently expressed delight by parents about and to the child not only promotes secure attachment but is also t

**Self-Esteem Development**

- Defined as linking positive affect states to self-representation (Sander, 1975)
- Narcissistically vulnerable adults failed to link positive affect states directly to their self-representation during childhood
- Healthy self-esteem: backdrop of positive feeling when evoking sense of self in everyday life
- Parents' expressed delight is foundation for healthy self-esteem development
- **Kohut's assertions:** every child needs reinforcement of being seen as "gleam in mother's eye"
- Parents who raise children with healthiest self-esteem: express joy about child's actions and being
- Repeated expressions of joy and delight by parents becomes developmental bridge for linking deeply positive emotional states to child's self-representation
- Modern Western culture parents often focused on job of parenting over joy, preoccupied with what child does rather than who they are
- **IPF method:** supports self-esteem development through ideal parent figures expressing utter joy or delight about child's behavior and being.

### 5. Felt Support for Best Self/Unconditional Support and Encouragement Parents of secure children who develop a strong, autonomous, and complex sense o

**Attachment-Based Imagery for Secure Self-Development**

* Parents of secure children encourage exploration and self-development by providing unconditional support and encouragement.
* Winnicott emphasized the importance of free play and exploration in self-development.
* Secure attachment is essential for healthy self-development, especially for those with anxious-preoccupied or disorganized attachment patterns.
* Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) imagery is used to provide support and encouragement for self-exploration and play.

**Early Stages of Treatment**

* Focus on attachment-promoting conditions: safety/protection, being seen and known/attunement, felt comfort/soothing, and feeling valued/expressed delight.
* IPF imagery serves as a secure base for exploration.
* Goal: foster the development of a strong, autonomous sense of self (the "best self").

**Middle to Late Stages of Treatment**

* Emphasis shifts from secure-base-promoting imagery to exploration-in-the-context-of-secure-base imagery.
* Support for internal and external explorations.
* Goal: further develop a strong, autonomous sense of self.
* Introduction of developmental sequence imagery protocols to promote self-definition, self-organization, self-agency, self-esteem, self-in-relation-to-others, and self in expanded contexts.
* Patient creates a coherent life narrative about themselves.

**Late Stages of Treatment**

* Introduction of imagery of secure intimacy with a partner.
* Goal: establish a positive map for secure intimacy as a reference point for future relationships.

**Overall Purpose**

* Utilize attachment-based imagery to efficiently foster the development of a new, unified positive internal working model (IWM) of secure attachment.
* As the patient uses the new, secure map as their basis of operation in everyday life, outdated insecure maps are eclipsed and reinforced by positive relational experiences.
* Effective treatment brings offline deactivated attachment systems and normalizes hyperactivated attachment systems.

### THE SECOND PILLAR: FOSTERING A RANGE OF METACOGNITIVE SKILLS

**The Second Pillar: Fostering a Range of Metacognitive Skills**

- Research using the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) shows that secure attachment is associated with high coherence of discourse and underlying mind, while insecure attachment is linked to low coherence. (Main, 1984)
- Coherence on AAI scales is strongly correlated with metacognitive capacity, or the ability to be aware of one's own and others' minds and mental activities. (Main et al., 2002)
- Individuals who score high on the AAI Metacognitive Processes scale are likely to have secure attachment patterns. (Main et al., 2002)
- Peter Fonagy, Anthony Bateman, and others at the London School discovered that enhancing metacognitive capacity through specific interventions contributes to the development of high coherence and secure attachment in patients with clinically significant attachment disturbances. (Fonagy et al., 1998)
- Mentalization-based treatment (MBT) was developed based on this finding, focusing on fostering curiosity about states of mind in self and others. (Bateman & Fonagy, 2004)
- MBT involves a process of joint awareness where the patient's mental states are explored, reducing reliance on content and encouraging multiple perspectives. (Bateman & Fonagy, 2004)
- A therapist adopts a "mentalizing stance" to help patients develop metacognitive skills by consistently questioning why individuals say or behave in certain ways. (Bateman & Fonagy, 2004)
- Mentalization entails exploring a mind by a mind and leads to the development of general metacognitive capacity in the patient. (Fonagy et al., 1998)
- The Reflective Functioning Scale (RF-S) assesses reflective capacity, which is the psychological processes underlying the ability to mentalize. (Fonagy et al., 1998)
- MBT significantly contributes to the development of reflective function, enabling patients to become more aware of their immediate mental states, label and understand affective and cognitive states, identify limitations of knowledge, contextualize states of mind, and mentalize about others' states of mind and transference in therapy. (Fonagy et al., 1998)
- The process of mentalizing through MBT can lead to the development of new positive representations for intimate relationships by enabling patients to create mental representations of themselves and their therapists. (Fonagy et al., 2002)

### The London group’s findings that mentalization-based methods contribute to reflective capacity, to increasing coherence of mind, and ultimately to ear

**London Group Findings:**

- Mentalization-based methods contribute to reflective capacity, coherence of mind, and earned security.
- Metacognitive function is associated with coherence of mind and attachment security.
- Essential component of attachment-based treatment: developing metacognitive, reflective capacity.

**Limitations of London Approach:**

- Assumes reflective capacity is a unitary construct.
- All types of metacognition result from a single reflective function process.

**Rome School Findings:**

- Specific metacognitive deficits associated with specific diagnostic conditions.
- Metacognitive mastery, differentiation, and integration capacities vary between disorders.
- Condition-specific approach: identify and develop deficient metacognitive abilities for each patient.

**Shared Beliefs:**

- Specific metacognitive skills are remarkably absent in patients of specific diagnostic groups.
- Consistent focus on metacognition improves general reflective capacity and functioning.

**Differences between Approaches:**

- London: general approach, fosters development of general reflective capacity.
- Rome: condition-specific approach, identifies and develops deficient metacognitive abilities for each patient.

**Combined Approach:**

- Identify specific metacognitive abilities lacking in a patient.
- Foster the development of those specific abilities while supporting general reflective capacity.

**Expanded Model of Cognitive Development:**

- Beyond formal operational thinking (London approach).
- 12 stages of cognitive development, including postformal stages.
- Fosters higher-level metacognitive skills for overall mental health.

**Metacognitive Skills Relevant to Attachment-Based Psychotherapy:**

- Strengthen existing skills and promote the development of advanced skills.
- London and Rome groups' identified skills plus more advanced skills.

**Categories of Metacognitive Potentials:**

- Basic: fundamental metacognitive abilities.
- Intermediate: metacognitive abilities that build on basic skills.
- Advanced: metacognitive abilities representative of higher levels of cognitive development.

### Basic metacognitive skills 1. Awareness of the state of mind of self or other 2. Monitoring the accuracy of state of mind 3. Awareness of one’s own in

**Metacognitive Skills**

_Basic Metacognitive Skills_
- Awareness of state of mind (own or others)
  * Monitoring accuracy
  * Influence on behavior
- Regulation of state of mind
- Awareness of action plans and goal-directedness
- Meaning-making

_Intermediate Metacognitive Skills_
- Recognition of past influences
- Relativity of states of mind
- Seeing beyond information given
- Optimizing action plans
- Sensitivity to contextual effects
- Perspective-taking

_Advanced Metacognitive Skills_
- Wider, supersystemic perspective
- Awareness of past/present, self/other orientations
- Degree of organization or coherence of mind
- Recognition of interdependence
- Articulating ultimate concerns
- Direct awareness of wider reality
- Highest-order metacognitive skills (spacious freedom, wisdom)

**Assumptions and Goals of Treatment**
- Increase patient's facility with overall mentalizing and various metacognitive skills
- Achieve earned security and high coherence on Attachment Anxiety Inventory (AAI)
- Advancement to and stabilization of higher levels of adult cognitive development
- Fostering the widest possible range of metacognitive skills is a second pillar of comprehensive approach to treating attachment disturbance.

### THE THIRD PILLAR: FOSTERING COLLABORATIVE NONVERBAL AND VERBAL BEHAVIOR

**The Third Pillar: Fostering Collaborative Behavior, Nonverbal Communication, and Verbal Communication**

* Human species is uniquely cooperative and collaborative in three ways: cooperative shared behavior, nonverbal communication, and verbal communication (Gilbert, 1989; Tomasello, 2009)
* Securely attached infants exhibit collaborative nonverbal and verbal communication with caregivers (Beebe & Lachmann, 2014; Tomasello, 2010)
* Infants in insecure attachment systems deactivate their natural tendencies toward collaborativeness due to caregiving inadequacies (unresponsiveness, inconsistency, overinvolvement, rejection, etc.)
* Collaborative partnership is crucial for patients with insecure attachment, personality disorders, dissociative disorders, or psychotic disorders (Liotti, 2005)

**Fostering Collaborativeness: Therapist's Stance and Interventions**

* Attachment-based therapists view therapy as a mutually regulated process of collaboration
* Establishing a collaborative treatment frame at the onset of therapy (clear understanding of expectations for both patient and therapist)
* Confronting noncollaborative behaviors from either party (patient or therapist)
* Maintaining shared, collaborative responsibility

**Behavior: Collaborative Treatment Frame and Nonverbal Communication**

* Developing a collaboratively established treatment frame
* Confronting noncollaborative behaviors that interfere with therapy
* Careful observation and identification of mismatches in nonverbal communication
* Increasing nonverbal matching between patient and therapist
* Verbally bringing the patient's attention to impaired nonverbal patterns

**Verbal Communication: Coherent, Cooperative Discourse**

* Poor verbal collaborativeness in adults with insecure attachment often manifests through deviations from Grice's maxims of coherent, cooperative discourse
* Insecure preoccupied adults make irrelevant comments, wander off topic, lose track of the point, and use passive, vague, or jargon expressions
* Insecure dismissing adults do not express important points in sufficient detail and depth
* Pointing out patterns of noncollaborative verbal behavior and exploring their antecedent states of mind
* Developing better metacognitive awareness of these patterns and their precipitants
* Replacing noncollaborative verbalization with more collaborative expressions

**Overall Outcome: Consistently Collaborative Dialogue and Enhanced Verbalization Capacity**

* More consistently collaborative dialogue based on coherent, cooperative verbal expression throughout treatment
* Enhancement of collaborative verbalization capacity and behavior outside of therapy.

### The Interdependence of the Three Pillars of Treatment

**Three Pillars of Treatment: Attachment, Metacognitive Development, and Collaborativeness**

* The methods for fostering collaborativeness in therapy are collaborative by nature and enhance metacognitive development
* The attachment system, peer-collaborative behavioral system, and metacognitive system are interdependent in treatment
* Six interrelationships between the three pillars:

**Attachment Imagery Promotes Metacognitive Development**

* Engaging with ideal parent figure imagery promotes metacognitive awareness
* Represents healthy attachment, builds metacognitive awareness, and experiences metacognitive mastery

**Metacognitive Development Promotes Attachment**

* Teaches patients to be aware of their states of mind and attachment patterns
* Provides windows into the therapist's state of mind
* Supports accurate and realistic internal representations of attachment figures

**Collaborativeness Promotes Metacognitive Development**

* Collaborative goal of therapy development of metacognitive skills
* Therapist models and emphasizes collaboration
* Patient becomes more aware of noncollaborative behaviors and develops greater collaborative behavior

**Metacognitive Development Promotes Collaborativeness**

* Calling attention to noncollaborative treatment-frame behavior and nonverbal/verbal behaviors promotes metacognitive awareness
* Patient is more likely to develop greater collaborative behavior

**Collaborativeness Promotes Attachment**

* Mutually respectful, collaborative relationship promotes positive, healthy Internal Working Model (IWM) or attachment map
* Collaborative therapy relationship and Ideal Parent Figure protocol build positive attachment representations

**Attachment Imagery Promotes Collaborativeness**

* Patients develop positive attachment imagery in the Ideal Parent Figure protocol
* Builds an experience of a mutually collaborative relationship with ideal parent figures
* Enhances overall collaborativeness and adds collaborative elements to the developing internal working model of attachment.

### The Benefits of Three Pillars Treatment

**Three Pillars Treatment:** A therapeutic approach for patients with attachment disturbances that includes:

1. **Repeated engagement with ideal parent figure imagery:**
   - Formation of new, positive stable internal working models for healthy adult attachment

2. **Promoting a wide range of metacognitive skills:**
   - Manifestation of a range of metacognitive skills, including:
     * Higher-order metacognitive skills utilized in everyday life
     - Achievement of some level of postformal cognitive development
   - No longer meeting diagnostic criteria for a personality or dissociative disorder

3. **Fostering behavioral, nonverbal, and verbal collaborativeness:**
   - Manifestation of healthy behavioral, nonverbal, and verbal collaborative behaviors
   - Achievement of high coherence of discourse and mind on the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI)
   - Capacity for secure intimacy in adulthood

**Primary Indicators of Successful Attachment-Based Treatment:**

- Formation of new, positive stable internal working models for healthy adult attachment
- Manifestation of a range of metacognitive skills
- Achievement of some level of postformal cognitive development
- Manifestation of healthy behavioral, nonverbal, and verbal collaborative behaviors
- Achievement of high coherence of discourse and mind on the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI)
- No longer meeting diagnostic criteria for a personality or dissociative disorder
- Capacity for secure intimacy in adulthood

### CHAPTER 8 The First Pillar The Ideal Parent Figure Protocol Overview: Positive Remapping

**Chapter 8: The First Pillar**

**Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) Protocol Overview:**

- Therapist facilitates development of a positive, stable inner working model of attachment relationships.
- Uses imagery to remap attachment representations from insecure to secure.
- Patient engages with imagery of positive attachment figures and secure experiences.
- Repetition and elaboration establish new internal model of secure attachment.

**Skillful Use:**

1. Familiarity with the tool.
2. Recognizing patient's unique patterns and needs.
3. Understanding caretaking qualities that promote secure attachment.

**IPF vs Traditional Attachment-Based Treatments:**

- Primary agent of change is patient's relationship with imagined attachment figures.
- Applied in therapeutic relationship context.
- Therapist embodies qualities that promote secure attachment.

**Qualities That Promote Secure Attachment:**

1. Physical presence, reliability, consistency, and interest.
2. Protection when patient is at risk.
3. Accurate attunement to behavior, state, developmental level.
4. Soothing and reassuring.
5. Expressing delight or positive regard.
6. Support for inner and outer exploration.

**Therapist's Role in IPF Protocol:**

- Present, reliable, consistent, and interested.
- Protective when needed.
- Attuned to patient's experience.
- Soothing and reassuring.
- Expresses delight in positive experiences.
- Embodies support for exploration.

**Incorporating Attachment-Promoting Qualities:**

- Therapist recognizes, mirrors, and enhances qualities that promote secure attachment as they appear in patient's imagery.
- Draws from patient's unique attachment history to suggest specific attachment-promoting qualities if they do not appear spontaneously.

**IPF Process:**

- No preestablished scripts.
- Effective when therapist understands underlying principles and responds freshly during each session with a particular patient.

### The Foundational Protocol SETTING THE THERAPEUTIC GROUND

**IPF Protocol: Setting the Therapeutic Ground**

* The therapist helps the patient establish a calm, relaxed, inwardly focused state of awareness
* Focusing on body experience enhances attachment formation during critical period (12-20 months)
* Establishing this state increases vividness of imagery and decreases distractions

**Establishing a Calm, Relaxed State**

* Patient settles comfortably into chair, letting the body relax
* Therapist guides patient to focus on body sensations and feelings
* Additional guidance may be necessary for anxious or distracted patients
* Dissociative patients may enter preparatory state easily

**Deepening the Inwardly Focused State**

* Charcot's method: eyes open during inhalation, close during exhalation
* Therapist makes supportive comments and enhances focus on calming and relaxation
* Patient becomes more aware of exhalations and releases tension with each exhale
* Final step: brief body scan to find most comfortable area and focus on it

**Preparing the Patient for Subsequent Imagery Work**

* Therapist is free to choose methods based on understanding of preparatory phase principles.

### FACILITATING THE CREATION OF IDEAL PARENT FIGURE IMAGERY

**Facilitating the Creation of Ideal Parent Figure Imagery**

* Patients engage with self-generated, affect-rich imagery of being a child in the presence of positive attachment figures
* Therapist sets up conditions to evoke child experience and ideal parent figure imagery
* Evoke child experience: patients imagine themselves as young children and feel the experience
* Prompt for ideal parent figure imagery: patients create fictional parents who promote secure attachment
* Use "imagine" instead of "visualize" to allow multi-sensory experiences
* Inquire about patient's imagery and assess adequacy

**Setting up the Conditions**

* Evoke child experience:
  * Patients imagine themselves as young children
  * Indicate when child experience is evoked
* Prompt for ideal parent figure imagery:
  * Patients create ideal parents, not actual or known figures
  * Parents have qualities that help patient feel safe and secure
  * Use "imagine" instead of "visualize"

**Assessing the Adequacy of Imagery**

* Ideal imagery:
  * Multisensory experience of two parent figures
  * Not actual or known parents
  * Parents behave in attachment-promoting ways
* Adequate imagery:
  * At least one parent figure not actual or known
  * Contact through some sensory modality
  * Absence of harmful behaviors

**Common Challenges**

* If no parent figures are imagined, focus on evoking the imagery and encouraging creativity
* If patient indicates actual parents, provide guidance for letting go and creating fictional ideal parents
* Additional prompting and correction may be needed for more severe attachment disorders.

### CLINICAL VIGNETTES

**IDEAL PARENT FIGURE THERAPY: CLINICAL VIGNETTES**

* Illustrative introduction to ways adequate or better ideal parent figure imagery may manifest during early sessions
* Patients' experiences and therapist responses for supporting and enhancing positive remapping process

**First Session:**

* Patient feels cared for and loved by imagined parents
* Therapist reinforces focus on parents' ways of being
* Patient experiences calmness, security, proximity-seeking, and fun with parents

**Third Session:**

* Patient describes imagined parents as kind, attentive, close by, caring, nurturing, and playful
* Therapist reinforces focus on parents' ways of being rather than actions
* Patient experiences happiness, energy, and security

**Fifth Session:**

* Patient feels comforted by imagined parents' demeanor, caring, and nurturing qualities
* Therapist reinforces focus on parents' interest in patient's experience
* Patient experiences happiness, energy, and positive self-esteem

**Common Themes:**

* Therapist emphasizes the importance of focusing on parents' ways of being rather than actions
* Patients describe imagined parents as caring, nurturing, attentive, and interested in their experience
* Patients experience positive emotions (calmness, happiness, energy) and security when interacting with imagined parents
* Therapist reinforces focus on patients' inner experiences and feelings to anchor positive states in the body.

### As noted in the commentaries about the above two vignettes, the therapist’s language oriented the patient’s focus to the parent figures’ ways of being

**Therapy Sessions Focusing on Parent Figures' Ways of Being**

* Therapist's language in sessions orients patient's focus to parent figures' ways of being, not just what they do
* Instrumental or caretaking behaviors are common for patients to report and not inherently problematic
* Therapist validates positive caretaking actions and refocuses attention on the more important quality from which it comes

**Example of Reporting Instrumental Love**

* Patient reports parent figures helping with tasks and making things
* Therapist acknowledges and validates actions while emphasizing positive qualities of being

**Establishing Ideal Parent Figure Imagery in IPF Sessions**

* Patients with insecure attachment may find it challenging to establish clear imagery
* Therapist provides support by expressing belief in patient's ability to create imagery and affirming importance of their preferences
* Shaping parent figure imagery to emphasize child's preferences, interests, and needs promotes secure attachment

**Patient with Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment (E2)**

* Therapist anticipates patient may focus more on parent figures' states and needs than her own
* Shaping parent figure imagery to emphasize child's preferences and interests can help balance attention
* Spontaneous reporting of parents being reassuring is beneficial, and therapist can further shape IPF imagery with positive opposites of problematic qualities in actual parents.

### The therapist pointed the patient’s attention to her own state of mind and then mirrored her positive states and suggested a felt sense of security (“

**Principles and Practices for Framing and Responding to Patients' Experiences in Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) Sessions**

**General Guiding Principles:**

- Therapist acknowledges patient's current state of mind and emotions.
- Therapist mirrors positive states and validates experiences.
- Therapist shapes imagery to be accepting, patient, protective, and attuned.
- Validation of the primacy of the patient's own experience and learning.
- Use of gentle push towards realizing developmental potential.
- Importance of self-validation in the process.

**Interaction between Patient and Therapist:**

1. **Patient expresses lack of security:** The therapist recognizes the patient's doubt and incorporates it into the imagery process, indicating that parent figures are attuned to the patient's internal state.
2. **Therapist suggests feeling secure:** Although the patient does not feel secure at that moment, this suggestion serves as a test and an expression of confidence in her developmental potential.
3. **Patient asserts own experience:** The patient's willingness to assert her own experience against the therapist's suggestion is an important self-validation signifying the possibility of security.

**Variability in Patients' Responses:**

- Wide range of attachment insecurity types and manifestations.
- Variability in patients' responses to IPF sessions, from vivid and detailed imagery to imagining harmful or no parent figures.

**Addressing Challenges:**

- Particular challenges addressed later in the chapter.
- Considerations for each insecure attachment subtype addressed in Chapters 11, 12, and 13.

### GENERAL CONTEXT PRINCIPLES

**Int Intensive Parenting Formula (IPF) Method**

- Based on patient's active engagement with ideal parent figure imagery
- Therapist's way of being facilitates imagery work and attachment repair
- Good-enough therapist embodies: physical presence, consistency, reliability, interest
- Foundation for "five behavioral pillars" promoting secure attachment

**Therapist's Role in IPF Process**

- Helps patient establish calm, relaxed state
- Welcomes and accepts whatever arises during protocol
- Responds attentively to patient's moment-to-moment experience
- Expresses delight in patient's reports of ideal parent figures
- Encourages exploration of inner and outer world
- Dedicated to protecting patient's wellbeing

**Patient's Ideal Parent Figure Imagery**

- Frame for developing attachment security
- Patient imagines parents who provide safety and security
- Patient accesses unique needs, projecting qualities into imagined parents
- Foundation based on patient's deepest knowing of what was missing during childhood

**IPF Process**

- Therapist asks patient to imagine ideal parents
- Patient accesses own awareness of attachment needs
- Imagined parents have foundation in patient's unique needs
- Promotes attachment repair through relational experience.

### SHAPE THE PATIENT’S IMAGERY AND EXPERIENCE

**Attachment-Focused Imagery Protocol**

* Therapist shapes patient's imagery and experience towards attachment security
* Patient describes parent figures and experiences with them
* Therapist points attention to attachment-promoting qualities
	+ Notice ways they're being with you
	+ Notice how they're promoting security
	+ Reinforces creative and generative process
* Mirror and enhance patient's descriptions of attachment-promoting qualities
	+ Use patient's words and phrases
	+ Build upon literal expressions
	+ Validate and strengthen confidence in imagery and process
* Effective mirroring contributes to power of imagery to create new, positive attachment representations
* Therapist's marked expressivity reproduces beneficial developmental circumstance

**Patient's Experience**

* Deeply felt experience of imagined parent figures
* Focus on ways they promote attachment security
* Stay engaged with the imagery and experience

**Therapist's Role**

* Frequently ask patient to describe parent figures and experiences
* Point attention towards attachment-promoting qualities
	+ Notice how they're being with you
	+ Reinforce creative and generative process
* Mirror and enhance patient's descriptions of attachment-promoting qualities
	+ Use patient's words and phrases
	+ Build upon literal expressions
	+ Validate and strengthen confidence in imagery and process
* Effective mirroring contributes to power of imagery to create new, positive attachment representations
* Marked expressivity reproduces beneficial developmental circumstance.

### SUGGEST ATTACHMENT-PROMOTING QUALITIES IN THE IMAGINED PARENT FIGURES

**Attachment-Promoting Qualities in Imagined Parent Figures**

- Therapist encourages patients to imagine ideal parent figures with attachment-promoting qualities
- Patients may struggle to generate these qualities, so therapist provides suggestions
- Suggestions include:
  * Correcting focus on parent figures' doing and highlighting underlying qualities of being
  * Introducing general principles of secure attachment formation
  * Introducing positive opposites of problematic aspects of patients' experiences with actual parents

**Correcting for Instrumental Caretaking**

- Patients may describe parent figures engaging in instrumental actions, but these actions don't necessarily promote attachment security
- Therapist mirrors and enhances any attachment-promoting qualities present in the patient's imagery
- Suggestions include:
  * Pointing attention to positive qualities of being underlying the action
  * Linking the action, affective state, and relationship to provide mirroring and shaping

**Examples of Therapist Interventions**

Patient: Mother is making dinner for me.
- Therapist mirrors action and suggests attachment-promoting qualities of being: happy, there with patient, making what patient enjoys

Patient: Mother is brushing my hair.
- Therapist mirrors action and suggests attachment-promoting qualities of being: present, soothing, loving, interested, caring

### Suggest that the imagined parent figures have qualities that are known in general to support secure attachment. In response to particular attachment-p

**Imagined Parent Figures: Qualities to Support Secure Attachment**

**General Factors**
- *PHYSICAL PRESENCE*
  - Genuinely accessible and available
  - Consistently present when needed
  - Special relationship with reliable availability

- *CONSISTENCY*
  - Consistent ways of being over time
  - Know what helps the individual feel secure and safe
  - Provide consistent support every time

- *RELIABILITY*
  - Always there in needed ways
  - Trustworthy and understanding
  - Always turn to them for help

- *INTEREST*
  - Genuinely interested in individual's thoughts and feelings
  - Happy to learn about the individual
  - Attentive and engaged

**Behavioral Conditions**
- *PROTECTION*
  - Dedicated to protecting the individual from harm
  - Provide a sense of safety
  - Always watch out for the individual's wellbeing

- *ATTUNEMENT*
  - Attuned to the individual's needs and feelings
  - Notice shifts in behavior and internal state
  - Know how much is right for developmental stage

- *SOOTHING AND REASSURANCE*
  - Provide comfort when upset
  - Help the individual accept and contain strong feelings
  - Reassure and accept inner experience

- *EXPRESSED DELIGHT*
  - Delighted to be the parent
  - Communicate delight in the individual's being
  - Encourage exploration and discovery

**Suggestions for Imagined Parent Figures**
- Suggest qualities that support secure attachment from "four general factors" and "five conditions"
- Link suggestions to patient's descriptions of parent figures
- Use patient's language and experiences to enhance the imagery
- Counteract developmentally disruptive ways of actual parents with positive opposites in imagined parent figures.

### Information about a patient’s early experiences with parents can be obtained in several ways. The Adult Attachment Interview (AAI; George, Kaplan, & M

**Obtaining Information about a Patient's Early Parental Experiences**

* The Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) was developed to obtain attachment status information.
* Conducted by trained therapists or referred to qualified administrators.
* Yields adjectives describing patient's relationships with mothers and fathers during early childhood.
* Negative qualities indicate parental relationship issues, positive opposites guide IPF sessions.

**AAI Transcript and Scoring**

* Reveals nature of parental relationships and contributing qualities.
* Valuable for therapists in shaping imagined parent figures.
* Positive opposites of negative adjectives:
  * Relationship with mother: Calm, Present, Accepting, Safe, Warm, Attuned caretaking, Clear, Predictable, Relaxed, and Attuned playfulness.
  * Relationship with father: Responsible, Calm, Present, Warm, Attuned caretaking, Clear, Predictable, Relaxed, and Attuned playfulness.

**IPF Sessions with Positive Opposites**

* Therapist suggests qualities of imagined parent figures based on positive opposites.
* Calm, Present, Accepting, Safe, Warm, Attuned caretaking, Clear, Predictable, Relaxed, and Attuned playfulness.
* Patients may accept suggestions or have difficulty recognizing positive opposite qualities.
* Therapist's repetition supports appearance and stability in patient's imagery.

**Alternative Method: Exploring Parental Relationships during Initial Evaluation**

* If AAI transcript unavailable, explore parental relationships in detail during history-taking.
* Obtain as much detail as possible about parents' typical ways of being and patient's experiences.
* Transform negative feelings into positive opposites for IPF sessions.

**Single Parent or Caregiver Household**

* Focus on patient's experience with single parent.
* Probe for feelings related to absence of other parent.
* Transform negative feelings into positive opposites for ideal father figure.

### CONTEXTUALIZE THE PATIENT’S EXPERIENCES WITHIN THE IPF FRAME

**Intro:** In Intensive Psychotherapy for Attachment (IPF), the therapist establishes an imagery frame during a session, which becomes the context for the patient's experiences. The therapist responds to the patient based on the parent figures' responses within this frame.

**Maintaining Engagement with Parent Figures:**

* Patients may experience fear or resistance towards parent figures in the imagery frame.
* Therapist responds by acknowledging and validating the patient's feelings, while encouraging the parent figures to help the patient feel safe and supported.
* The goal is for patients to experience the parent figures as beneficially responding to their entire range of experiences.

**Difficulties with Parent Figure Imagery:**

1. **Separation or distance:** Patients may feel physically or emotionally separated from the parent figures.
   * Therapist responds by acknowledging the resistance and encouraging the parent figures to help bridge the gap, allowing the patient to choose how to connect.
2. **Decrease in engagement:** Patients may disengage from the imagery frame and the parent figures.
   * Therapist responds by validating the patient's feelings and encouraging the parent figures to continue showing their love and support, even if it feels too perfect or unreal.
3. **Mistrust or emotional distancing:** Patients may struggle with trusting or emotionally connecting with the parent figures.
   * Therapist responds by acknowledging the patient's mistrust and encouraging the parent figures to show understanding and patience, allowing the patient to gradually build trust.
4. **Uncomfortable affective states:** Patients may experience uncomfortable emotions during the IPF process.
   * Therapist responds by validating the patient's feelings and encouraging the parent figures to help the patient process and manage these emotions in a safe and supportive way.

**Examples of Difficulties and Responses:**

* **Separation or distance:** Patient feels separated from parents by a gauzy, hazy material; therapist encourages parents to help patient connect by pulling aside the material.
* **Decrease in engagement:** Patient experiences parent figures as perfect but unreal; therapist validates the patient's feelings and encourages parents to continue showing love and support.
* **Mistrust or emotional distancing:** Patient does not see any parents; therapist encourages patient to create ideal parents who will help him/her feel safe and supported.
* **Uncomfortable affective states:** Patient feels overwhelmed by parent figures' love and perfection; therapist validates the patient's feelings and encourages parents to continue showing understanding and patience, allowing the patient to gradually accept their love.

### At the beginning of this session, the patient was very engaged with the imagery and the process and generated a vivid, positive interaction with the p

**Imaginal Exposure Therapy (IPF) Session:**

**Engagement and Doubt:**
- Patient was engaged with the IPF process and experienced positive interactions with parent figures.
- Doubt emerged, but patient continued to experience positive affect towards parents.
- Patient withdrew from the frame, expressing it as "faking."
- Therapist maintained the presence of the frame by acknowledging patient's doubt and suggesting that parents understood his perspective.
- Therapist encouraged patient to reengage with the imagery by highlighting how parent figures protected him and supported him in dealing with doubt.

**Reengagement:**
- Patient reengaged with the imagery, experiencing parental reassurance and support.
- However, his reengagement was short-lived as he felt the experiences were still "too perfect" and not real.
- Therapist pointed back to the frame, acknowledging patient's difficulty in accepting it but emphasizing that parent figures recognized this.

**Maintaining Connection:**
- Patient experienced intermittent withdrawal from the IPF frame, expressing mistrust or emotional distancing towards parent figures.
- Therapist acknowledged and validated patient's experiences, suggesting that parent figures were attuned to his needs and would promote trust and safety when he was ready.

**Affective States:**
- During IPF sessions, therapist watches for signs of affective distress in the patient and suggests that parent figures provide soothing and reassurance.
- Therapist is attuned to nonverbal indications of affective shifts and brings them into the imagery frame.
- When patient reports an affective shift, therapist names the state and encourages patient to take in the parental reassurance and support.

### In the following example, the patient reported an affective shift and kept it in the IPF frame herself: Patient: I’m feeling angry at them. I want to 

**Intro:**

- Patient reports feeling angry and seeks validation in IPF frame
- Therapist encourages patient to feel emotions and observes their response from imaginary parents

**Patient's Experience in IPF Frame:**

* Patient feels understood and respected by imaginary parents
* They take her emotions seriously, listen attentively, and don't laugh at her
* They stay with her through her anger
* She feels a sense of respect from them

**Patient's Childhood Experience:**

* Previous experience of mother laughing at her when angry

**When Patient Cannot Respond in IPF Frame:**

* Therapist suggests scene change if patient is unable to respond or receive relief
* New scene appears with ideal parents providing comfort and help

**Effect of Scene Change:**

* Patient feels more calm and open to receiving help from imaginary parents
* Therapist's action reflects attunement, protection, and soothing for the patient.

### SUGGEST CHANGES IN SCENES OR NEW SCENES WITH THE IDEAL PARENT FIGURES

**Ideal Parent Figures (IPF) Sessions: Changes in Scenes or New Scenes**

**Benefits of Changing Scenes with Ideal Parent Figures:**

* Consolidation of positive attachment experiences
* Generalization across various situations and contexts
* Opportunity for patients to project additional attachment-promoting qualities

**Changes within Scenes:**

* Suggesting changes within scenes is best during early IPF sessions
* Open-worded suggestions leave creation of specific parental ways to the patient
* Example: Therapist suggests calm, happy, and fully present parent figures

**Changes of Scenes:**

* After several constructive IPF sessions
* Patient given freedom to project qualities onto new scene's parent figures
* Example: Therapist suggests warm, easygoing, and trustworthy parent figures

**Timing of Suggesting Scene Changes:**

* When patient is having difficulty that parent figures cannot help with
* When patient has been having positive experiences
* Mirror and enhance positive experiences until their attachment-promoting benefits peak
* Intuition-based recognition of scene's purpose fulfillment

**Bring Attention to Experience and Amplify Positive States:**

* Central to IPF method's contribution to attachment repair
* Therapist points patient's attention to experiences of parent figures' attachment-promoting ways
* Example: Therapist suggests comforting, then asks patient to report feelings and amplifies them
* Amplification enhances awareness and differentiation of affects, facilitates introjection of positive attachment representations.

### Phrasings for bringing attention to felt experience. The therapist can be creative with phrasings, guided by the simple goal of pointing the patient’s

**Phrasings for Bringing Attention to Felt Experience**

* Therapist can use creative phrasings to draw patient's attention to positive states elicited by attachment-promoting qualities in ideal parent figures.
* Phrases may include: "How does that feel?", "Notice what happens inside you as they...", "Feel what that’s like.", etc.

**Phrasings for Amplifying Positive States**

* Therapist can use tonally expressive language to enhance the impact of positive states described by the patient.
* Phrases may include: "Really feel what that's like.", "You can feel that [happiness] even more strongly now.", etc.

**Phrasings for Stabilizing and Extending Positive States**

* Once a patient experiences positive states, therapist can use phrases to help them hold onto these feelings.
* Phrases may include: "Focus on that feeling of [safety]", "Notice what it’s like to begin to hold that feeling of [safety] continuously in your awareness.", etc.

**Anchoring Positive States in Body Experience**

* Linking positive states to bodily sensations can help strengthen the attachment repair process.
* Phrases may include: "Notice what that feels like now in the body.", "Feel it in your body.", etc.

During the critical period of attachment formation (12-20 months), identity is primarily based on body experience and memory is behavioral/enacted rather than mental/narrative. Therefore, linking positive states to bodily sensations can facilitate the formation of new attachment representations. The therapist may use attention-directing, amplifying, and stabilizing phrases to help patients connect with their felt experiences and strengthen their attachment bonds.

### INTEGRATE METHODS FOR ENHANCING POSITIVE STATES

**Integrating Positive States in Psychotherapy**

* Enhancing positive states: making patients aware of and familiar with positive feelings towards parent figures
* Elements for enhancing positive states: amplifying, stabilizing, extending, anchoring
* Therapist's role: guiding the patient to integrate these elements

**Interaction Example:**

* Patient expresses feeling comforted by parents
* Therapist guides patient to focus on and feel the sensation of comfort in their body
* Patient becomes more familiar with the feeling, leading to continuous experience
* Scene changes to enhance comfort further

**IPF Session Conclusion:**

* Longer focus on stabilizing, extending, anchoring, and integrating positive states
* Suggesting that patients retain good feelings as scene fades away
* Creating a bridge for smooth transition back to therapy room

**End of Session:**

* Therapist suggests that patients keep positive feelings as scene fades away
* Bridge created for conscious awareness to return smoothly to the therapy room.

### REORIENT THE PATIENT TO THE PRESENT

**IPF (Imaginal Exposure with Parent Figures) Process**

* Reorienting the patient to present time is crucial in IPF process, as some patients may have vivid experiences of being a child during sessions.
* Therapist should allow but not encourage patients to speak about their session experience, addressing any unpleasant feelings or thoughts before ending the session.
* Normalize and reassure patients about negative experiences, acknowledging that doubts and uncertainties are common and indicative of progress in IPF treatment.

**Post-IPF Interaction**

* Patients may doubt the parent figures and IPF frame during sessions.
* Therapist should validate their experience, normalize doubts, and reassure patients that it's a part of the process.

**Signs of Progress in IPF Treatment**

1. **Engagement with attachment-promoting imagery:**
   * Patients with deactivated or imbalanced attachment systems may have difficulty engaging with parent figures.
   * Indications of progress include reports of being with parent figures who show essential qualities like physical presence or interest.
2. **Beneficial effects of positive attachment representations:**
   * Patients begin to experience the benefits of secure attachment, such as feeling soothed and comforted by the parent figures.
3. **Manifestations of an internalized secure base:**
   * Patients start to develop a sense of security within themselves, leading to improved emotional regulation and increased self-confidence.

**Engagement with Attachment-Promoting Imagery**

* Patients show varying degrees and forms of initial engagement with imagined parent figures based on their attachment system's deactivation or imbalance.
* Indications of progress include reports of being with parent figures who display essential qualities like physical presence or interest.
* Engagement is indicated by strong clarity, vividness, close felt connection, and generative responses in the patient's imagery.

### SIGNS OF THE BENEFICIAL EFFECTS OF POSITIVE ATTACHMENT REPRESENTATIONS

**Positive Attachment Representations and Their Beneficial Effects**

* Activation of attachment-promoting imagery is necessary for beneficial effects
* Indications of progress include:
  * Emotion regulation
    * Decreased emotional discomfort or distress
    * Modulation of parent figures' behavior
  * Experience of safety
    * Feeling secure and relaxed
    * Trust and calmness
  * Proximity-seeking
    * Hugging and being close to parent figures
    * Scampering to be close
  * Self-esteem
    * Acceptance and love for who they are
    * Authentic engagement with the world
  * Self-development
    * Interest in patient's interests
    * Encouragement of connections
    * Enhancement of curiosity and learning

**Emotion Regulation**

* Decreased feelings of emotional discomfort, agitation, or distress
* Modulation of parent figures' behavior: calm, affectionate, warm, emotionally soothing

**Experience of Safety**

* Feeling safe and secure
* Trust and deep relaxation
* Calmness instead of worry
* Ability to bring up difficult topics without fear of abandonment
