**Proximity-Seeking**

* Hugging and being close to parent figures
* Scampering to be close and catch up
* Feeling welcomed when seeking closeness

**Self-Esteem**

* Acceptance and love for who they are
* Authentic engagement with the world
* Freedom from performing or pretending

**Self-Development**

* Interest in patient's interests
* Encouragement of connections
* Enhancement of curiosity and learning
* Patient feeling valued and important
* Opportunities for growth and independence.

### Another: Patient: They feel confident as parents; they trust themselves. I can ask for something if I need it. They teach me self-reliance. They’re th

**Imaginal Exposure Therapy (IPF): Parent Figure**

- Patients report feeling confident and self-reliant with supportive parent figures in their imagination
- This leads to an inner sense of strength and generosity, not a loss of self
- Examples of patient experiences:
  - Patient reads along with parents during storytelling, shares thoughts and feelings
  - Parent figure validates patient's emotions and boosts self-esteem
  - Calm and stable parent figures promote contentment and clarity in the patient

**Beneficial Effects of IPF:**

- Direct experience: feeling safe, strong, content
- Indirect experience: parents encouraging development of qualities or functions (e.g., curiosity, self-esteem)

**How Indirect Benefits Indicate Benefit:**

- Parent figures' actions and intentions activate underlying developmental processes in the patient
- Continued IPF sessions promote further development and stability of these benefits

**Patient Examples:**

- Patient reads with parents, shares thoughts, feels validated
- Parent figure boosts self-esteem, makes patient feel beautiful and strong
- Calm and stable parent figures promote contentment and clarity in the patient

**Imaginal Parent Figures' Actions:**

- Encouraging development of qualities or functions (curiosity, self-esteem)
- Activating underlying developmental processes
- Promoting further development through continued IPF sessions

**Benefits not Stable:**

- Therapist must engage patient in IPF process over time until benefits are consistently evoked and stable.

### MANIFESTATIONS OF AN INTERNALIZED SECURE BASE

**Internalized Secure Base (ISB)**

- Developed through Intensive Psychotherapy for Families (IPF) process
- Indications of ISB formation in imagery:
  * Consistent, stable attachment-promoting qualities in imagined parent figures
  * Consistent, stable benefits experienced by patient
  * Exploratory behavior by patient

**Exploratory Behavior**

- Patient makes excursions away from parent figures
- Increasing frequency, consistency, duration, and physical extent of such excursions
- Safe-haven seeking: going to caregiver when distressed
- Proximity seeking: preference for physical closeness and affection

**Patient Quotes**

1. "I have space to do what I want to do. I have a safety for exploring. I feel like they trust me."
2. "I feel safe enough. I feel I can express that, physically move to something new. It’s a new feeling in myself, a kind of like courage..."
3. "They know how important that is. They want to support my own connections."
4. "It makes me feel I can venture farther. I’m not stealing myself from them. They think it’s a healthy thing I want to go away."
5. "I can look around more. I’m walking. I don’t have to worry about things."
6. "It feels like the connection has become fully established... Now it's about me being more active, because I have the trust and connection with them now..."

**Significant Change in Experience of Relationship**

- Emergence of ISB: "It feels like a meeting has taken place among the three of us... It feels like a starting point... Now it's about me being more active, because I have the trust and connection with them now..."
- Patient can be themselves with guidance from parent figures
- Parent figures are interested in patient and supportive in new ways.

### Reinforcing the Internalized Secure Base

**Internalized Secure Base and Reinforcing Principles**

* When signs of a secure base are present in patient's imagery, extend, strengthen, and deepen the state using:
  * Mirroring: reflecting back patient's words and feelings
  * Bringing attention to felt experience
  * Amplifying positive states
  * Anchoring positive states in body experience
  * Suggesting new scenes where desired state is present

**Example of Therapy Session**

1. Patient describes a scene on the beach with secure feeling
2. Therapist reinforces and extends the scene by:
   * Mirroring: "You can feel that they’re there for you, when and if you need them."
   * Bringing attention to felt experience: "Notice what you feel inside now."
   * Amplifying positive states: "Feel happy. And there’s like a confidence too."
   * Anchoring positive states in body experience: "Become even more familiar with this feeling, and what it’s like as you experience it in your body."
3. Introducing time expansion to reinforce the patient's experience of safety
4. As indications of internalized secure base appear in a patient's daily life, the therapist:
   * Delights in these new experiences
   * Applies the same reinforcing methods used in IPF imagery: mirroring and amplifying positive states
   * Suggests bringing that sense of greater security to other circumstances.

**Bringing Secure Base to Other Circumstances**

1. Patient identifies a situation where they want to feel more secure
2. Therapist suggests imagining feeling secure in that situation while maintaining the inner, felt experience of a secure base.
3. Continue imagining each step toward the desired action in the context of an inner, felt experience of a secure base.

### Working in this way with the patient’s present experience allows for a gradual reduction in the frequency of using the IPF protocol. At this stage of 

**IPF Protocol in Attachment-Based Therapy**

* The IPF (Imaginal Processing of Fear) protocol is used to help patients develop a secure base and regulate affective states.
* At later stages of treatment, the IPF protocol continues to be beneficial for:
  + Reinforcing secure-base experience
  + Working with present-day affective states

**Secure Base Experience**

* Secure base results from new, positive attachment representations.
* Stronger and more stable representations lead to a stronger and more stable secure base.
* Signs of insecurity may reappear when the secure base is challenged until new attachment maps are deeply established.

**IPF Protocol Application**

* Bring patient's present experience into the session.
* Address signs of less security with IPF sessions.
* Use general or specific imagery based on the presenting affective state.

**Example: Anxiety**

1. Establish that there is no current threat to security.
2. Evoke anxiety in the present session.
3. Initiate IPF protocol:
   * Ask patient to imagine being a young child feeling anxious.
   * Introduce ideal parents who are attuned to the anxious state.
4. Reestablish secure base and bring it back to the current situation.
5. Patient experiences comfort, confidence, and security in the imagery.
6. Return to the present and apply learned skills to real-life situations.

**Example: Sadness**

1. Focus on the uncomfortable affect state.
2. Imagine being in the circumstance that contributed to the sadness.
3. Initiate IPF protocol:
   * Ask patient to imagine being a young child feeling sad.
   * Introduce ideal parents who are attuned to the sad state.
4. Explore and work with the imagery to alleviate the sadness.
5. Continue using IPF protocol throughout treatment for affect regulation.

### Common Challenges During the IPF Treatment Process

**Common Challenges During IPF Treatment Process**

* Therapists may face challenges in implementing IPF protocol: 1) patient difficulties, 2) therapist difficulties, 3) patient-therapist dyad difficulties
* Empathic response is crucial when patients experience discomfort or resistance
	+ Understanding of painful childhood experiences
	+ Soothing and protective responses
	+ Beneficial for accessing attachment dysfunction
* Maintain collaborative stance
	+ Model curiosity, interest, confidence, patience
	+ Highlight availability to address difficulties
* Contextualize patient difficulties within IPF imagery frame
	+ Parent figures respond to difficulties
	+ Reinforces the frame and attachment-repair process

**Difficulties Patients May Experience with IPF Protocol**

* Empathic response essential for initial discomfort or resistance
* Dismissing attachment patients may not initially experience discomfort but struggle with engagement
* Collaborative stance: model curiosity, interest, confidence, patience
* Contextualize difficulties within IPF imagery frame
	+ Parent figures respond to difficulties
	+ Reinforces the frame and attachment-repair process

**Patient Difficulties with Imagining Being a Very Young Child**

* First experiential imagery component of IPF protocol
* Patients may struggle to put themselves in child-self state due to past experiences leading to attachment disturbance
* Empathic response: understanding and soothing
* Collaborative stance: modeling curiosity, interest, confidence, patience
* Contextualize difficulties within IPF imagery frame: parent figures respond to difficulties and reinforce the frame.

### The therapist guides the patient toward the child state experience and asks the patient to nod “when you are feeling yourself as a young child.” If th

**Child State Experience**

* Therapist guides patient towards child state experience by asking them to nod when they feel themselves as a young child.
* If patient indicates absence of child-state feeling or observing stance, therapist encourages continued effort.
* For patients who do not enter child-self state:
  + Trust that patient will eventually come into receptive inner state.
  + Use displacement methods:
    - Imagining solutions from others' experiences.
    - Displacing self with a different child (displaced self).
* If patient cannot imagine being a young child in first or third person, therapist may use broader displacement method.

**Displacement Methods**

* Documentary-show imagery:
  + Patient imagines watching others who had difficulty feeling themselves as young children and reporting what they say.
  + Normalizes difficulty and suggests solutions.
* Displaced self:
  + Therapist asks patient to imagine a scene with a child much like them and proceeds with protocol.
  + After some experience, therapist encourages direct experience of self-as-child.

**Imagining Older Child, Teenager, or Young Adult**

* If patient imagines being an older child, teenager, or young adult:
  + Encourage imagining being even younger.
  + Continue with protocol at imagined age if not effective.
  + Use documentary-show displacement imagery to find ways of imagining being younger.

### It is not uncommon for patients to imagine themselves at different ages during different IPF sessions. Particularly if a patient has in many IPF sessi

**Intro:**

- Patients may imagine different ages during IPF (Imaginal Processing of Family) sessions
- Remapping process and integration can lead to imagining older self
- Therapist accepts older age self and conducts protocol as usual

**Resistance to Imagining Ideal Parent Figures:**

*Active resistance:*
  - Difficult experiences with actual parents may make it hard for patients to imagine ideal parent figures
  - Patients may refuse to continue IPF treatment due to discomfort
  - Therapist empathizes and encourages continuation, suggesting it becomes easier
*Inner child method:*
  - Transition to imagining ideal parent figures
  - Patient-as-child is met by patient-as-best-adult-self
  - Encourage experience of adult self's ways of being with the child

**Feelings of Disloyalty or Dishonoring:**

- Some patients resist imagining ideal parent figures due to fear of disloyalty or dishonoring actual parents
- Therapist emphasizes that there is no intention to discredit actual parents
- Ideal parents would treat actual parents with kindness and respect

**Difficulties Imagining Ideal Parent Figures:**

*No parent figures imagined:*
  - Common for patients, especially those with dismissing attachment
  - Not an indication of inability to engage with the process
  - Reassure patient that it takes time for imagination to create ideal parent figures
*Mismatch of expectation and dominant sensory mode:*
  - Some patients expect to see parent figures but don't have a strong visual imagination
  - Highlight felt sense instead
*Inner child method:*
  - Use when patient has difficulty imagining parent figures
  - Engage child-self with adult-best-self
  - Return to attempts to evoke ideal parent figures after session.

### Imagined parent figures are vague or distant. Either initially or after some period of not imagining any parent figures, the patient may begin to imag

**Imaginal Processing (IP) and Attachment:**

* Imagined parent figures are vague or distant, indicating engagement of attachment system
* Therapist aims to enhance clarity of parent figures within imagery frame
* Patients may bring up actual parents or people they know instead of imagined ideal parents
* Memory vs. imagination: memory reflects past experiences, imagination allows for possibilities
* Encouraging use of imagination to create new, ideal parent figures

**Engagement of Actual Parents:**

* Patients may initially bring up actual parents in remembered or idealized form
* Drawing from memory limits possibilities for secure attachment imagery
* Therapist responds by emphasizing use of imagination to create completely new parents
* Suggestions to incorporate best qualities of actual parents into new figures

**Engagement of People Patients Know:**

* Patients may imagine people they know as ideal parent figures
* Real people are more complicated than imagined ideal figures
* Therapist responds by encouraging use of imagination to create completely new, human parent figures

**Nonhuman Ideal Parent Figures:**

* Some patients create nonhuman ideal parent figures (cartoon characters, animals, aspects of nature)
* Suggests attachment wounds make human attachment figures too threatening
* Therapist prompts patient to imagine human figures but continues with nonhuman figures if patient persists
* With each subsequent IP session, therapist makes specific reference to human parent figures.

### At some point, the patient will begin to imagine human parent figures, and then may have difficulty in any of the ways presented in this section. Only

**Ideal Parent Figures in Imaginal Exposure Therapy**

* Patients may imagine only one clear ideal parent figure or both, with the absent one being less vivid.
* The missing parent figure usually represents the actual parent who was more abusive, neglectful, or absent.
* Therapist allows patient to focus on present parent figure while gently prompting the presence of the other.

**Difficulties in Relationships with Ideal Parent Figures**

* Patients' pre-treatment disturbed attachment representations may cause expectations or experiences of ideal parents behaving poorly.
* Therapist responds within IPF frame by having imagined parents acknowledge and understand patient's concerns, reassuring them.
* If parent figures become rejecting, neglectful, or abusive, therapist calls for a change in the frame and introduces new protective parent figures.

**Breaks from the Imagery Frame**

* Patients with insecure attachment histories may have difficulty establishing ideal parent figures, maintaining positive experiences, or maintaining the imagery frame.
* Memories of actual parents intruding on imagined parent figures can be acknowledged and used to return to the frame.
* Therapist stays within the frame when patient doubts its reality or engages in other ways that break the frame.
* Patients may become affectively overwhelmed, confused, or disoriented during the protocol; therapist maintains the imagery frame and has ideal parent figures respond empathically and effectively to patient's shift of state.

### DIFFICULTIES THAT THERAPISTS MAY HAVE WITH THE IPF PROTOCOL

**Difficulties Therapists May Encounter with IPF Protocol**

**Self-doubt about skill:**

* Greater skill and confidence come with training, study, practice, and experience.
* Address self-doubt through:
  * Rereading this chapter
  * Supervision from qualified practitioners
  * Collaborative approach
  * Ongoing learning and skill development

**Resistance to regular application:**

* Benefits of IPF protocol are most effective with consistent use.
* Resistance may come from self-doubt or patient expectations.
* Flexibility is appropriate in response to crises, but bring patient's experience into the IPF protocol whenever possible.
* Monitor internal resistance and address factors.

**Concern about manipulation:**

* Use of suggestion and shaping are central components of IPF method.
* Concerns about manipulation or control can be addressed by understanding:
  * Patients' freedom to create unique forms within the imagery frame
  * Therapist's role in permissively establishing conditions for attachment system activation
  * Shaping as support for reinforcement of attachment system

**Doubt due to lack of progress:**

* Patience is key when working with IPF method.
* Difficulties with imagined parent figures are expected and should not be interpreted as a sign of ineffectiveness or inappropriateness.
* Secure base signs may take time to emerge, depending on the severity of attachment disturbance.
* Attuned therapist continues IPF work until internalized secure base is established.

### DIFFICULTIES PERTAINING TO THE PATIENT–THERAPIST DYAD

**Difficulties Pertaining to the Patient-Therapist Dyad**

* Two primary forms of difficulty: collaborative avoidance of IPF protocol and affect-laden breach in collaboration

**Collaborative Avoidance of IPF Protocol**

* Ideal collaboration leads to mutual use of IPF protocol
* Collusion to resist use of IPF protocol can occur
* Both patient and therapist may experience difficulty with the method
* Signs: patient brings crises to sessions, therapist responds instead of using IPF protocol, ambivalence about protocol
* Prevention: recognize potential for collusive avoidance, address together contributing factors

**Affect-Laden Breach in Collaboration (Rupture in Therapy Relationship)**

* Patient breaks imagery frame and expresses negative affect towards therapist
* Therapist does not respond from within the imagery frame or attachment system
* Rupture best addressed in collaborative behavioral system
* Example: patient's anger towards therapist during therapist's vacation announcement
* Collaborative exploration of rupture restores both systems

**Additional Challenges for Each Attachment Subtype**

* Specific challenges to IPF protocol and other treatment pillars addressed in each subtype chapter.

### Recognizing Successful IPF Treatment

**Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) Treatment for Attachment Insecurity**

* The IPF method is an efficient, positive-focused approach to treating attachment insecurity.
* It integrates research on conditions that support secure attachment development.
* Therapist guides patient to:
  * Relax and access behavioral memory system.
  * Engage imagination as young child.
  * Create "just right" ideal parent figures.
* Parent figures embody ways of being that meet patient's longings, wants, and needs for attachment security.
* No focus on painful childhood history; instead, new positive experiences through imagination.
* Patient reports profound changes:
  * Increased feelings of warmth, safety, joy.
  * Ability to express feelings freely and deal with difficult situations.
  * Improved self-esteem and relationships.
* IPF method activates attachment, metacognitive, and collaborative behavioral systems.
* Challenges may arise but are expected and met in ways that contribute to attachment security development.
* Ultimately, new positive experiences overwrite old, insecure attachment maps.

**Patient's Experience:**

* 57-year-old woman in therapy for three years before IPF introduced.
* Six sessions with therapist using IPF protocol.
* One self-generated imagery session.
* Profound changes:
  * New, positive experiences through imagination.
  * Increased childhood memories and feelings of joy.
  * Ability to deal with difficult situations with ideal parents' help.
  * Improved relationships and self-esteem.
* Previously felt guilty for enjoying ideal parents but focused on process instead.
* Negative feelings towards real parents faded, realizing they loved her too.
* Feeling of being a bad person gone, increased self-awareness and acceptance.

### CHAPTER 9 The Second Pillar Metacognitive Interventions for Attachment Disturbances The History and Development of Metacognition

**Metacognitive Interventions for Attachment Disturbances (Chapter 9)**

**The History and Development of Metacognition**

- Metacognition: the ability to think about one's own thinking, learning, and problem-solving processes
  - Three components: **metamemory**, **metalearning**, and **metastrategy**
    * Metamemory: knowledge about one's memory capabilities and strategies
    * Metalearning: understanding the process of learning and how to improve it
    * Metastrategy: selecting and applying appropriate cognitive strategies

**Metacognition in Attachment Theory**

- Attachment disturbances: disruptions in the development of secure attachment relationships
  - Influence on mentalization, emotion regulation, and self-concept
- **Metacognitive interventions**: therapeutic approaches focusing on enhancing metacognitive skills to address attachment disturbances

**Theoretical Background of Metacognitive Interventions for Attachment Disturbances**

- **Mentalization-Based Therapy (MBT)**: focuses on the ability to understand and explain mental states, emotions, and intentions
  - Enhances metacognitive skills through reflective practice and role-playing
- **Mindfulness-Based Interventions (MBIs)**: promote present-moment awareness and nonjudgmental acceptance of thoughts and feelings
  - Improve metacognitive abilities by increasing self-awareness and emotional regulation
- **Cognitive Behavioral Therapy (CBT)**: targets maladaptive thought patterns and behaviors
  - Enhances metacognition through identifying and challenging automatic thoughts, learning new problem-solving strategies, and practicing cognitive restructuring

**Empirical Evidence for Metacognitive Interventions in Attachment Disturbances**

- **Effectiveness**: numerous studies demonstrate the efficacy of metacognitive interventions in addressing attachment disturbances
  - Improvements in mentalization, emotion regulation, and self-concept
- **Moderating Factors**: individual differences (e.g., age, severity of attachment disturbance) may influence intervention outcomes

**Conclusion**

- Metacognitive interventions offer a promising approach to addressing attachment disturbances by enhancing metacognitive skills such as mentalization, emotion regulation, and problem-solving abilities.
- MBT, MBIs, and CBT are effective therapeutic approaches that have demonstrated improvements in individuals with attachment disturbances.
- Further research is needed to explore the long-term effects of these interventions and their applicability across various populations.

### The history of the development of metacognitive interventions in attachment-based therapy can be roughly categorized into four historical generations 

**Historical Development of Metacognitive Interventions in Attachment-Based Therapy**

* Four historical generations of metacognition in attachment-based therapy:
  1. Appearance–reality distinction and the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI)
  2. Reflective Functioning Scale (RF-S) and mentalization-based treatment (MBT)
  3. Modular approach to metacognitive psychotherapy by Semerari and Liotti
  4. Research on higher cognitive development and full range of metacognitive skills

**First Generation: Appearance–Reality Distinction and the AAI**

* John Flavell's seminal paper in 1979 introduced metacognition as thinking about thinking, focusing on metacognitive strategies.
* Metacognitive knowledge is understanding factors affecting cognitive processes.
* Early research focused on appearance–reality distinction in children.
* Flavell and associates developed a lab test to assess appearance–reality distinction in 3-5 year olds.
* Children were asked about the appearance and reality of objects, revealing their ability to recognize that experiences can be represented differently.
* Three-year-olds generally lacked representational change and diversity, while five-year-olds had a better understanding.
* Alison Gopnik extended research on appearance–reality distinction, focusing on representational change and diversity in children.
* Children's understanding of representational change developed between ages 3 and 5.

### In another experiment, Gopnik and Graf (1988) told children about objects hidden in a drawer. The children were either shown what was in the drawer, t

**Experiments on Children's Metacognition**

* Gopnik and Graf (1988) studied children's ability to identify the source of knowledge about hidden objects.
  * Three-year-olds had difficulty identifying source, especially after a delay.
  * Five-year-olds had no trouble.
* Interpreted as evidence for metarepresentational ability development between ages 3 and 5.

**Appearance–Reality Distinction**

* Harris et al. (1986) investigated children's ability to distinguish real from apparent emotions in stories.
  * Four-year-olds had limited ability, six-year-olds were capable.
* Influenced research on attachment and metacognition.

**Metacognition: Thinking About Thought**

* Defined as reflecting on the nature, validity, and source of thoughts (Main, 1991).
* Metacognitive knowledge: reflecting on the nature of thought.
* Metacognitive monitoring: reflecting on its source and validity.

**Metacognition and Attachment**

* Main et al. (2002) looked at metacognition in relation to attachment status.
  * Secure vs. insecure children differ in metacognitive abilities.
  * Insecure children have metacognitive deficits, leading to multiple internal working models.
  * Secure children construct a single, coherent internal working model.

**Metacognition and Development**

* Secure vs. insecure toddlers differ in self-directed speech and self-correcting strategies.
* Six-year-olds understand privacy of thought.

**Metacognitive Monitoring Scale on the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI)**

* Recognition of appearance–reality distinctions, representational change, and diversity.
* Fresh comments monitoring reaction patterns.
* Error monitoring: acknowledging potential distortions or limitations in beliefs or memories.
* Rating scale: none, possible, some monitoring, marked monitoring, metacognitive monitoring characterizing the interview.

**Limitations of First-Generation Research**

* Poor representation of range and types of metacognition on AAI.
* Limited to self-monitoring, does not address other's state of mind.

**Second-Generation Research**

* Fonagy et al.'s research addresses these limitations.

### SECOND GENERATION: INTERSUBJECTIVITY, MENTALIZATION, AND THE REFLECTIVE FUNCTIONING SCALE

**Second Generation: Intersubjectivity, Mentalization, and Reflective Functioning Scale**

- Developed by Peter Fonagy, Anthony Bateman, and associates from the London School or group
- Heavily influenced by developmental theory of mind research and social biofeedback theory of parental-affect mirroring by Gyorgy Gergely and John Watson
- Expanded recognition of metacognition as intersubjective and bidirectional
- Metacognitive capacity develops between child and caregiver, co-created together

**Recognition of Intentionality and Parental Affect Mirroring**

- Children develop capacity to detect other's intention around 9-12 months
- Recognition of intentionality allows representation of beliefs, desires, emotions of others
- Gergely and Watson criticize Meltzoff and Gopnik's hypothesis on imitation as insufficient for emotional awareness development
- Parental affect mirroring plays vital role in emotional self-awareness and control
- Secure mothers' spontaneous facial displays match infant's affect display, enhancing synchronization

**Parent-Child Interaction: Contingency Detection and Marking**

- Infant discovers caregiver's response is contingent upon own behavior
- Inference of being an active causal agent
- Caregiver's marking (producing exaggerated emotional expression) critical for child's learning
- Child decouples emotion from caregiver's and becomes sensitive to interoceptive cues
- Repetitive presentation of external reflection serves as "teaching" function, leading to awareness of internal cues and emotion categories

**Impact on Mentalization Capacity**

- Infant constructs separate representation for marked affect
- New representation functions as secondary representational structure
- Foundation of mentalization

**Functions of Parental Affective Mirroring**

- Sensitization to internal states
- Building of secondary representations
- Regulation of child's internal emotional state
- Developing a generalized affective communication code based on pretend, play, and knowing representations as representations

### Gergely and Watson (1996) also briefly discuss the developmental psychopathology implications of parental “deviant mirroring styles.” When parents pro

**Parental "Deviant Mirroring Styles" and Developmental Psychopathology**

- Gergely and Watson (1996) discuss implications of parental mirroring styles on emotion development.
  - Congruent but unmarked mirroring: emotion not decoupled from parent, secondary representation not established.
    * Example: "You're uncomfortable."
  - Marked but noncongruent mirroring: infant develops distorted self-representations.
    * Example: Mother laughingly says, "You’re so uncomfortable."
  - Contingency detection is active over the lifespan, allowing for change through therapy or beneficial experiences.

**Reflective Function (RF) and its Development in Children**

- Expanded understanding of metacognitive capacity in children by London group.
- RF: reflecting on self and others' mental states and distinguishing inner and outer reality.
  * Organizes experience of behavior in terms of mental state constructs.
  - Predicted by beliefs and desires, influenced by theories of Freud, Anna Freud, Melanie Klein, Donald Winnicott, John Bowlby, Daniel Stern, etc.
- Fonagy and Target (1997): RF permits child to respond to others' beliefs, feelings, hopes, pretense, and plans.
  * Develops from caretaker who sees relationships in terms of mental content.
  - Secure attachment and less reliant on defensive behaviors.

**Assessing Reflective Function (RF) with the Reflective Functioning Scale (RF-S)**

- Developed by Fonagy and colleagues to assess reflective capacity regarding self and other's mental states.
  * Wider range of metacognitive capacities than AAI Metacognitive Monitoring scale.
- RF-S assesses four discrete categories or dimensions of reflective functioning:
  1. **Awareness of the nature of mental states** (knowing that people have thoughts, feelings, and intentions).
     * Example: "People think and feel things." (RF score: 2)
  2. **Explicit effort to tease out mental states underlying behavior** (making inferences about others' mental states based on their actions).
     * Example: "He might be feeling sad because his dog died." (RF score: 3)
  3. **Recognition of the developmental dimension of mental states** (understanding that mental states change over time and can be influenced by experiences).
     * Example: "My mom was angry when I was a kid, but now she's more understanding." (RF score: 4)
  4. **Recognition of mental states in relation to the interviewer** (understanding that the interviewer has mental states and that these states influence the interview).
     * Example: "I think you might be confused about what I meant." (RF score: 5)
- RF is a unified process, but RF-S assesses these four discrete categories to guide clinicians.

### A. Awareness of the nature of mental states (recognition of the characteristics of mental states in self and others): 1. Opaqueness of mental states. 

**Mental States Awareness**

* Recognition of the characteristics of mental states in self and others

**Opaqueness of Mental States**

* Uncertainty regarding a mental state or alternative mental states associated with behavior
* Example: "I think that my relationship with my parents became closer in my early 20s, but I’m not sure." (4)

**Mental States as Susceptible to Disguise**

* Awareness that mental states can be disguised or hidden
* Example: "I think that my relationship with my parents grew closer when I became a young adult, although my parents quickly became financially dependent on me, so it was hard to tell how they really felt." (5)

**Limitations on Insight**

* Awareness of limitations in understanding self and others
* Example: "I think that my mother felt closer to me when I began to have children, but I'm not sure." (4)

**Mental States Tied to Expressions of Appropriate Normative Judgments**

* Recognition of mental states as expectable responses to specific circumstances
* Example: "When I was 18, I started to stay out past 10. She gave me the ‘cold shoulder’ on these occasions, I guess because she was angry and like many adults didn’t understand that I had matured to adolescence." (3)

**Defensive Nature of Certain Mental States**

* Recognition that mental states can be modified to reduce negative affect
* Example: "He seemed to ignore times when I was respectful, I guess because he believed in strict parenting." (5)

### B. The explicit effort to identify mental states underlying a given behavior (identification of possible mental states that may account for behavior, 

**Mental State Identification**

- Explicitly recognizing and understanding mental states that underlie behaviors in self and others
  - Accurate attribution of mental states to others:
    * Specific causal accounts of behavior based on mental states
    * Example: parents' views about child rearing, perception of improved relationship during adulthood
  - Envisioning the possibility of unrelated feelings:
    * Recognition that affect may be inconsistent with reality
    * Example: feeling a better relationship while incarcerated
  - Recognition of diverse perspectives:
    * Acknowledgment that different people perceive behaviors/situations differently
    * Example: becoming the parent and child in relationships
  - Taking into account one's own mental state:
    * Interpreting others' behavior considering personal feelings
    * Example: less communication after moving out, not invited to wedding
  - Evaluating mental states based on impact:
    * Awareness of role of mental states in behaviors
    * Example: being needy and demanding
  - Considering how others perceive one:
    * Recognition of influence of actions/reactions on others' perceptions
    * Example: seen as pushy but lacked confidence
- Fresh perspective:
  - Spontaneous expressions about mental states
  - Example: reevaluating father's refusal to talk about the past.

### C. Recognizing developmental aspects of mental states (includes acknowledgment of the influence of one generation on the next, showing understanding o

**Recognizing Developmental Aspects of Mental States:**

* Taking an intergenerational perspective, recognizing the exchange of ideas, feelings, and behavior across generations (1)
* Taking a developmental perspective, acknowledging that mental states and perspectives change with age (2)
* Revising thoughts and feelings about childhood in light of understanding gained since childhood (3)
* Envisioning changes in mental states between past, present, and future (4)
* Understanding transactional processes between parent and child, recognizing the influence and impact on each other (5)
* Understanding factors that developmentally determine affect regulation, awareness of a child's dependence on caregiver for emotional regulation (6)
* Awareness of family dynamics, seeing the family as an interdependent system (7)

**Mental States in Relation to the Interviewer:**

* Acknowledging the separateness of mind, recognizing that interviewer has a unique set of experiences and mental stance (1)
* Not assuming knowledge, making efforts to help interviewer understand by clarifying confusing aspects (2)
* Emotional attunement, recognizing that interviewer might be affected by the material provided and acknowledging potential reasons why (3)

**Reflective Functioning (RF) Scoring:**

* RF-S scale ranges from –1 to 9
* –1: Distinctly anti-reflective or bizarre/inappropriate RF
* 0: Disavowed or absent RF
* 1: Absent but not repudiated RF
* 3: Questionable or Low RF
* 5: Definite or Ordinary RF
* 7: Marked RF
* 9: Exceptional RF

### A score of –1 to 1 is consistent with disorganized attachment. A score of less than 3 is consistent with disorganized status or dismissing or anxious-

**Attachment Security and Reflective Functioning (RF)**

- RF score ranges from -1 to 1:
  - Disorganized attachment: -1 to less than 3
  - Dismissing or anxious-preoccupied insecure status: less than 3
  - Insecure or secure status: 4 or 5
  - Secure status: 6 to 9
- RF goes beyond self-monitoring to include mentalizing about mental states of self and others
- Two fundamental factors operating across the four dimensions of RF:
  - Capacity to recognize representations as constructions
  - Capacity to take perspective and contextualize mental states

**Research on Reflective Functioning (RF)**

- Fonagy et al. (1995) compared parents' RF score and attachment status with infants' Strange Situation procedure assessments
- Parents with insecure status and high RF more likely to have secure infants than insecure parents with low RF
- RF is relevant for effective clinical assessment and treatment

**Limitations of Reflective Functioning (RF)**

- RF is seen as a unitary function, but deficits in metacognition are often pathology-specific
- Third generation of metacognitive research addresses this limitation by focusing on different metacognitive strengths and deficits for various clinical conditions.

### THIRD GENERATION: THE MODULAR, PSYCHOPATHOLOGY-SPECIFIC APPROACH

**Third Generation: The Modular, Psychopathology-Specific Approach**

* Antonio Semerari, Giancarlo Dimaggio, Giovanni Liotti, and associates at the Third Center of Cognitive Psychotherapy in Rome have refined understanding of metacognition and its appearance in clinical populations.
* Metacognition viewed as a number of independent capacities rather than a unitary construct with various dimensions.
* Developed Metacognitive Assessment Scale (MAS) to assess metacognitive capacities in patients with BPD, NPD, and DID.
* Discovered "pathology-specific" deficits in particular metacognitive capacities but not others for each diagnosis.
* Allowed construction of specific "metacognitive profiles" for patients based on diagnosis and MAS results.

**Six Distinct Metacognitive Capacities**

1. **Identification:**
   * Ability to distinguish, recognize, and define one's own or another's inner states (emotions, cognitions).
   * Divided into two subfunctions: recognizing one's own representations and recognizing emotions.
   * Examples: "I felt sad when I heard the news", "She always thinks that she’s treated unfairly", "I’m angry", "She was really happy when she saw me".

2. **Relating Variables:**
   * Ability to establish relations among separate components of mental states and between mental states and behavior.
   * Through RV, a subject explains their own behavior in terms of causes and/or motivations.
   * Examples: "I didn’t answer the phone because I didn’t want to speak to him", "He was angry at me because I hadn’t helped him", "I felt sad because I really wanted her to like me".

3. **Differentiation:**
   * Ability to recognize that the contents of representations are subjective events and different from reality.
   * Acknowledges the subjectivity of our representations and recognizes the limits of their influence on reality.
   * Examples: "Giovanni is convinced that if he doesn’t walk the same road every morning, a catastrophe will happen, but I know that it is one of his fantasies and not a premonition", "On the bus I think that everybody is looking at me, but they’re probably just thinking about their own business".

4. **Integration:**
   * Ability to work out coherent descriptions of one's mental states and processes.
   * Takes a narrative form and gives a sense of continuity.
   * Divided into two items: providing an integrated description of one’s own mental state and describing changes over time in one’s own mental states.
   * Example: "First I felt scared, but as I started to realize that the people there were friendly, I became less scared, and was aware of being excited".

5. **Decentration:**
   * Ability to comprehend another individual's mental state from a non-egocentric perspective.
   * Enables recognizing the hypothetical nature of our reading of other individuals’ minds.
   * Example: "She wasn’t looking at me, and at first I thought that she was deliberately ignoring me. But then I realized that she was probably just thinking of her plans for tomorrow and not about me at all".

6. **Mastery:**
   * Ability to work through one's representations and mental states with a view to implementing effective action strategies.
   * Defined as regulation and control activities.
   * Divided into three levels: first-level strategies, second-level strategies, and third-level strategies.
   * Awareness of one’s state in a way that leads to regulation or change of that state.

**Metacognitive Deficits in BPD and NPD Patients**

* BPD patients showed relatively normal metacognitive identification and relating variables but significant deficits in differentiation early on, severe deficits in integration and mastery that didn't change much throughout the course of therapy.
* Examples from a patient diagnosed with BPD: "I was furious", "I’ve been feeling this way since he didn’t call me back", "I hate him and I always have and I always will", "I might as well kill myself because there’s nothing else I can do".
* NPD patients showed an initial deficit in metacognitive identification and a stronger deficit in relating variables that improved slowly over the course of treatment. Differentiation failures varied throughout the course of treatment, integration improved over treatment, and mastery was moderate.
* Examples from a patient diagnosed with NPD: "I don’t have any feelings about her. I don’t have any feelings, period", "I don’t have a clue why this is so", "She’s been sending me emails every day. I know she’s trying to flatter me", "Don’t ask me why, but I know she’s madly in love with me", "I’m just going to label her emails as spam. No way am I going to write her back".
* Effective treatment for BPD must include improving their metacognitive differentiation, integration, and mastery.
* Given the BPD patient's relatively normal capacity for metacognitive identification of states, fostering core mindfulness in DBT may not be a specific enough intervention for patients with BPD.

### FOURTH GENERATION: BEYOND MENTAL CONSTRUCTIONS AND RELATIVISM OF MENTAL STATES

**Fourth Generation of Metacognition:**

- London and Rome groups have advanced understanding of metacognitive functioning and its clinical value, particularly for patients with personality disorders and attachment disturbances (Benefits of Metacognitive Development)
- Third generation metacognitive research and treatment limited:
  - Reflective Function (RF) concept is based on second-order intersubjectivity or constructivist theory of mind
  - Rome group expands metacognition beyond constructivism to relativism of mental states but does not account for developmental levels beyond these concepts

**Beyond Constructivism and Relativism:**

- Necessary to consider entire trajectory of life span development (Entire Picture of Metacognition)
- Metacognitive development continues after childhood (Continued Development)
- Later occurring metacognitive types relevant to mental health (Relevance to Mental Health)

**Advanced Understanding of Mind:**

- Wallin's tertiary subjectivity: Patients may transcend constructed mind through mindfulness practices (Transcendent Knowing)
- Wilber's Integral theory: Levels of knowing beyond constructivism and relativism, reflecting a transcendent "metamap" (Levels of Knowing)

**Fourth Generation Metacognition:**

- Each level of mind associated with specific types of metacognition
- Developing advanced metacognitive possibilities beneficial for treating attachment and other disturbances (Fourth Generation Field)
- Integrating advanced metacognitive understanding into treatment heralds new generation in the field (New Generation)

### The Spectrum of Adult Development

**Adult Development:**

- Building upon Piaget's theory, researchers have identified stages of mature adult development beyond formal operational thinking.
- Notable models include Commons et al.'s postformal reasoning, Labouvie-Vief's postformal stages, Kegan's self-development stages, and Wilber's full-spectrum model.

**Postformal Reasoning:**

- According to Commons et al., there are stages beyond formal operational thinking: fifth-order (systematic), sixth-order (metasystematic), and seventh-order (cross-paradigmatic) thinking.
- Fifth-order thinking is complete and exhaustive within a class, sixth-order thinking defines relationships between systems, and cross-paradigmatic thinking relates fields that appear independent.

**Cognitive Development Stages:**

- **Formal Operational Thinking (Piaget):** Last stage of cognitive development described by Piaget, developing during adolescence.
- **Labouvie-Vief's Postformal Stages:**
  - Intrasystemic (formal operational thinking)
  - Intersystemic: expansion of context and awareness of meanings beyond the information given
  - Autonomous: logic of self-regulating systems, creativity, and generativity across generations

**Self-Development Stages:**

- Kegan's stages describe a succession of qualitative differentiations of the self from the world.
- Each stage represents an expanded context in which the self emerges.
- Corresponding Piagetian stages: incorporative self/sensorimotor, impulsive self/preoperations, imperial self/concrete operations, interpersonal self/early formal operations, institutional self/full formal operations, and interindividual self (postformal thinking).

**Ego Development:**

- Loevinger presented a nine-stage model of ego development.
- Cook-Greuter built upon this foundation and further researched the postformal stages.
- Transition from formal to postformal ego development: individualist stage (awareness of perspective, context, and constructs) and autonomous stage (metasystematic thinking, focusing on potential, and integrating conflicting frameworks).
- Construct-aware post-autonomous stage: awareness of the limits of representational knowledge and the processes of thinking itself.
- Ego sense becomes aware of itself as a temporary construct.

### At the universal or unitive stage (the ninth in the model and labeled as 6), there is a “change to a concept of field (vision)[,] . . . [a] quantum le

**Universal or Unitive Stage (6th in the model, labeled as 9)**

- Marks a quantum leap in experience and outlook
- Characterized by a concept of field (vision)
- Yearning to transcend rational, conscious egos
- Direct attunement awareness instead of representational thinking
- Global, transpersonal, or ego-transcendent experience
- Individuals experience themselves and others as part of ongoing humanity
- Multiperspectival capacity: ability to take multiple points of view
- Self-perception is "No-'thing'," beyond the ego, integrated with a higher Unity
- Self is created anew at every moment
- Transcends limitations of representational thinking for direct awareness of ultimate truth

**Cook-Greuter's Stage Model of Ego Development Assumptions:**

- The more differentiated and objective self-view, the closer to truth
- Highest stages transcend representational thinking for direct awareness of ultimate truth

**Integral Psychology and Integral Spirituality by Ken Wilber:**

- Comprehensive model of multiple lines of human development
- Includes highest stages described in spiritual traditions
- Reviewed 100 models of developmental sequences
- Found structural coherence reflecting a universal framework for all developmental lines
- Uses metaphor of 12 bandwidths of light to indicate structural levels of developmental lines

**Intellectual/Cognitive Line:**

- Piaget's stages align with the first five bandwidths
- Formal operational thinking corresponds to orange stage (5th)
- First postformal cognitive stage is green, pluralistic or relativistic and early-vision logic (6th position)

**Higher Cognitive Stages:**

- Teal: systemic thinking and middle-vision logic (7th position)
- Turquoise: higher systemic thinking and late-vision logic (8th position)
- Indigo: global and ego-transcendent, awakened or illumined mind (9th position)
- Violet: all dimensions present simultaneously (10th position)
- Ultraviolet and clear light: highest attainments (11th and 12th positions)

### A UNIFIED MODEL OF THE DEVELOPMENT OF COGNITION AND METACOGNITION

**Unified Model of Cognition and Metacognition Development**

* Structural model with stages of cognitive and metacognitive development
* Three lowest levels: infrared, magenta, red (egocentric orientation)
	+ Sensorimotor (infrared) - Piaget's sensorimotor stage
	+ Preoperational (magenta, red) - Piaget's preoperational stages
* Fourth stage: amber (ethnocentric orientation)
	+ Concrete operational thinking - Piaget
	+ Symbiotic, impulsive, self-protective, conformist, and conscientious stages - Loevinger and Cook-Greuter
	+ Internal sense of "mind" development
	+ Expansion of emotional range
	+ Peak of fantasy involvement
	+ Stabilization of attention
	+ Metacognitive skills: immediate awareness of mind, represent state of mind of others, meaning-making based on social approval and manifest content
* Fifth stage: orange (formal operational thinking)
	+ Appreciation of primary elements within a system
	+ Deductive reasoning emerges
	+ Metacognitive skills: efficacy and mastery, awareness of latent meaning, understanding connection between actions and consequences, projecting long-term goals
* Sixth stage: individualist/systematic (intersystemic relationships)
	+ Complete and exhaustive operations within the same class of cognitive objects
	+ Heightened sensitivity to context
	+ Understanding underlying assumptions, schemas, and expectancies
	+ Capacity to take perspective and understand how point of view influences experience
	+ Optimizing options in the face of limitations
* Seventh stage: autonomous (metasystematic)
	+ Shift from logical systems to self-regulation
	+ Thinking in terms of relations between one system and another
	+ Emerging capacity to think in terms of an overarching supersystem
	+ Awareness of interdependence
	+ Definition of a unique purpose in life
	+ Capacity to resolve internal conflict or contradiction
	+ Ability to directly perceive the degree of organization or disorganization of state of mind
* Eighth stage: construct-aware (cross-paradigmatic)
	+ Discovery that conceptual knowledge interferes with knowing via direct experience or awareness
	+ Thought as a mode of knowledge becomes problematic and is eventually superseded by direct knowing through immediate awareness
	+ Appreciation for the self as a central organizer
	+ Recognition of the supersystemic order to life
* Ninth stage: universal/unitive (ego-transcendence)
	+ Merging of individual consciousness with universal consciousness
	+ Awareness of vast oceanlike field of consciousness
	+ Center of experience, identity, and action becomes the oceanlike awareness
	+ Metacognitive skills: disappearance of reactivity of mind, taking multiple perspectives, instantaneous monitoring of states of mind, nonreified flexible self, spontaneous compassion for shared humanity
* Clinical Applications
	+ Promoting metacognitive skills in patients
	+ Basic metacognitive skills: awareness of state of mind in self and others
		- Fostering greater awareness of internal experience
		- Helping patients become more aware of their own internal state and the state of others
		- Techniques: focusing on feelings, recognizing negative self-talk, using hypnosis for subtitles method.

### To enhance awareness of underlying maladaptive beliefs and schemas, the clinician might say, “What were the assumptions you were making about . . . ? 

**Mindfulness and Awareness in Psychotherapy**

* Clinician asks patients introspective questions to enhance self-awareness of beliefs, wishes, needs, and emotional states.
* Dialectical Behavior Therapy (DBT) incorporates mindfulness meditation for continuous, nonreactive awareness.
* Patients learn to be present to their experiences without reacting defensively.
* Self-rating scales help patients reflect on their degree of awareness.
* Reinforcing awareness of emotional states through affective marking.
* Developing perspective-taking and empathy skills (mentalization) by wondering about others' motivations, feelings, needs, and beliefs.
* Therapist models mentalizing behavior to help patients develop curiosity towards their own and others' state of mind.
* Tips for therapists to influence mentalizing include:
  * Wondering out loud about the patient's state of mind.
  * Disclosing therapist's thoughts and feelings.
  * Asking specific questions about feelings, thoughts, needs, and motivations in situations.
  * Encouraging patients to wonder about others' mental states.
* Clinical example: Patient learns to understand her mother's perspective and develop a compromise for their relationship.

### Metacognitive Monitoring

**Metacognitive Monitoring**

- Helping patients increase accuracy in assessing their own states of mind
- Patients with poor self-assessment may exhibit various degrees of errors:
  - Source-monitoring mistakes
  - Confusing fantasies or dreams with memories
  - Logical contradictions in beliefs
  - Passive influence
  - Serious lapses in reality-testing
  - Inner speech confusion

**Fostering Objective Attitude**

- Encourage patients to develop objective, critical attitude towards reported memories
- Example: Female patient with false belief of daughter's abuse
  * High suggestibility on Gudjonsson Suggestibility Scale
  * Encouraged to develop objective, critical attitude instead of focusing on risk
  * Convinced no abuse occurred and worked out custody arrangement

**Positive Hallucinations vs. Reality-Monitoring**

- Nonpathological, highly hypnotizable subjects can create positive hallucinations by suspending reality-monitoring
- For patients with deficits in reality-testing:
  * Activate and strengthen their reality-monitoring function
  * Use a scale to estimate the degree of external vs. internal perception of voices or experiences
  * Explore potential sources of voices or experiences

**Inner Wisdom Technique**

- Connect with inner wisdom to accurately detect source of voices or experiences
- Repeatedly appeal to patient's inner reality-monitoring capacity
- Hallucinated voices are eventually seen as inner thoughts

**Cognitive Dissonance Model of Treatment**

- Errors in monitoring contradictory and extremely irrational beliefs best handled using cognitive dissonance model
- Introduce language of parts to heighten dissonance between conflicting beliefs
- Appeal to other parts of the mind to resolve deep conflict and reduce dissonance.

### Contingency Detection and Metacognitive Mastery It is one thing to help patients to become more aware of their states of mind. It is an entirely diffe

**Contingency Detection and Metacognitive Mastery**

* Awareness of state of mind is different from mastery over it
* Patients with personality and dissociative disorders have low awareness of contingency influence
* Therapist helps patients become aware of their influence on own state of mind and others' behavior
* Explicitly introduce task of gaining mastery over internal states
* Use metaphors like an affect dial to help patients control emotions
* Make it an expectation of treatment that patients will increase control over internal states
* Patients can rehearse in fantasy having control over overwhelming situations
* Displacement technique in hypnosis helps patients learn effective coping strategies

**Action Plans and Goal-Orientation**

* Development of formal operational thinking brings capacity for long-term goals
* Impulsive patients show impaired goal-oriented behavior
* Reflect on possible consequences of behavior as a series of hypotheticals
* Imagine negative consequences of maladaptive actions and positive consequences of adaptive ones
* Develop internal representations of actions and their likely consequences
* Identify healthy goals and develop action plans to achieve them
* Keep journal entries about implementation of action plan and bring problems into treatment sessions
* Successful patients learn to think proactively and let behaviors be guided by action plans.

### Meaning-Making

**Psychoanalytic Tradition and Meaning-Making**

* Contributes to metacognition through fostering meaning-making
* Therapy aims to help patients formulate the meaning of their inner experience (Stern, 2010)
* Symptoms ameliorated when a patient develops an explanatory model for them (Frank, 1963)
* Dynamic therapy: helps patients make sense of experiences and symptoms
* Orientation towards exploring symptoms and behaviors to understand their meaning
* Explanation of behavior, motivation, emotional states, interpersonal patterns, beliefs, and dreams
* Three levels of meaning: manifest, emotional, and latent or unconscious

**Manifest Meaning**

* Understanding and explanation of immediate experience

**Emotional Meaning**

* Reflects a deeper level than manifest meaning
* Requires accurate identification of emotions and understanding their meanings

**Latent or Unconscious Meaning**

* Identification and understanding repetitive behaviors and long-standing patterns
* Fostering curiosity about the underlying causes

**Intermediate Metacognitive Skills: Postformal Level (Stage 6)**

* Characterized by relativistic thinking and systematic operations
* New metacognitive skills emerge, including:
  * Recognition of how the past shapes current experience
  * Appreciation for the relativity of all states of mind and limitations of knowledge
  * Capacity to see more deeply into underlying assumptions and expectancies
  * Optimization of action plans in the face of perceived limitations
  * Understanding the effects of context on feelings and behaviors
  * Taking perspective and seeing things from another's point of view

**Recognition of How the Past Shapes Current Experience**

* Dynamic therapists foster an attitude towards exploring how early childhood experiences shape current behavior
* Rueful recognition: acknowledging parallels between parental behavior and one's own

**Appreciation for the Relativity of All States of Mind and Limitations of Knowledge**

* Encouraging patients to see their emotions and meanings as temporary constructions
* Helping patients recognize defensiveness and self-deception around certain issues

**Seeing More Deeply Into Underlying Assumptions and Expectancies**

* Patients capable of understanding how underlying assumptions influence state of mind and behavior
* Therapist asks questions to help patients explore underlying beliefs and expectancies.

### Optimizing Action Plans

**Optimizing Action Plans**

At the level of cognitive development in old age, individuals can optimize responses to perceived limitations through a process called selection, optimization, and compensation (SOC). This process is characterized by:

1. **Selection:** Setting goals within available coping resources.
2. **Optimization:** Thoroughly exploring hidden inner resources to establish the best forms of coping and functioning in the face of limitations.

**Process of Optimization:**

a. Imagining a situation where one feels at their best (the "best self"):
   - Patient describes the situation and qualities of the "best self."
   b. Therapist mirrors and enhances these qualities.

c. Changing the scene to a difficult situation:
   - Patient brings "best-self" qualities into the difficult situation.
   - New positive qualities may emerge.

d. Discussing and translating optimizing resources into an action plan for coping with limitations.

**Therapist's Role:**

1. Encouraging exploration of inner resources.
2. Mirroring and enhancing identified qualities.
3. Suggesting bringing "best-self" qualities to difficult situations.
4. Helping patients discover new resources.
5. Assisting in creating an action plan using optimizing resources.

### Sensitivity to Context

**Sensitivity to Context:**

- People become increasingly aware of how context affects their experiences.
- Understanding the context helps with forgiveness and making sense of past behaviors.
- Therapist can foster context-sensitivity by asking about the context of experiences and exploring its relationship to feelings or behaviors.

**Capacity to Take Perspective:**

- People at this stage understand that perspectives are relative and unique.
- Developing a personal perspective is essential for self-development.
- Therapist can foster perspective-taking by asking patients to consider different viewpoints, including their own and others'.

**Advanced Metacognitive Skills: Later Postformal Levels**

**The Ability to Take a Wider Perspective:**

- Patients develop a wider perspective on their condition.
- Therapist can help patients see how their understanding has changed over time and appreciate representational diversity.
- Family-tree work can aid in developing a transgenerational perspective.
- Perspective-taking is fostered by asking patients to take others' perspectives, including the therapist's perspective on their experiences.

### The Ability to Develop an Orientation

**Orientation**

- Specific kind of perspective-taking
- Two types: past versus present, self versus other

**Past vs. Present Orientation**

- Anxious-preoccupied patients: preoccupied with unresolved past experiences
- Therapist helps patient become more aware of relative orientation to past and present
- Use anchoring scale (1-10) to assess awareness

**Self versus Other Orientation**

- Narcissistically vulnerable patients: all self-transference or all other-transference
- Unable to hold both self and other in mind simultaneously
- Therapist uses anchoring scale to help develop metacognitive capacity

**Awareness of Organization or Coherence of Mind**

- Patients with personality and dissociative disorders, insecure attachment have low coherence
- Benefit from learning to accurately perceive organization/disorganization of mind
- Use anchoring scale (1-10) to assess level of organization
- Gradually learn to recognize degree of organization and its association with mental states and external context
- Repeated use fosters increased metacognitive awareness and coherence of mind

**Recognition of Interdependence**

- Patients develop wider perspective recognizing interconnection and interdependence of all things
- Encouraged to adopt common humanity perspective
- Therapist helps patient see connection between own suffering and that of others

**Metacognitive Development**

- Patient develops wider perspective or ultimate concern
- Develops action plans for actualization
- Achieves freedom from internal constraints.

### Contradiction

**The Psychology of Ultimate Concerns** by Emmons (1999)

* Individuals may have contradictory personal strivings leading to diminished wellbeing.
* Developing higher-order goals, or an ultimate concern, reduces internal conflict and increases wellbeing.
* Therapist suggestions for discovering one's ultimate concern:
  * Reflect on central guiding principle of life
  * Identify core purpose giving deepest meaning
  * Articulate details of meaning-system
  * Observe how it affects activities and engagement

**Cognitive Development (Stage 8 - Turquoise)**

* Discovers limitations of conceptual thought in favor of direct awareness.
* Patients become aware of their representational construction processes.
* Direct awareness is seen as a more valid means of knowing than conceptual thought.
* Making the supersystem the basis of operation allows for switching out of conceptual thought.
* Everything can be seen accurately through direct awareness, free from limitations of conceptual knowing.
* Creativity and generativity become salient.

**The Self as a Temporary Functional Organizing Principle (Late Stages of Development)**

* Individual self is no longer experienced as solid or permanent.
* It serves as a functional structure to operate in the relative world but is less the locus of identity.
* Higher-order metacognitive skills develop at Stage 9 (Indigo).

**Higher-Order Metacognitive Skills (Stage 9 - Indigo)**

* Developing universal or ego-transcendent perspective.
* Shifting from systems to a larger field in Indo-Tibetan Buddhism.
* New metacognitive skills:
  * Developing simultaneous, multiple points of view free of contradiction.
  * Immediate awareness and automatic monitoring of ongoing processes.
  * Diminished reactivity to immediate experiences or states of mind.
* These forms of metacognitive functioning may emerge spontaneously through practice.
* Therapist encourages reflection, description, and recognition of changes in these skills.

### Overview

**Metacognitive Capacity Development in Psychotherapy**

- Not all metacognitive capacities described in this chapter will be applicable to every patient.
- Patients exhibit a spectrum of developmental levels and corresponding foundations for cognitive and metacognitive functioning.
- Tailor metacognitive capacity development based on individual patient needs:
  * Perspective-widening methods for limited perspective-taking ability.
  * Organization methods for disorganized thinking.
  * Later-stage development and higher metacognitive capacities as patients progress.
- Collaborative approach to therapy:
  * Patient-centered treatment based on problematic deficits.
  * Continued development towards greater potentials of functioning and wellbeing.
- Next chapter focuses on direct methods for enhancing collaborative capacity.
- Collaboration-enhancing methods beneficial for patients with attachment and personality disorders.
- Incorporating collaboration-enhancing methods contributes to efficient and stable treatment outcomes.

### CHAPTER 10 The Third Pillar Fostering Collaborative Capacity and Behavior

**Chapter 10: The Third Pillar - Fostering Collaborative Capacity and Behavior**

**The Importance of Collaborative Capacity**
- Securely attached individuals exhibit collaborative behavior, which is essential for treatment.
- Cooperation and collaboration are hardwired in humans.
- Insecure attachment impairs the emergence of natural collaborative functioning.

**Cooperative Behavior as an Evolutionary Adaptation (Gilbert, 1989)**
- Cooperative behavior evolved to modify individualistic competitiveness.
- Cooperation leads to social rules and division of labor.
- Cooperativeness is concerned with linking, joining, and minimizing differences.
- Key to cooperation: sharing, communication, and learning social rules.

**Maladaptive Lack of Cooperativeness**
- Inability to cooperate and share lies at the heart of many psychological difficulties.
- Includes tendencies towards competition, submission, fear of deception, etc.

**Healthy Cooperative Behavior**
- Sense of "togetherness" and joining.
- Mutuality and reciprocity.
- Status defined by altruism and contribution.

**Human Infants vs. Apes: Cooperativeness (Tomasello, 2009)**
- Human infants are intrinsically altruistic.
- Display helping, informing, and sharing behaviors.
- Shared intentionality is the underlying principle of human cooperativeness.

**Shared Intentionality**
- Ability to create joint intentions and commitments in cooperative endeavors.
- Structured by processes of joint attention and mutual knowledge.

**Human Infants' Cooperative Behavior**
- Helping, informing, and sharing behaviors emerge early.
- Indiscriminate cooperativeness at a young age.
- Cultural norms internalized by the end of third year.

### Collaborative Communication and Shared Intentionality

**Collaborative Communication and Shared Intentionality**

- Tomasello (2009) defines collaboration as mutualism, shared intentionality, joint goals, division of labor, and shared focus of attention (pp. 52-58, 61, 67, 70, 90).
- Collaboration is essential for human evolution and development (p. 54).
- Human children collaborate with others from early ontogeny (p. 105).
- Cooperation and collaborative communication coevolved (p. 74).
- Communication behavior in infancy is inherently collaborative (p. 242).

**Cooperation and Collaboration in Children with Caregivers and Peers**

- Secure children expect caregiver's help, while insecure children do not (pp. 129-131).
- Secure infants look longer when caregiver leaves during distress, insecure infants are surprised by their return (Tomasello, 2009, citing Johnson, Dweck, & Chen, 2007).
- Abused children do not show empathic concern for distressed peers (Tomasello, 2009, citing Main & George, 1985).
- Heard et al. describe a "peer interest sharing" system where participants are equals (pp. 87-89).

**Clinical Relevance of Lack of Cooperation and Collaborativeness**

- Insecure and abused children show impaired nonverbal and verbal cooperative and collaborative behavior (p. 132).
- These patterns persist throughout childhood and into adulthood (p. 132).
- Therapeutic task is to provide corrective developmental conditions for collaboration in therapy (p. 132).

### Defining Psychotherapy as Collaborative Partnership: The Contribution of Interpersonal Psychotherapy Harry Stack Sullivan was the first psychotherapis

**Defining Psychotherapy as a Collaborative Partnership**

- Harry Stack Sullivan's definition of psychotherapy:
  - Cooperation: favoring shared goals over competition
  - Collaboration: intersubjectivity, sensitivity to another person, and mutual exploration
  - Distinct from juvenile cooperation and competition

**Sullivan's View on Psychotherapy as a Collaborative Partnership**

- Cooperation: giving and taking around shared goals
- Collaboration: feeling of sensitivity to another person
- Therapy is "a matter of we"
- Evolutionary perspective: cooperation puts aside competition for shared interests, collaboration involves mutual regulation

**Interpersonal Psychotherapy (IPT) as a Collaborative Partnership**

- Teyber and McClure's definition: psychotherapy as a collaborative partnership
- Therapist's role: articulate clear expectations, enact collaborative behavior
- Therapy is a shared interaction requiring participation from both parties

**Application of the Collaborative Partnership Concept in Psychotherapy**

- Applicable to all good psychotherapies
- Challenge with attachment-impaired patients: they are not cooperative or collaborative, yet therapy requires cooperation and collaboration
- Focus of attachment-based treatment: help patients become cooperative and collaborative for their lives and therapy process

**Starting Points: Fostering Collaboration at the Beginning of Therapy**

- Importance of fostering collaboration from the start
- Clear communication, empathy, and understanding
- Establishing a safe and trusting environment
- Encouraging active participation and engagement from the patient.

### A MUTUALLY NEGOTIATED TREATMENT PLAN AND INFORMED CONSENT

**Collaborative Therapy:**

- Begins with exploring patient's hopes and expectations for treatment
- Identifying main problems and establishing mutually agreed-upon goals
- Asking hypothetical questions to invite exploration of treatment success
- Sharing therapist's impressions and expectations
- Mutually negotiated treatment plan: engaging client in assessing issues, past experiences, and shared goals
- Shared decision-making about best approach to treatment
- Transparent therapists openly discuss diagnostic impressions, reasoning, and anticipated difficulties
- Stronger alliance between therapist and patient
- Collaborative interpersonal process provides corrective emotional experience
- Determinant of treatment outcome: mutual collaboration

**Process of Collaboration:**

- Exploration of patient's hopes and expectations
  * Identifying main problems
  * Hypothetical question about treatment success
- Sharing therapist's impressions and expectations
  * Mutually negotiated treatment plan
  * Transparent therapists: open discussion of diagnostic impressions, reasoning, and difficulties
- Informed Consent Document
  * Explicit and tangible collaboration partnership
  * Signed by both patient and therapist

**Benefits of Collaborative Therapy:**

- Stronger alliance between therapist and patient
- Corrective emotional experience for clients
- New way of interacting for many clients
- Determinant of treatment outcome: mutual collaboration

### ESTABLISHING THE TREATMENT FRAME AS COLLABORATIVE PARTNERSHIP

**Establishing the Treatment Frame as Collaborative Partnership**

- Patients with attachment disturbances have impaired collaborativeness and may withdraw or make excessive demands.
- A detailed treatment frame contract is necessary to define roles and expectations, reducing misunderstandings and treatment ruptures.
- Emphasis on negotiation and collaboration during contract development, not the contract itself as a signed document.

**Safety Expectations in Treatment Frame Contract**

- Clear expectations and rules regarding safety are essential for attachment-based treatment.
- Establishing safety sets the foundation for therapy as a secure base.
- Addresses potential threats to patient's safety, such as self-harm, substance use, abusive relationships, and dissociative reenactment.

**Collaborative Behavior and Therapy-Interfering Behaviors**

- Collaborative behavior is a goal throughout therapy.
- Clear understanding of how to handle therapy-interfering behaviors like lying, refusing to discuss certain topics, or toxic behavior.
- Open, honest disclosure and communication are crucial for the therapeutic relationship.

**Consistency and Reliability in Treatment Frame Contract**

- Explicit understanding of handling potential disruptions like extended absences, decreased therapy frequency, or financial issues.
- Collaborative development of treatment frame understanding, plan, and informed consent document.

**Fostering a Collaborative State of Mind in Psychotherapy: The Role of the Rome**

- First few sessions prioritize attunement and collaboration before task focus.
- Therapist must be willing to slow down and address patient reactivity for mutual negotiation.

### CENTER OF COGNITIVE PSYCHOTHERAPY

**Center of Cognitive Psychotherapy (Rome) and Attachment Disturbances**

* Importance of fostering collaboration in psychotherapy for patients with attachment disturbances (Liotti, 1999; Cortina & Liotti, 2010)
* Cooperative behavioral system: an innate motivational system mediating cooperative behavior (Liotti, 1999)
* Superior cooperative and communicative abilities essential for early human groups' survival and prosperity (Cortina & Liotti, 2010)

**Fostering a Collaborative State of Mind**

* Deactivated in patients with significant attachment disturbance (Cortina & Liotti, 2010)
* Begin during the first therapy session (Liotti, 1999)
* Foundation of safety and protection in therapy (Liotti & Gilbert, 2011)
* Explicit agreement of goals and rules of therapeutic work (Liotti, 1999)

**Mutual Understanding and Collaborative Exploration**

* Key to cooperation between patient and therapist (Liotti, 2005)
* Ongoing dialogue is more effective than traditional one-sided interpretation (Liotti, 2005)
* Collaborative exploration essential for patients with attachment disturbances (Dimaggio et al., 2007)
* Mutual sharing of information about treatment problems and goals (Dimaggio et al., 2007)
* Therapist's open sharing of session main points fosters collaborative exploration (Dimaggio et al., 2007)

**Collaborative Empiricism in Attachment-Based Treatment**

* Collaborative exploratory exchanges promote mentalizing ability (Allen, 2013)
* Ruptures in therapeutic alliance require collaborative exploration and repair (Liotti, 2005)
* Cooperative/collaborative behavioral system distinct from attachment system (Liotti, 2005)
* Collaborative exploration outside the attachment system can strengthen the attachment relationship (Liotti, 2005)

**Addressing Impaired Collaborative Behavior in Ongoing Therapy**

* Two broad areas of impaired collaborative behavior: nonverbal and verbal

**Nonverbal Collaborative Behavior**

* Synchronization and match with the other's nonverbal behaviors indicative of secure adult patients (Beebe & Lachmann, 2014)
* Desynchronization and mismatches common in adult patients with some pattern of insecure attachment (Beebe & Lachmann, 2014)

**Nonverbal Collaborative Behavior: Focus of Research by Beatrice Beebe**

* Synchronization in timing and match with the other's nonverbal behaviors in secure adult patients (Beebe & Lachmann, 2014)
* Desynchronization and mismatches common in adult patients with some pattern of insecure attachment (Beebe & Lachmann, 2014)

**Verbal Collaborative Behavior**

* Secure attachment on the Adult Attachment Interview indicated by healthy collaboration between interviewee and interviewer (Main, 1991)
* Individuals with insecure attachment rarely show collaborative verbal behavior on the AAI (Main, 1991)
* Collaborativeness assessed through meeting or violating Grice's maxims (Main, 1991)

**Improving Nonverbal and Verbal Collaborative Behavior Throughout Therapy**

* Third pillar of attachment-based treatment (not explicitly stated in text but implied)

### IMPROVING NONVERBAL COLLABORATION

**Improving Nonverbal Collaboration**

Beebe and Lachmann (2014) emphasize the importance of understanding nonverbal interactive mismatches in therapy, as they can be traced back to early interaction disturbances. These mismatches are particularly relevant for individuals with insecure attachment styles. Beebe's research on nonverbal collaboration between infants and caregivers identified two main types of dyadic nonverbal exchanges: collaborative and contradictory.

**Collaborative Nonverbal Exchanges:**

- Secure infant and caregiver show reciprocal influence and highly coordinated, synchronized behaviors.
- Contingent behaviors: each knows that initiated behavior has predictable effects on self or other.
- Repairable mismatches and ruptures.
- Heightened moments of positive affective intensity.
- Child develops well-organized patterns of dyadic interaction and a sense of effectance.

**Contradictory, or Noncollaborative, Mutual Nonverbal Exchanges:**

- Significant and repetitive mismatched nonverbal influence.
- Uncoordinated and desynchronized exchanges.
- Lack of contingent behaviors.
