- Frequent ruptures with little possibility of repair.
- Virtual absence of heightened moments of positive affect.
- Child develops dysfunctional patterns of reciprocal nonverbal exchanges and a lack of effectance.

Beebe's research on nonverbal collaboration between infants and caregivers led to the development of methods for transforming dysfunctional patterns of exchange. This work has influenced therapeutic approaches by focusing on nonverbal collaborativeness:

- Beebe and her associates used videotapes to provide mothers with direct visual feedback, helping them learn healthy patterns of exchange and transform interactions with their infants.
- They later applied the video microanalysis method to investigate nonverbal patterns of interaction between adult patients and therapists.
- The "transformational view": dysfunctional nonverbal patterns can be fundamentally changed through therapy, supporting the emergence of secure patterns of interaction.

### The first step toward developing healthy nonverbal collaboration in insecurely attached adult patients is to establish a nonverbal secure base in the 

**Developing Healthy Nonverbal Collaboration in Insecurely Attached Adult Patients**

1. **Establishing a Nonverbal Secure Base:**
   - Therapist's continuous, nondistracted attention and intense engagement
   - Proper position, head orientation, body posture, stillness, gaze, and facial affect display
   - Adjusting arousal and degree of engagement to match the patient
   - Avoiding patterns of nonverbal distancing, hyperengaging, or mismatching

2. **Observing Main Patterns of Nonverbal Exchange:**
   - Using Beebe's categories for coding nonverbal patterns as a starting point
   - Modifications for adult nonverbal exchanges:
     1. Chair position and posture
     2. Head orientation
     3. Gaze
     4. Facial affective display
     5. Initiated self-touch
     6. Postural gestures
     7. Breathing
     8. Vocal rhythm
     9. Vocal tone
     10. Vocal pitch
   - Carefully observing salient and repetitive patterns for both patient and therapist
   - Being vigilant and attuned to nonverbal behaviors, some of which may occur outside awareness

**Patient Nonverbal Patterns:**

- **Dismissing Attachment:**
  * Sits back or slumps in chair
  * Infrequent head and gaze orientation towards therapist
  * Off-putting facial display patterns
  * Defensive postural displays
  * Self-regulatory behaviors
  * Extended silences and curtailed vocalizations
  * Soft or barely audible voice, with descending pitch

- **Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment:**
  * Sits forward in chair or looms
  * Manifests hypervigilant fixed stare
  * Variety of frequent facial displays of distress
  * Exaggerated spontaneous postural displays
  * Plaintive postural displays
  * High postural anxiety like rubbing hands and feet, jiggling legs
  * Inability to remain silent or take turns in vocalization
  * Fast vocal rhythm
  * Vocalizations over the therapist
  * Descending pitch

- **Disorganized Attachment:**
  * Contradictory mixture of dismissing and anxious-preoccupied nonverbal behaviors

### The third step is to identify the most salient and repetitive nonverbal patterns of exchange, especially the most dysfunctional patterns. Identificati

**Step 1:** Identify salient and repetitive nonverbal patterns of exchange, focusing on dysfunctional ones.

- Determine main attachment pattern (secure, dismissing, anxious-preoccupied, or disorganized)
- Identify primary nonverbal behaviors exhibited frequently and repetitively by the patient

**Step 2:** Transform dysfunctional nonverbal exchange patterns through:

- Nonverbal engagement
- "Match-and-change" activity
  * Continually rematching when mismatches occur
  * Responding with purposeful nonverbal behavior
  * Matching patient's nonverbal behavior moment by moment

**Case Illustration:** Dolores

- Failed to make eye contact: Beebe made steady eye contact
- Oriented body posture away from therapist: Identified and collaboratively worked on face-to-face interaction
- Became inaudible: Repeated back patient's utterances with a more flowing rhythm
- Produced still-face reaction: Attempted to make responsive and exaggerated facial displays
- Mutual review of video footage used for learning about own feelings
- Emergence of mutual, dyadic nonverbal affective exchange

**Step 3:** Transform dysfunctional nonverbal exchange patterns through enhancing metacognitive awareness.

- Collaboratively share observations on salient nonverbal behaviors
- Set context for intervention by asking permission to share observations in the moment they occur
- Contribute to decrease of dysfunctional, noncollaborative nonverbal communication and increase of healthy nonverbal communication and collaboration

### Once the patient’s metacognitive awareness of a nonverbal behavior is established, our method entails engaging the patient with experiential focusing 

**Method for Addressing Dysfunctional Nonverbal Behaviors**

1. **Metacognitive Awareness**: Patient becomes aware of specific nonverbal behaviors and their associated emotions.
2. **Experiential Focusing**: Patient explores bodily felt sense of nonverbal behavior and its function (self-regulation or interactive regulation).
3. **Direct Change Experiment**: Patient attempts to change nonverbal behavior to something more collaborative, focusing on resulting felt sense.
4. **Targeted Self-Regulation**: Patient experiments with discontinuing specific self-regulatory behaviors and focuses on underlying emotional/arousal state.
5. **Meaning-Making**: Understanding historical context and meaning of dysfunctional nonverbal patterns through co-constructed interpretation.

**Effects of Interventions**

1. **Contingent Effectiveness**: Patient learns that nonverbal behavior affects therapist's response, and vice versa.
2. **New Pattern of Nonverbal Exchange**: Mutual match-and-change affective displays become frequent, mismatches diminish.
3. **Heightened Positive Affective Moments**: Increase in positive affective moments between patient and therapist contributes to secure therapeutic base.
4. **Nonverbal Disconnection Repair**: Nonverbal disconnections are repaired more easily.
5. **New Preverbal Schemas**: Patient develops new schemas for healthy, collaborative nonverbal exchanges with others.
6. **Enhanced Sense of Being Seen and Known**: Patient experiences increased sense of being seen, known, and contingently matched by the therapist.

### IMPROVING VERBAL COLLABORATION

**Improving Verbal Collaboration**

**Origins of Verbal Collaborative Behavior**

- Human communication is a cooperative enterprise (Tomasello, 2010) based on:
  - Mutually assumed common conceptual ground
  - Mutually assumed cooperative communicative motives
- Shared intentionality is essential for collaborative activities
- Cooperative communication evolved from nonverbal pointing and pantomiming in apes
- Human verbal communication adds collaboration, not just requests (Tomasello, 2010)
- Important element: referential intention (what the speaker intends to communicate)

**Structure of Verbal Collaborative Behavior: Grice's Maxims**

- Cooperative principle: speaker implicates intended meaning, and listener inquires for understanding
- Four maxims ensure meaningfulness in collaborative discourse
  - Quality: accurate, genuine, and well-evidenced communication
  - Quantity: right amount of information about shared topic
  - Relevance: content stays close to referential intention and topic
  - Manner: clear, unambiguous, and orderly expression

**Collaborative Verbal Communication in Children's Development**

- Collaborative verbal communication evolves from daily routines between child and parent
- Central to this process is the development of grammatical conventions (Tomasello, 2010)
  - Grammar of requesting: combinations of pointing and language
  - Grammar of informing: multigestural combinations identifying objects and information
  - Grammar of sharing: use of narratives to share experiences

**Origins of Human Communication (Tomasello, 2010)**

- Cooperative communication is a fundamental aspect of human communication
- Shared intentionality is necessary for collaborative activities
- Cooperative infrastructure evolved as part of larger human adaptation for cooperation and cultural life
- Pointing and pantomiming were critical transition points in the evolution of human communication
- Conventional languages arose by piggybacking on these already understood gestures

**Human Communication is Cooperative (Tomasello, 2010)**

- Human communication operates naturally within contexts of:
  - Mutually assumed common conceptual ground
  - Mutually assumed cooperative communicative motives
- Shared intentionality is necessary for engaging in uniquely human forms of collaborative activity
- Cooperative communication arose as a way to coordinate collaborative activities more efficiently

**Collaborative Communication and Evolution (Tomasello, 2010)**

- Cooperative communication emerged as part and parcel of the evolution of humans' unique forms of collaborative activity
- During children's development, collaborative language evolves in the context of daily collaborative routines between child and parent
- Central to this process is the development of grammatical conventions

**Grice's Maxims (Grice, 1989)**

- Cooperative principle: speaker implicates intended meaning, and listener inquires for understanding
- Four maxims ensure meaningfulness in collaborative discourse
  - Quality: accurate, genuine, and well-evidenced communication
  - Quantity: right amount of information about shared topic
  - Relevance: content stays close to referential intention and topic
  - Manner: clear, unambiguous, and orderly expression

**Politeness Principles in Conversation (Leech, 1983)**

- Six politeness standards or maxims in modern Western culture
  - Tactful communication: minimizes cost, maximizes benefit to others
  - Generous communication: minimizes benefit to self, maximizes benefit to others
  - Approval: minimizes dispraise of others, maximizes praise and enhancement of others
  - Modesty: minimizes praise to self, maximizes praise to others
  - Agreement: minimizes disagreement, maximizes common interests and agreement
  - Sympathy: minimizes antipathy, maximizes listening to the needs of the other
- Positive opposite of toxic verbal communication

### An enormous amount of difficulty all through life arises from the fact that communicative behavior miscarries because words do not carry meaning, but 

**Communicative Difficulties and Sullivanian Therapy**

* Communicative behavior fails due to words not carrying meaning but evoking meaning instead
* Early Sullivanian therapists focused on enhancing collaborative verbal communication in treatment
* Importance of providing honest feedback when patient's communication doesn't make sense
* Mary Main and associates studied failures of collaborative verbal communication using the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI)

**Collaborative Verbal Communication and Grice's Maxims on AAI**

* Secure individuals exemplify collaborative behavior with truthful, adequate, relevant, and clear expression
* Insecure individuals show various types of noncollaborative verbal behavior:
  * Dismissing: numerous violations of quality (overgeneralizations, untruthful or contradictory statements, succinct descriptions)
  * Anxious-preoccupied: numerous violations of quantity, relevance, and manner (wandering off topic, adding too much information, vague and ambiguous statements)
  * Disorganized: numerous violations of all four maxims

**Collaborative Verbal Communication in Everyday Life and Psychotherapy**

* Securely attached individuals talk openly, patiently, clearly, stay on topic, evoke emotions appropriately, take responsibility, reflectively listen, and validate each other's perspectives
* Dismissing patients: talk too little, overly positive, intellectualized, avoid conflict, give ultimatums, make threats, argue from a fixed position
* Anxious-preoccupied patients: talk too much, interrupt, perseverate on topics, repeat points, demand listener's attention, use vague and ambiguous language, exhibit excessive verbal demands, complain, blame self or other
* Disorganized patients: confusing and contradictory mixture of dismissing and anxious-preoccupied behaviors, disorganized and incoherent speech, shifting dissociative states
* Therapist's role: provide feedback when communication doesn't make sense, help patient clarify and make more sense, ensure patient is aware of what was said during dissociative states.

### A Model for Collaborative Dialogue in Treatment

**Collaborative Dialogue in Psychotherapy: A Model by Karlen Lyons-Ruth**

* Based on observations of infant-caregiver interactions
* Focuses on developing representation of nonverbal, implicit experiences
* Two-person collaborative dialogue for facilitating developmental change

**Components of Collaborative Dialogue**

* Implicit relational level engagement
* Careful attention to intersubjective experience
* Open acceptance of a broad range of affects
* Active scaffolding to inclusive levels of dialogue
* Engaged struggle and negotiation during periods of change

**Outcome: Coherent Communication**

* Eliciting collaborative state of mind
* Efficient repair of ruptures
* Establishing new levels of awareness
* Brokering process of change

**Eleven Steps for Establishing Collaborative Verbal Communication in Insecurely Attached Patients**

1. **Establish a verbal secure base:**
   * Reflective listening and validation
      - Manifest content
      - Affective meaning
      - Latent meaning
      - Consistency and depth
2. **Therapist's collaborative stance:**
   * Mutual exchange and collaboration through verbal exchange is important, welcome, and beneficial
3. **Explicit ground rules of verbal discourse:**
   * Make explicit the rules for collaborative verbal dialogue
   * Dismissing patients: full disclosure, no censorship
   * Soft-spoken behavior: speak loudly and clearly
4. **Encourage reflection:**
   * Encourage patients to reflect on their inner process
   * Help patients understand the importance of self-exploration
5. **Understand patient's attachment style:**
   * Adapt communication style based on patient's attachment style
6. **Repair ruptures:**
   * Repair communication breakdowns promptly and effectively
7. **Use metaphors and stories:**
   * Use metaphors and stories to facilitate understanding
8. **Encourage emotional expression:**
   * Create a safe space for emotional expression
9. **Maintain a nonjudgmental attitude:**
   * Avoid negative judgment and criticism
10. **Use open-ended questions:**
    * Encourage exploration through open-ended questions
11. **Provide structure and predictability:**
    * Offer a consistent, predictable therapeutic environment.

### In addition to sometimes letting the patient know verbally, as above, that we can’t hear what he or she is 255 saying, we find it useful at times to u

**Establishing Collaborative Verbal Communication**

1. *Signal for inaudible speech:* Use nonverbal signals, such as raising a hand or using metaphors, to help soft-spoken patients develop metacognitive awareness of their speech volume.
2. *Turn-taking:* Teach anxious-preoccupied patients the rules of turn-taking by pausing, allowing responses, and resuming speaking when the therapist has stopped talking. Use nonverbal signals to indicate violations of turn-taking.
3. **Metacognitive inquiries:** Explore with patients how they know they are being heard and what indicates listening, supporting them in moving beyond content into co-creation of a mutual connection.
4. **Secure base for verbal exchange:** Make explicit rules for communication, such as allowing patients to express uncertainty or ask questions, and encouraging clarification from the therapist.
5. **Observing problematic patterns:** Collaboratively observe and list violations of verbal collaboration with the patient, modeling cooperative activity and supporting metacognitive awareness.
6. **Ameliorating dysfunctional patterns:** Identify and target specific dysfunctional verbal exchange patterns, primarily through intervention and determination of attachment pattern (secure, dismissing, anxious-preoccupied, or disorganized).

* Soft-spoken patients:* Use nonverbal signals to indicate inaudible speech and help them develop metacognitive awareness.
* Anxious-preoccupied patients:* Teach turn-taking rules and use nonverbal signals for violations.
* Metacognitive inquiries:* Explore how patients know they are being heard and what indicates listening.
* Secure base for verbal exchange:* Make explicit rules for communication, allowing uncertainty and questioning.
* Observing problematic patterns:* Collaboratively list violations of verbal collaboration.
* Ameliorating dysfunctional patterns:* Identify and target specific dysfunctional verbal exchange patterns through intervention.

### Since dismissing patients typically show a normal capacity for exploration except around attachment themes, it is important that the therapist encoura

**Attachment-Based Therapy for Dismissing and Preoccupied Patients**

**Dismissing Patients:**

* Normal capacity for exploration except around attachment themes
* Encourage exploration of attachment themes
* Overly succinct and unemotional discourse
* Validate positive experiences, then encourage exploration of negative ones
* Idealize attachment figures, put positive spin on negative experiences
* Mark any affect that appears during exploration

**Interaction with Dismissing Patients:**

* Express interest and give feedback for more information
* Invite patient to describe underlying emotional experience
* Encourage exploration of full range of early attachment experiences
* Mark any affect that appears during exploration

**Preoccupied Patients:**

* Speak on and on, wander off topic
* Use "stop-action technique" for pauses and reflection
* Fear losing contact without ongoing speaking
* Encourage exploration in general and of canned or wandering discourse
* Unclear or ambiguous expressions require experiential focusing and clear description
* Undeveloped sense of self, overly concerned about other's state of mind

**Interaction with Preoccupied Patients:**

* Use "stop-action technique" for pauses and reflection
* Guide patient to attend to immediate felt experience
* Build ability to bring attention to internal states
* Use anchoring scales to direct focus away from others to self
* Mark any affect that appears during exploration
* Redirect attention and mark affect when describing past experiences.

### The seventh step entails identifying and modifying toxic noncollaborative verbal behavior. For the dismissing patient, toxic verbal behavior typically

**Step 7: Identifying and Modifying Toxic Noncollaborative Verbal Behavior**

* For Dismissing Patient: derogation, invalidation, ultimatums, themes of devaluation and toxic shaming
* For Preoccupied Patient: excessive blaming of self and others, excessive demands or imperatives, self-defeating thinking, overreaction in the face of conflict, frequent "mind-reading"
* Therapist gives immediate feedback on toxic discourse and explores underlying attachment themes
* Co-construct positive opposite of observed toxic verbal behavior
	+ Dismissing derogation: "I deeply value the depth of my connection with you"
	+ Preoccupied excessive blaming: correct focus to internal self-experience, create healthy need statements, offer appreciation instead of blame
* Patients benefit from Leech's (1983) six maxims of politeness in imagination

**Step 8: Therapist's Verbal Match-and-Change**

* Dismissing patient: reflectively mirror and develop content, mark underlying feelings
* Preoccupied patient: call for pause, summarize main point, request clearer communication, highlight severe violations of manner and ask for "best-fit" words.

### The ninth step entails enhancing verbal contingency communication and dialogue. Patients with insecure attachment need to see directly and frequently 

**Step 9: Enhancing Verbal Contingency Communication and Dialogue**

* Patients with insecure attachment need to see the impact of their verbal communication on others.
* Reflective listening and mirroring by the therapist is the first step in showing the patient that their communication has had an effect.
* Therapist gives feedback on the immediate effect of the patient's communication on their state of mind.
* Feedback can be given nonverbally or verbally, showing emotional impact or expressing thoughts arising from the patient's communication.
* Frequent naming of effects helps the patient understand the contingency between their words and the therapist's response.

**Step 10: Enhancing Patient's Metacognitive Awareness of Discourse Violations**

* Therapist addresses violations in quality, quantity, and relevance of speech.
* Asks questions to increase patient's awareness of discourse violations.
* Helps patient stay on topic and be aware of irrelevancies.

**Step 11: Collaborative Co-constructed Meaning-making**

* Focuses on the content of verbal discourse.
* Therapist helps patient clarify primary referential intention.
* Develops a back-and-forth interaction to explore and shape understanding.
* Helps patient formulate own experience.
* Co-created meaning-making starts with focus on current states and situations, later develops into a coherent life narrative.

**Signs of Successful Activation of the Collaborative Behavioral System**

* Nonverbal behaviors become matched, synchronized, and contingently effective.
* Verbal communication follows normal rules of collaborative discourse.
* Verbal communication is sensible and meaningful, following Grice's maxims with few violations.
* Verbal communication is coherent and scores 7-9 on the AAI.
* Verbal communication is contingently effective on the listener.
* Patient monitors lapses in verbal discourse accurately and ongoing.

**Outcomes of Successful Treatment**

* Healthy nonverbal and verbal communication in everyday conversation.
* Capacity for healthy, meaningful, coherent, and contingently effective communication.
* Direct experience of benefits and rewards of healthy communication with others.
* Overcoming developmental arrest of the collaborative behavioral system.

### Case Illustration The transcript below is from a session with a man in his 30s. “John” had presented for therapy for symptoms of depression and low se

**Case Illustration: John's Therapy Sessions**

**Background**
- John, a man in his 30s, presented for therapy with symptoms of depression and low self-esteem.
- Assessed as having a mixed attachment style, predominantly anxious-preoccupied with some dismissing features.
- Struggles with rapid speech and fading slurs at the end of sentences, making it difficult for therapist to understand him.

**Session Excerpt**
- Therapist notices John's speaking patterns and asks him to slow down and clarify.
- John acknowledges his fast speech developed as a kid to avoid attention or criticism.
- Therapist suggests John take time with what he says, breathe deeply, and enunciate clearly.
- John expresses difficulty handling a conflict at work with a co-worker (Jake).
- Both found each other stressful; communication was noncollaborative.

**Therapist's Approach**
- Maintains positive, collaborative engagement.
- Points out nonverbal and verbal lapses in collaboration with interest and acceptance.
- Encourages John to translate nonverbal signals into clear, collaborative comments.

**Impact on Treatment**
- Enhances understanding through effective communication.
- Increases John's metacognitive awareness of his communication patterns.
- Reflects the interdependence of effective communication and attachment security in treatment.

### PART IV TYPE-SPECIFIC TREATMENT Application of The Three Pillars Treatment To Dismissing, Anxious-Preoccupied, and Disorganized Attachment

**Part IV: Type-Specific Treatment**

* Application of The Three Pillars Treatment to: Dismissing, Anxious-Preoccupied, Disorganized Attachment

**Dismissing Attachment**

* Developmental care-receiving experiences: Minimization or denial of need for comfort and connection
* Tailoring the three pillars:
  * **Secure Base:** Validate feelings and needs, offer emotional support
  * **Challenges:** Address dismissive defenses, explore attachment injuries
  * **Repair:** Practice emotional communication and expression

**Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment**

* Developmental care-receiving experiences: Fear of abandonment or rejection, anxiety about relationship closeness
* Tailoring the three pillars:
  * **Secure Base:** Provide consistent presence and emotional responsiveness
  * **Challenges:** Address anxious defenses, explore attachment injuries
  * **Repair:** Practice emotional regulation and secure attachment behaviors

**Disorganized Attachment**

* Developmental care-receiving experiences: Unpredictable or contradictory caregiving, confusion about attachment needs
* Tailoring the three pillars:
  * **Secure Base:** Establish safety and predictability, provide clear communication
  * **Challenges:** Address disorganized defenses, explore attachment injuries
  * **Repair:** Practice emotional regulation and secure attachment behaviors

**General Considerations**

* Treatment is individualized to the patient
* Understanding specific developmental experiences guides treatment approaches
* The Three Pillars of Treatment are adapted based on each patient's needs.

### CHAPTER 11 Treating Dismissing Attachment The Origins of Dismissing Attachment

**Chapter 11: Treating Dismissing Attachment**

**Origins of Dismissing Attachment**

* Ainsworth et al. (1978) identified repeated maternal rejection as the origin of avoidant attachment in children.
* Mothers of avoidant children were described as especially rejecting, particularly during physical contact.
* Rejection communication included direct and indirect expressions of negative affect and behavior.
* Avoidant children often failed to show collaborative nonverbal behaviors like head-turning and gaze fixing on the parent.
* Main and Weston (1981) confirmed that repeated rejection is related to avoidant attachment development.
* Mothers of avoidant children were less emotionally expressive than secure or other insecure attachment types.

**Characteristics of Dismissing Mothers**

* Selectively misattuned to negative affects, especially when directed toward them.
* Comfortable attuning to positive affect and play.
* Rejecting of children's bids for comfort and reassurance.
* Use comments to override babies' affect.
* Misattunement likely reflects the mother's internal state and needs.

**Therapeutic Stance with Dismissing Patients**

* Underrepresented in clinical settings, often seek treatment for reasons other than relational dissatisfaction.
* When presenting with relational disturbance, attribute source to others' behavior.
* Consistent positive engagement by therapist is crucial for reactivating attachment system.
* Careful, accurate attunement to patient-initiated proximity- and contact-seeking, verbal expressions of felt needs, and negative affects.
* Permissive encouragement of exploration of attachment experiences, including past and current relationships.
* Support the emergence and experience of negative affect related to attachment.
* Encourage free exploration of self in the context of attachment relationships.

### Using Ideal Parent Figure Imagery With the Dismissing Patient

**Ideal Parent Figure Imagery with Dismissing Patients**

**Ideal for early phase treatment**
- Structures sessions on attachment themes
- Allows relational distance from therapist
- Less threatening than here-and-now relationship

**Benefits of Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) imagery**
- Transforms unrealistic idealization into healthy internal working model
- Acceptable to patient due to strong parental idealization

**Challenges and initial reactions**
- Patient may feel bewilderment or threat
  * Opportunity for therapist to help identify missing sense of secure attachment
- Patient may become fearful
  * Therapist helps patient understand attachment as a source of threat
- Collaborative interaction between patient and therapist

**Closeness/distance regulation**
- For patients unable to tolerate proximity with IPFs
- Imagery includes experimenting with closeness and distance
- Patient learns self-regulation and control in imagery

**Considerations when working with IPF imagery**
1. Shaping elements of IPF imagery based on research
   - Emphasize deep acceptance and encouragement of attachment behavior
2. Tailoring IPF imagery to suit particular dismissing patient
- Co-created spontaneously in ongoing collaborative interaction

**Characteristics of imagined ideal parent figures**
- Deeply accepting and encouraging of child's attachment behavior
- Welcoming and open to physical and emotional contact
- Never uncomfortable with closeness or distance as desired by the child.

### Over time, the goal is to transform the approach/avoidance conflict regarding attachment needs into a stable, positive representation of parent figure

**Secure Attachment:** The goal is to transform the approach/avoidance conflict regarding attachment needs into a stable, positive representation of parent figures who are deeply accepting and encouraging of the child's attachment needs.

**Imagery Process:** Patients are helped to imagine ideal parents (IPFs) who are attuned and contingently responsive to their emotional states, including underlying vulnerabilities and deeper emotional themes. IPFs stay with the patient during uncomfortable moments instead of turning away.

**Dismissing Patients:** Specific considerations for dismissing patients include:

- Encouraging identification and expression of negative emotions, especially vulnerabilities and deeper feelings.
- Introducing open, flexible, and permissive IPFs to help the patient positively remap their internal working models (IWMs).

**General Considerations for IPF Imagery:**

- Parental presence, consistency, reliability, and interest.
- Protection: IPFs provide protection specifically in the attachment relationship.
- Attunement: IPFs are attuned to physical and emotional proximity-seeking and a wide range of negative emotions.

**IPF Imagery for Dismissing Patients:**

- Protection: IPFs protect the patient in the attachment relationship.
- Attunement: IPFs are especially attuned to negative emotional expressions and welcome physical and emotional closeness.

**Additional Considerations:**

- Repeatedly shape and reshape the imagery to provide optimal discomfort for the patient, neither too little nor too much.
- Introduce corrective IPF imagery that contributes to the development of positive representations and expectancies of others being comfortable with negative emotions.

### Third, with respect to soothing, the therapist suggests scenes in which the child is emotionally upset and the IPFs are consistently responsive to the

**Attachment-Based Therapy for Dismissing Patients**

1. **Soothing:** Therapist suggests scenes where IPFs (ideal parent figures) respond consistently to child's emotional upset with soothing, comfort, and reassurance to develop a stable, positive representation of others as open and normal sources of comfort.
   - Child experiences emotional upset in scene.
   - IPFs tune in and find ways to soothe effectively.

2. **Expressed Delight:** Caregivers of dismissing patients typically lacked expression of delight or selectively expressed it around child's independence. Imagery is used to support the experience of IPFs expressing genuine delight about emotional expressions and desire for closeness.
   - IPFs are delighted when child wants to be close and share feelings.
   - They welcome all feelings, even uncomfortable ones.

3. **Fostering Best Self-Development:** Early childhood caregivers reinforced independent self by selectively attuning to exploratory behavior, contributing to maladaptive belief that self is best apart from relationship. IPF imagery introduces secure base for exploration.
   - Child explores with IPFs as a secure base.
   - They support exploration while providing consistent encouragement and safety.

4. **Individualizing IPF Protocol:** Use adjectives or phrases from AAI (Adult Attachment Interview) to individualize IPF protocol by translating negative descriptions into positive opposites for genuine, not false-positive imagery.
   - Translate unrealistic, idealized parental relationship descriptions into their genuine, positive opposites.
   - Use these positive opposites in IPF imagery to address what was lacking in the early childhood relationships.

5. **Case Illustration:** A dismissing patient's descriptions of his parents were unrealistically idealized and positive. The therapist translated these into genuine positive opposites for use in IPF imagery:
   - Mother: From "loving" to "deeply physically affectionate," from "orderly routines" to "savoring moments together," from "concerned" to "always attuned to your needs," and from "helpful" to "deeply attuned to your needs and feelings."
   - Father: From "around a lot" to "carefully attuned to your needs and feelings," from "plays with me" to "enhancing sense of self," from "strong" to "trusting your longings," from "caring" to "the fun of just being together and not doing anything," and from "learning" to "fostering the joy of discovering and learning."

### Fostering Collaboration in the Dismissing Patient

**Fostering Collaboration in Treating Dismissing Patients**

* Dismissing patients resist engagement on attachment themes due to deactivated attachment system
* Early focus is on engaging cooperative behavioral system for collaboration and teamwork
* Therapist introduces shared goals and mutual understanding of treatment issues
* Foundation: both patient and therapist need to show up emotionally as well as physically
* Self-exploration in therapy occurs within the context of a relationship
* Collaborative agreement and interaction around IPF method reduces resistance
* Address noncollaborative nonverbal behavior:
  * Dismissing patients may sit far away, rarely turn head, frequently avert gaze
  * Therapist uses metacognitive awareness to help patient become aware of nonverbal behaviors
  * Experiential focusing identifies state of mind associated with nonverbal gesture
* Focus on contingently matching and transforming affective facial displays
* Dismissing adults violate Grice's maxim of truthful quality and quantity in discourse about attachment themes
* Therapist calls attention to overly positive, succinct, or lacking verbal communication
* Encourage patient to explore underlying emotions, memories, assumptions, and maladaptive schemas associated with attachment themes.

### Enhancing Metacognitive Skills in the Dismissing Patient

**Metacognitive Skills Development for Dismissing Patients**

* Therapist's consistent reflective stance contributes to metacognitive skills development (Allen, 2001, 2013)
* Specific techniques like affect marking and experiential focusing are effective

**Metacognitive Identification of Internal States**

* Dismissing patients have poor metacognitive identification of internal states
* Use simple anchoring scales to enhance awareness (1-10 scale for degree of awareness)
* Scales can show dismissing patients how little they are aware of their inner state
* Exploration of states of mind becomes a primary treatment goal

**Affect and Underlying Vulnerabilities**

* Dismissing patients resist accessing negative and positive affects, especially in relation to attachment experience
* Encourage patients to look beyond surface-level experience to deeper levels
* Use affect dial technique to promote staying with resisted states
* Patients learn to identify specific emotions and experience them fully
* Experiential focusing helps develop metacognitive identification in general

**Marked Affectivity and Contingent Responsiveness**

* Mentalization-based treatment emphasizes marked affectivity and contingent responsiveness
* Marked affectivity: therapist mirrors patient's affect using a verbal intensifier
* Encourages metacognitive identification of state of mind
* Fosters awareness of contingent responsiveness
* Patients learn to imagine ways they directly affect IPF responses and notice the effect on their inner state

**Metacognitive Awareness of Context**

* Dismissing patients have poor awareness of contingency effects
* Encourage metacognitive awareness of contextual factors (life circumstances, history)
* Patients recognize specific ways early parental rejection affected them
* Develop rueful recognition of negative ways they have become like their parents
* Later in treatment, encourage taking a wider perspective to articulate a central guiding purpose or ultimate concern.

### Stages in the Treatment of the Dismissing Patient

**Stages in the Treatment of the Dismissing Patient**

1. **Introducing Cooperative Behavior:**
   - Therapist introduces concept of cooperative behavior as a way to work on reactivating attachment system indirectly.
   - Use of IPF imagery and addressing nonverbal and verbal misattunements.
   - Encouraging metacognitive awareness during misattunements.

2. **Reactivation of Attachment System:**
   - Longing for relationship connection reappears in successful treatment.
   - Patients value attachment, long for closeness despite defenses.
   - Longing is accompanied by anxiety, sadness, shame, and vulnerability.
   - Movement from avoidant or fearful to secure attachment involves ambivalence.
   - Normalizing interventions to address toxic shame.
   - IPF imagery focuses on accepting, soothing parent figures.

3. **Clear Signs of Attachment System Reactivation:**
   - Nonconflicted reports of proximity-seeking and contact-seeking between child and IPFs.
   - Preference for using IPF imagery.
   - Spontaneous comments about preferring therapist's presence.
   - Emergence of healthy protest behavior at therapist's unavailability.
   - Therapeutic ruptures likely to occur due to deactivated attachment system towards the therapist.
   - Collaborative metacognitive inquiry into patient and therapist states of mind during treatment ruptures.

4. **Middle to Late Stages of Treatment:**
   - Dismissing patient capable of using therapist as a secure base.
   - Focus on exploration of secure intimacy, not early childhood attachment themes.
   - Use of ideal partner imagery to develop stable, positive map for adult secure intimacy.
   - Ideal intimate partners provide safety, responsiveness, secure base, safe haven, contingent responsiveness, comfort, and encouragement of self-development within the context of intimacy.

### Case Illustration

**Case Illustration: Dismissing Attachment Style**

- Patient was a successful businessman in his early 50s with a dismissing attachment style (Ds3)
- Married and divorced after a short-term relationship, never had an intimate gay relationship, only hired escorts for physical contact
- Initially came to treatment for being overweight and developed chronic low self-esteem despite success
- First treatment focused on enhancing self-esteem and metacognitive awareness to manage binge eating
- After a year, patient lost weight and resolved chronic low self-esteem issues
- Terminated treatment after therapist suggested addressing absence of close relationships
- Returned two years later due to weight gain and diabetes diagnosis

**Second Treatment: Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) Imagery**

- Patient reported patterns of emotional eating at the start of second session
- Tried using IPF imagery independently during the week

*Transcript from the second session:*

**Patient:** I tried to use the IPF imagery on my own, but it didn't really work. It felt kind of artificial. [_The patient is expressing a dismissive attitude towards the IPF imagery._]

**Therapist:** (Empathically) I understand that it might feel artificial at first, but as we continue to practice, it can become more natural and effective. [_The therapist acknowledges the patient's feelings and offers reassurance._]

**Patient:** But I don't really see how it applies to me. I mean, I've been successful in my business and my life. I don't need anyone else to make me feel secure. [_The patient is expressing a common dismissive belief that they don't need attachment._]

**Therapist:** (Reflectively) It seems like you have a strong sense of independence and self-sufficiency, which is great. However, even successful people can benefit from feeling securely attached to others. [_The therapist validates the patient's feelings while introducing the importance of attachment._]

**Patient:** I guess that makes sense, but it still feels a bit strange. [_The patient remains hesitant._]

**Therapist:** (Gently) It's normal to feel uncomfortable with new things, especially when they challenge our beliefs or ways of thinking. But remember, we're not asking you to change who you are; we're just exploring the possibility of adding a new tool to your emotional toolbox. [_The therapist reassures the patient and emphasizes the voluntary nature of the exercise._]

**Patient:** I see what you mean. Maybe I can give it another try. [_The patient is open to considering the IPF imagery further._]

### Therapist: When you feel the need to emotionally eat, you use the ideal parent figures to comfort you? But earlier you talked about some discomfort ab

**Therapy Session:**

**Patient's Emotional Eating and Attachment:**

* Patient uses ideal parent figures for emotional comfort during therapy but feels uncomfortable with it.
* Metacognitive awareness of the process: patient is more comfortable utilizing techniques at home, but feels uneasy in the therapeutic relationship.

**Metacognitive Awareness:**

* Patient shows metacognitive awareness of their state of mind and limitations of knowledge.
* Patient experiences metacognitive monitoring of immediate state of mind during therapy session.

**Underlying Vulnerability:**

* Patient feels deeply uncomfortable with being seen or having someone be interested in them, fearing it may not be genuine.
* This fear stems from a negative attachment experience in the past.

**Imagery Exercise:**

* Therapist introduces imagery exercise to help patient imagine ideal parents and the qualities that would make them comforting and non-threatening.
* Patient struggles with generating positive images, focusing on absence of emotion and instrumental tasks instead.

**Positive Parental Responsiveness:**

* Patient spontaneously introduces the idea of positive parental responsiveness during imagery exercise.
* Therapist encourages patient to continue exploring the theme of emotional closeness with ideal parents.

### Therapist: The scene will change, and in the next scene you will learn something more about preferring the closeness without being threatened by it . 

**Session Overview:**

* The patient describes a scene of positive proximity-seeking with responsive Insecurely Attached Figures (IPFs) in the park.
* Therapist introduces the concept of positive expectancy about IPFs' responsiveness.
* Patient shows metacognitive awareness of unresponsiveness of family-of-origin parents and attachment approach/avoidance struggle.
* In a subsequent session, the patient discusses overeating and mindless eating during reading.
* Therapist fosters metacognitive awareness of state of mind and relating variables.
* Patient shows metacognitive awareness of deactivation of attachment and illusion of self-sufficiency.
* Patient expresses dislike for imagined tenderness and support, but wonders if it could be easier to imagine.
* Therapist introduces positive IPF imagery around emotional closeness.

**Positive Proximity-Seeking:**

* Scene in the park with responsive IPFs.
* Patient seeks closeness without feeling threatened.
* Quality of IPFs' responsiveness helps patient want closeness.
* Patient collaboratively generates positive imagery to counteract rejection experiences.

**Metacognitive Awareness:**

* Patient shows awareness of unresponsiveness of family-of-origin parents and attachment approach/avoidance struggle.
* Therapist fosters metacognitive awareness of state of mind and relating variables.
* Patient makes a connection between lingering and filling the void of attachment needs.
* Patient discovers a connection between binge eating and longing for connection.

**Imagery and Emotional Closeness:**

* Therapist introduces positive IPF imagery around emotional closeness.
* Patient initially resists the idea, but wonders if it could be easier to imagine in certain conditions.

### Therapist: Let’s see what the conditions might be under which you could allow this? So go ahead and imagine just the right conditions under which you 

**Treating a Patient with Dismissing Attachment:**

**Persistent Active Engagement:** The therapist takes an active stance towards the patient's protests and attempts to deactivate attachment behavior, using imagery of ideal parent figures (IPFs) as the main approach.

**Imagery of Ideal Parent Figures:** The therapist asks the patient to imagine the conditions under which they could allow themselves to feel a supportive, tender, loving connection with IPFs. This helps the patient discover that positive remapping of childhood IPF attachment works better than imagery of physical and emotional closeness in current relationships.

**Metacognitive Identification:** The therapist fosters metacognitive identification in the patient of their discomfort with attachment closeness, helping them understand their limitations and emotions.

**Observable Changes:** In just two sessions, the patient shows recognition and experience that attachment longings are not a source of threat and shame but can be a source of security and satisfaction. They begin developing a new, positive internal working model (IWM) of attachment, including comfort with physical closeness, being deeply seen and known, soft emotions, and spontaneous exploration in the context of secure attachment.

**Early Signs of Normalization:** The patient summarizes these positive changes as "lingering with the blissfulness of being together" and shows early signs of shifting towards finding this blissful lingering in the context of an attachment relationship instead of with eating behaviors.

**Main Features of Treating a Patient with Dismissing Attachment:**

- Persistent active engagement from therapist
- Imagery of ideal parent figures who are consistently responsive to the patient-as-child
- Fostering metacognitive identification in the patient
- Developing a new, positive IWM of attachment
- Early signs of normalization of attachment longings.

### CHAPTER 12 Treating Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment

**Chapter 12: Treating Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment**

**Origins of Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment**

* Developed from various forms of maternal unresponsiveness (Ainsworth et al., 1978; Belsky, 1999b; Mikulincer et al., 2003; Shaver & Mikulincer, 2009)
* Mothers were inconsistent and unpredictable in responding to infants' signals of distress
* Poor timing of responsiveness and partial attention due to multitasking
* Inconsistent responsiveness contributed to anxious preoccupation
* Selective misattunement to infant's exploratory initiatives and expressions of exuberance
* Anxious-preoccupied mothers often anxious in caregiving context and spilled their anxieties onto infants

**The Therapeutic Stance with the Anxious-Preoccupied Patient**

* Focus on patient's own state of mind and self-experience (inside-out orientation)
* Reverse patient's outside-in orientation by collaborating to focus on internal states
* Consistently attuned and responsive to moment-by-moment shifting affective states (vitality affects)
* Fully present, moment-to-moment with the patient
* Limited therapist self-disclosure to avoid reactivating hyperactivating style within transference
* Self-disclosure should orient patient towards own state of mind during ruptures in therapeutic alliance.

### What’s it like for you now, hearing what I just said? Third, since research suggests that caregivers of children who have become anxious-preoccupied a

**Working with Anxious-Preoccupied Patients**

1. **Therapeutic Stance:**
   * Therapist does not selectively attune to patient's fear states or self-disclose own concerns.
   * Becomes a consistently calming presence, providing protection, reassurance, and metacognitive support.
   * Focuses on closeness–distance regulation using protective bubble imagery.
2. **Protective Bubble:**
   * Patient imagines a clear, safe, protective bubble.
   * Adjusts the size and position of the bubble to regulate closeness and distance.
   * Helps patients learn voluntary control over closeness–distance regulation and emotions.
3. **Exploratory Behavior:**
   * Therapist selectively attunes to patient's exploratory behavior and self-development.
   * Mirrors and encourages self-observations and expressions of interest.
   * Introduces direct inquiry to prompt and reinforce self-exploration.
4. **Working with Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) Imagery:**
   * Anxious-preoccupied patients find it easier to use IPF imagery due to dissatisfactions with family-of-origin parents.
   * Introduction of IPF imagery may invoke threat, loyalty issues, and abandonment fear.
   * Therapist explains that imagination creates new possibilities while acknowledging the importance of actual parents.
   * Encourages patients to add positive qualities from their actual parents and use free imagination for ideal parent figures.
5. **IPF Imagery Considerations:**
   * Emphasize pervasive attunement and accurate contingent responsiveness in IPFs.
   * Tailor IPF imagery to the particular preoccupied patient, avoiding canned scripts.
   * Cultivate spontaneity in patients' lives through collaborative creation of IPF imagery.

### The creative process of the imagery allows for the possibility of very careful attunement each moment, in contrast to the pervasive misattunement to w

**Imagery Therapy for Anxious-Preoccupied Patients**

* The imagery process allows for attunement between patient (as child) and ideal parent figures (IPFs), contrasting with past misattunements.
* IPFs are attuned to the child's states, initiatives, and emotional expressions, providing recognition, acceptance, and appropriate responses.
* IPFs address over-involvement by being fully present and focused on the child.
* Consistent attunement reduces hypervigilance towards IPFs' states and experiences.

**Addressing Anxious Hyperarousal**

* Ideal parent figures are consistently attuned to and soothe the child's general anxiety and specific shifts towards high anxious arousal.
* Patients experience relief from burden of caretaking or controlling IPFs' responses.

**Addressing Hyperactivation of Attachment Behavior**

* Ideal parent figures accept and comfort the child when fearing disconnection or abandonment.
* Patients learn to feel secure in their connection with IPFs, reducing excessive attachment behavior.

**Interventions for Rapidly Escalating Abandonment Threat**

* Therapist suggests focusing on fear of abandonment and feeling more distressed to experience IPFs' attunement and effective responses.
* Patients describe how IPFs help them feel better, reinforcing inside-out orientation.

**Minimizing Self-Reference**

* Therapist avoids self-reference during sessions to reduce outside-in orientation and reinforce inside-out orientation.
* Validation and reinforcement of patient's inner states through affective marking.

**Separation and Reunion Episodes**

* Patients imagine brief separations from IPFs followed by reunions with appropriate responses during reunion episodes.
* Patients freely describe their experiences to enhance inside-out orientation.

### Working with IPF imagery in these specific ways will over time further reduce hyperactivated attachment behavior and promote attachment security and t

**IPF Imagery for Anxious-Preoccupied Patients**

IPF imagery is used to reduce hyperactivated attachment behavior and promote attachment security in anxious-preoccupied patients. This type of therapy emphasizes the importance of parent figures who are:

* Protective and provide a safe haven
* Carefully attuned to the patient's behaviors, inner states, and developmental capacities
* Soothing, comforting, and reassuring
* Openly express delight and pleasure regarding the patient's exploratory behavior
* Comfortable with and supportive of the child's initiatives of exploration and discovery

Anxious-preoccupied patients are characterized by:

* Fearfulness and anxiety disorders
* Childhood ambivalent/resistant attachment with significant caregiver misattunement
* Inhibited self-development

IPF imagery is tailored to address these specific needs:

**Protection and Safety:**

* Introduce protective parent figures to promote positive remapping of attachment figures as a source of safety and protection.

**Attunement:**

* Shape IPF imagery to provide experiences of careful attunement, encouraging healthy exploratory behavior and the development of positive expectancies that others can accurately understand the patient's ongoing experience.

**Soothing and Comfort:**

* Emphasize soothing qualities to help patients develop a stable representation and set of expectancies that others can be openly comforting and demonstrate that hyperactivated attachment behaviors are unnecessary.

**Expressed Delight:**

* Encourage parent figures to openly express delight and pleasure regarding the patient's exploratory behavior to address the missing experience in anxious-preoccupied patients.

**Fostering Best Self-Development:**

* Emphasize parent figures who are entirely comfortable with, deeply supportive of, and strongly confident about the child's initiatives of exploration and discovery to enable the developing child to find courage and tolerate fear and uncertainty in the face of new discoveries.

IPF imagery is tailored based on the patient's attachment interview or clinical interview to address their specific needs and experiences.

### Fostering Collaboration in Anxious-Preoccupied Patients

**Fostering Collaboration in Anxious-Preoccupied Patients**

* Anxious-preoccupied patients easily self-disclose, but it's not always a sign of collaboration
* Excessive self-disclosure is often an attempt to please and represents outside-in orientation
* Therapist should acknowledge disclosure and redirect focus to patient's experience

**Patient Interaction**

* Patient expresses worries and fears, feeling overwhelmed and exhausted
* Therapist acknowledges concerns and encourages exploration of feelings
* Redirects focus from external issues to internal experiences

**Nonverbal Behavior**

* Preoccupied patients cry easily, not always a positive sign of emotional release
* Frequent crying may indicate hyperactivation of attachment system
* Therapist should address underlying abandonment fears directly

**Nonverbal Attunement**

* Patient's nonverbal behavior is attuned to therapist but one-sided
* Therapist must transform patient's one-sided, outside-in nonverbal attunement into collaborative dialogue
* Develop experience of being attuned to nonverbally by the therapist

**Verbal Behavior**

* Anxious-preoccupied patients are verbally noncollaborative
* Talk on and on without pauses, fail to provide turn-taking opportunities
* Fail to meet basic rules of verbal coherence
* Therapist should highlight patient's failure to collaborate verbally
* Encourage conscious monitoring of speech and framing discourse in succinct meaningful units
* Use nonverbal indicators to signal when to bring discourse to a close
* Help patient become metacognitively aware of wandering off topic, irrelevancies, passive speech, and jargon
* Engage patient's cooperativeness to reexpress essential points clearly.

### Enhancing Metacognitive Skills in Anxious-Preoccupied Patients

**Enhancing Metacognitive Skills in Anxious-Preoccupied Patients**

* Anxious-preoccupied patients have low reflective capacity, requiring a therapeutic approach that enhances general reflective capacity and targets specific metacognitive skill deficiencies.

**Therapist's Role:**

* Maintain consistent mentalizing stance to offset patient's hypervigilance.
* Help patient identify their own affect experiences and realize they originate from within.
* Bring metacognitive focus to the relativity of all states during times of fear of abandonment.
* Assist in determining effective action strategies for responding to emotional states.
* Teach impulse control through anticipating outcomes, developing action plans, and organizing behaviors.

**Metacognitive Skills Development:**

* Develop postformal metacognitive skills to modify maladaptive schemas of emotional deprivation and abandonment.
* Use techniques from schema therapy to bring underlying assumptions into awareness.
* Recognize and address low coherence or disorganization in patient's discourse.

**Metacognitive Awareness Enhancement:**

* Use anchoring scales to improve metacognitive awareness of organization or coherence of mind.
* Consistently ask patients about their relative degree of disorganization or organization at each session.
* Gradually help patients develop an overall present orientation by fostering metacognitive awareness of past and present orientation.
* Use anchoring scales to improve metacognitive awareness of self/other focus.
* Consistently ask patients about their relative degree of self-focus or other-focus at each session.
* Gradually help patients develop the capacity for collaborative exchange and exploration by enhancing self-awareness and reducing preoccupation with others' states of mind.

### Stages in the Treatment of the Anxious-Preoccupied Patient

**Stages in the Treatment of the Anxious-Preoccupied Patient**

1. **Initially:**
   - Introduce cooperative behavior to temper attachment system hyperactivation and reactivate exploratory behavior.
   - Use Interpersonal Process Recall (IPF) imagery with noninvolving, accurately attuned parent figures.
   - Emphasize soothing and reassurance to reduce overall anxious arousal and temper heightened arousal during abandonment fear episodes.

2. **Middle Stage:**
   - Focus on repairing ruptures in the therapeutic relationship caused by empathic failures or heightened abandonment fear.
   - Collaborate with the patient to understand their state of mind during hyperactivated attachment behavior episodes.
   - Encourage self-exploration and trust within the attachment relationship.

3. **Middle to Late Stages:**
   - Enhance self-development and self-esteem, focusing on a healthy exploratory system for mature growth.
   - Serve as a secure base for self-exploration.
   - Signs of progress: reduced hyperactivation, diminished preoccupation with past hurts, here-and-now focus, inside-out orientation, and reactivated exploratory system.

4. **Later Stages:**
   - Develop a positive Internal Working Model (IWM) for secure intimacy.
   - Use ideal partner imagery to emphasize noninvolving, accurately attuned partners who provide a secure base and encourage self-development within the context of intimacy.
   - Objective: create a stable, positive map for adult secure intimacy.

### Case Illustration

**Three Pillars Treatment Approach**

- Case illustration of a woman named Helen with predominantly anxious-preoccupied attachment pattern
- Diagnosed with various psychiatric disorders: bipolar II disorder, OCD, borderline personality disorder, panic disorder, and dissociative disorder not otherwise specified
- Seven overdoses between ages 16 to 30, none suicidal but for relief from agitation
- Insecure attachment classification of CC/E3/Ds1 from Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) at beginning of treatment
  * Mixed attachment strategy with anxious-preoccupied pattern as predominant
  * Some dismissing attachment features
  * Coherence of pre-treatment AAI transcript was 2.0
- After three years of Three Pillars method, Helen's attachment status changed to secure (F5)
  * Post-treatment AAI coherence was 8.0 out of 9
  * Reflective functioning improved from a 2 at pre-treatment to a 5 at follow-up

**Helen's Background**

- Only child with both parents working, often left in care of babysitters
- Preoccupied attachment themes throughout development
- Early memory of sitting with father during mother's hospitalization
- Extreme fear when left alone or without parental presence
- Difficulty connecting with babysitters and feeling separate from her mother
- Mother's death and Helen's broken wrist went unnoticed for days
- Mother's emotional expression discouraged, father unemotional but practical
- Passive not-knowingness about family history and secrets

**Adolescence and Young Adulthood**

- Undiagnosed bipolar disorder causing erratic affective surges
- Passivity limited ability to describe experiences effectively
- Increasingly agitated in interpersonal situations
- Began taking over-the-counter medications, hid behavior from parents
- Sent to reputed hospital for overdosing behavior
- Frightening experiences with other patients and dissociative responses
- Confusing and shameful reactions to warm interactions with nurses
- Ends of therapy sessions evoked feelings of being "alone and unprotected"

**Three Pillars Treatment Approach**

- Goal: Secure attachment and improve reflective functioning
- Three pillars:
  * Building a secure therapeutic relationship
  * Understanding the patient's attachment history and its impact on current relationships
  * Developing new ways of thinking about self and others
- Helen's treatment included:
  * Consistent, empathic, and attuned therapeutic relationship
  * Exploration of her attachment history and its influence on her experiences
  * Development of skills to regulate emotions and improve reflective functioning.

### Dialectical behavioral therapy (DBT) treatment was sought by the patient and her family because of her extreme difficulty regulating her emotions and 

**Dialectical Behavioral Therapy (DBT) and Helen's Struggles**

* Patient, Helen, sought DBT treatment due to emotional regulation difficulties and interpersonal communication issues
* Impressive initial improvement but felt invalidated by therapist's skill-focused approach
* Preoccupied attachment patterns emerged: fixation on therapist's personal details, loss of internal focus

**DBT Therapy Challenges**

* Suicidal ideation questioning set up outside-in orientation, worsening Helen's thoughts about suicide
* Self-disclosure by therapist to provide reassurance and rebuild alliance hyperactivated anxious-preoccupied attachment pattern
* Frustration and stuckness for both parties; attachment-focused consultation recommended

**Attachment-Focused Therapy**

* Transcripts from treatment with attachment-focused therapist
* Preoccupied attachment characteristics:
  * Reactive, sensitive, moody, sad, and distant (mother figure)
  * Serious, hardworking, demanding, protective, and playful (father figure)
* Ideal parent figures:
  * Mother figure: highly responsive, sturdy, consistent, strong, carefully attuned
  * Father figure: never angry, present, deeply patient, seeing how special she is (kept protective and playful)

**Middle State of Treatment Transcript**

* Helen discussing preparation for professional competency exam
* Therapist's words and patient's words in plain text
* Posthoc observations and explanations bracketed and italicized

**Dream Interpretation Session**

* Patient (Helen) had a dream where she noticed her therapist reacting to what she was saying during an interview, but she wasn't sure what to make of it.
* Therapist encouraged Helen to explore the themes in her dream and open herself up to feelings and emotions from the memory of the interview.

**Text Summary:**

* Therapist acknowledges patient's improvement and encourages continued progress.
* They discuss using ideal parent figures in therapy session for emotional support.
* Patient has difficulty focusing during the exercise but eventually imagines being with her ideal parents in a hospital setting, feeling calmer and reassured.

* Patient reports increased feelings of safety and care from her ideal parents' presence.

**Key Concepts:**

* Metacognitive mastery: the ability to recognize and understand one's own thoughts, emotions, and mental states.
* Ideal parent figures: a therapeutic technique used in Dialectical Behavior Therapy (DBT) to help patients develop emotional regulation skills by imagining supportive parents.
* Guided imagery: a mindfulness practice that involves using the imagination to create mental images to promote relaxation and reduce stress or anxiety.
