**Imaginal Exercise:** Therapist guides patient through an imaginal exercise to create a scene of being with ideal parents for emotional security and well-being.

* Patient experiences difficulty imagining the scene due to self-consciousness.
* Therapist explores the root cause of self-consciousness and addresses it using the ideal parent protocol.
* Patient imagines ideal parents sitting next to him/her on the bed, providing calming presence and deep reassurance.
* Therapist engages patient's body experience to support deep integration of positive feelings.
* Patient feels deeply relieved and relaxed, able to keep good feelings with them as the scene dissolves away.

**Key Concepts:**

* **Imaginal Exercise:** A therapeutic technique where patients use their imagination to create a scene that fosters emotional security and well-being.
* **Ideal Parent Figure (IPF):** A mental representation of ideal parents who provide unconditional love, acceptance, and support.
* **Self-consciousness:** Feeling uncomfortable or anxious about one's own actions or appearance in the presence of others.
* **Collaborative Exploration:** A therapeutic approach where therapist and patient work together to understand and address underlying issues.

### As was noted in the introduction to this case, the Three Pillars treatment led to an increase of Helen’s coherence on the AAI transcript from a 2 to a

**Three Pillars Treatment Outcome for Helen**

- *Introduction:* Helen's coherence on AAI transcript improved from 2 to 8, and her attachment status shifted from disorganized (CC/E3/Ds1) to earned secure with some anxious-preoccupied features (F5).

**Manifesations of Insecure Strategies**

- Anxious-preoccupied features may resurface periodically at the outer ranges of secure attachment spectrum, especially at F1 and F5.
- More likely to occur during relationally intimate experiences, including psychotherapy interactions.
- Helen continued to exhibit some anxious-preoccupied features but significantly less so than during early treatment period.

**Factors Affecting Anxious-Preoccupied Patterns**

- Comorbid bipolar spectrum disorder made Helen vulnerable to high stress levels and disrupted sleep.
- In the face of challenges, she was susceptible to mood instability and reappearance of anxious-preoccupied patterns.

**Benefits of Secure Attachment**

- Enhanced coherence of mind and metacognitive capacities allowed Helen to recognize and regulate anxious-preoccupied states.
- Greater internal security enabled her to tolerate processing traumatic material from past.
- Improved ability to experience felt sense of self (interoception) was stabilizing and organizing for Helen.

**Importance of Interoception in BPD Treatment**

- Patients with borderline personality disorder (BPD) often lack interoceptive abilities, focusing on reading others' feelings instead.
- New psychotherapies for BPD should aim to increase patients' interoception ability.
- Three Pillars model integrates interoceptive development into therapy process.

**Treatment Considerations for Disorganized Attachment with Anxious-Preoccupied and Dismissing Strategies**

- Focus of next chapter.

### CHAPTER 13 Treating Disorganized or Fearful Attachment

**Chapter 13: Treating Disorganized or Fearful Attachment**

**Origins of Disorganized or Fearful Attachment**

* Main and Solomon (1986) identified a fourth prototype of infant attachment called disorganized, characterized by contradictory attachment behaviors.
* Children with disorganized attachment lack a single, unified Internal Working Model (IWM) for attachment.
* Grossmann, Grossmann, and Waters (2005) differentiated the disorganized category into three subtypes: contradictory behaviors, goal-oriented behaviors lacking, and direct disorganization.
* Disorganized attachment may result from prolonged or repeated separations from caregivers (Chisholm, 1998; Solomon and George, 1999), maternal depression and alcoholism (Main and Solomon, 1990), frightening and frightened parental behavior (Main and Hesse, 1990), or unresolved states of mind in parents regarding past trauma, abuse, or loss (George and Solomon, 1996).
* Maternal dissociated behavior is a stronger predictor of infant disorganized attachment than frightening/frightened parental behavior (Schuengel et al., 1999).
* Unresolved states in parents can lead to pervasive misattunement, disruptive affective communication, and child maltreatment (Lyons-Ruth et al., 1999).
* Disorganized attachment occurs in seemingly normal families with little distinguishing mothers from those of secure, avoidant, or ambivalent children (Solomon and George, 1999).

**Characteristics of Children with Disorganized Attachment**

* Show multiple, contradictory attachment behaviors – both deactivating and hyperactivating.
* Have more dissociative experiences and behaviors than other children.
* Are often in states of unmitigated high-fear arousal.
* Show significantly greater negative emotional arousal than children with other attachment prototypes (Spangler and Grossmann, 1993).
* Have a poor capacity for affect regulation, contributing to chronic negative affect states.
* Display inhibited exploratory behavior and markedly impaired self-development (Solomon and George, 1999).

### The Therapeutic Stance With the Disorganized Patient

**Therapeutic Stance with Disorganized Patients:**

* Important for therapist to be a safe haven and not exacerbate fear
* Calming, soothing, reassuring presence
* Actively engaging but not involving in therapist's state of mind
* Comforting, fearless, confident, accepting, welcoming, determined, proactive
* Fully present, consistent, steady, predictable
* Consistently attuned to and nonverbally matching patient's affects
* Encourage and reinforce positive emotional expressions
* Careful not to interrupt patient's attachment displays

**Working with Ideal Parent Figures (IPF) Imagery:**

* Majority of disorganized patients have personality and/or dissociative disorders
* Rapid and marked shifting of states of mind and behaviors
* Each shifting behavior and state a spontaneous expression of attachment need
* Facilitate patient's experience of separate and unique set of IPFs for each shift
* DID patient may switch to fearful child state, imagine parents responsive to fear
* DID patient may switch to dismissing state, imagine engaging and responsive parents
* DID patient may switch to hostile-aggressive state, imagine parents who safely contain aggression
* For each significant behavioral or state shift, suggest new set of IPFs
* Patient's overall experience approximates continuous IPF responsiveness.

### Therapists of disorganized patients benefit from understanding the different forms of dissociation that such patients might manifest. Van der Hart, Ni

**Understanding Dissociation and Structural Dissociation**

- Van der Hart et al. (2004) argue for one type of dissociation: structural dissociation, which involves rigid compartmentalization of dissociative parts.
- Traditional treatment practices, such as calling forth and sending back alter personalities, reinforce this compartmentalization.
- Instead, therapists should ask alters to stay present after communication, allowing for multiple alters to be present during a session.

**Structuralizing Technique with IPFs**

- Originally developed for trauma processing with Multiple Personality Disorder/Dissociative Identity Disorder (MPD/DID) patients.
- Adapted for working with disorganized patients on attachment-related themes.
- After working with one alter state, therapist invites other parts to come forward while the current part remains present.
- When a patient experiences a spontaneous structural dissociative shift, the therapist invites the previous part to remain present with ideal parents while the new part engages with them.

**IPF Imagery and Disorganized Attachment**

- Disorganized patients exhibit both deactivating and hyperactivating attachment strategies.
- IPFs provide a deep sense of security while being attuned to patient's fear and mistrust.
- Encourage both attachment behavior and exploratory behavior in the same interactive sequence.
- Address chronic-heightened fear arousal by shaping IPFs that are carefully attuned to the child's fear.
- Be fiercely protective but not intrusively protective of the patient-as-child.
- Stimulate the patient-as-child's full range of emotional arousal in intersubjective collaboration.

### Seventh, since many disorganized patients have learned to expect others to be dissociative and not present, IPFs are shaped to be fully present, consi

**Interpersonal Repair Facilitators (IPFs) for Disorganized Patients:**

**1. Fully Present and Attuned:**
- IPFs are fully present and attentively engaged with the patient-as-child.
- They provide a sense of reassurance through their consistent presence.

**2. Consistently Responsive:**
- IPFs respond accurately to the communications of the patient-as-child.
- They help patients feel seen, supported, and safe.

**3. Effective Limiting and Containment:**
- IPFs set appropriate limits during states of rage or hostile aggression.
- They help patients express intense emotions in safe ways.

**4. Consistency and Predictability:**
- IPFs provide a steady, reliable presence.
- Their consistent behavior helps disorganized patients regulate themselves.

**5. Provide Appropriate Structure:**
- IPFs offer just enough structure to help disorganized patients feel clear and coherent.

**Supporting Secure Attachment:**

**Protective IPFs:**
- Offer an approachable safe haven without fear arousal.
- Provide calmness and serenity.

**Attuned IPFs:**
- Consistently recognize and respond to the full range of negative and positive emotional states.
- Support intersubjective reciprocal exchange of vitality affects and categorical emotional states.

**Protection:**
- IPFs provide a safe haven, reducing fear arousal.
- They help patients feel protected from harm.

**Attunement:**
- IPFs respond accurately to the patient's emotional states.
- They support intersubjective understanding and emotional connection.

**Soothing:**
- IPFs help patients regulate their emotions and reduce distress.
- They provide comfort and reassurance during times of need.

**Expressed Delight:**
- IPFs express delight in the patient's positive qualities and achievements.
- They foster self-esteem and a sense of worth.

**Fostering Best Self-Development:**
- IPFs encourage patients to develop their best selves.
- They help patients build resilience and coping skills.

### Thereby, the disorganized patient-as-child learns to trust his or her affects and to become effective in expressing them in the context of the imagine

**Attachment-Focused Therapy (AFT) with Disorganized Patients:**

**Background:**

* AFT helps disorganized patients develop trust in affects and effective expression through imagined attachment relationship with IPFs.
* IPFs respond contingently to patient's attachment behaviors, supporting reactivation and balance of the attachment behavioral system.

**IPF Responsibilities:**

* Support proximity-and contact-seeking while ensuring safety.
* Soothe high-fear arousal through appropriate responses.
* Encourage self-development at every stage.
* Individualize IPF protocols based on five adjectives given in AAI.

**IPF Interaction with Disorganized Patients:**

* Respond effectively to problematic attachment behaviors, enabling healthy behavior activation.
* Help patients express positive emotions and prosocial emotions.
* Encourage self-development for a coherent, unitary sense of self.

**Example Interaction:**

* Patient expresses fear and desire for proximity.
* IPF responds by acknowledging feelings and encouraging safe expression.

**IPF Response to Adjectives in AAI:**

* Caring: Memories of nurturing actions.
* Nurturing: Specific examples of care shown.
* Torturous: Memories of traumatic experiences.
* Angry: Instances of yelling or conflict.
* Moody: Recollections of unpredictable behavior.

**IPF Interaction with Disorganized Patients' Family-of-Origin Parents:**

* Both a source of attachment need and support, and fear and threat.
* IPFs respond based on adjectives given in AAI to understand patient's experiences.

### The therapist translated the five adjectives for each parent into their positive opposites, which were then given to the ideal parent figures in the i

**Imaginal Reprepresentation (IPR) Therapy for Disorganized Patients**

* IPR therapy involves creating ideal parent figures based on positive opposites of family-of-origin parents.
* Ideal mother figure: deeply caring, nurturing, calm, soft-spoken, and steady.
* Ideal father figure: physically affectionate, serene, gentle, empowering, and protective.
* Therapist draws patient's attention to the effect of ideal parent figures on inner state.
* Consistent recognition of positive experiences contributes to organization of mind.

**Fostering Collaboration with Disorganized Patients**

* Clear, collaborative treatment-frame agreement necessary due to higher risk of treatment-frame violations.
* Nonverbal communication between disorganized patients and their infants shows significant differences and mismatches by four months.
* Therapist recognizes, acknowledges, and addresses negative-affective displays early in treatment.
* Collaborative help for patient to bring metacognitive awareness to nonverbal behaviors.
* Methods for collaboratively promoting communication described in relevant chapters.

**Enhancing Metacognitive Skills in Disorganized Patients**

* Reflective capacity or mentalizing skill is very low in disorganized patients.
* Importance of increasing patient's range of metacognitive skills.
* Disorganized patients show low coherence of mind and frequent state shifts.
* Use of disorganization/organization anchor scale to identify and master states.
* Developing specific metacognitive skills, such as recognizing current orientation and taking a wider perspective.

### Stages in the Treatment of the Disorganized Patient

**Stages in the Treatment of the Disorganized Patient**

1. **Collaborative Negotiation of a Mutually Agreed Upon Treatment Frame:**
   - Begin treatment with clear understanding of mutual expectations, responsibilities, and ground rules.
   - Address disruptions caused by noncollaborative behavior of disorganized patients.

2. **Establishing Attachment and Reducing Fear:**
   - Work to attenuate patient's fearfulness using ideal-parent-figure (IPF) work.
   - Introduce IPFs who provide safety without fear to fearful parts or self-states.
   - Normalize longing for attachment and reassure patient that it is okay to desire closeness.

3. **Addressing Dismissing Parts or Behaviors:**
   - Utilize IPF guidelines to engage and respond contingently to deactivated attachment systems.
   - Address shame, distress, and acting-out impulses with compassionate IPFs.

4. **Engaging Outlier Parts or Self-States:**
   - Use cooperative behavioral system when strong resistance is encountered from outlier parts.
   - Help patient form positive relationships between outlier parts and IPFs.

5. **Bringing All Parts Together:**
   - Call forth parts that need to feel securely attached, leaving each previous part present.
   - Decrease dissociation and foster unified sense of self around a central core self.

6. **Activating Exploratory Behavioral System:**
   - Use IPF imagery for patient to experience parent figures who encourage self-development.
   - Support healthy exploration with a secure base.

7. **Cultivating Postformal Metacognitive Skills and Developing Secure Adult Intimacy:**
   - Foster metacognitive awareness of mind organization, self/other orientation, past/present orientation, and child/adult orientation.
   - Encourage patient to articulate a clear, coherent life narrative.
   - Develop secure adult intimacy in the last phase of treatment.

### Case Illustration

**Case Illustration:** A patient with PTSD, major depression, and dissociative identity disorder (DID) underwent various treatment methods due to her complex trauma profile and childhood sexual abuse experiences.

**Background:** The patient's parents had significant issues – her mother was overly focused on appearance, and her father was an alcoholic minister who sexually abused her throughout her childhood and adolescence. She participated in an incest survivor group and individual phase-oriented trauma treatment to process the abuse memories and emotions.

**Treatment:** Initially, she responded well but later became increasingly dissociated, leading to a diagnosis of DID. The treatment was changed to attachment-based methods focusing on introducing Internal Parent Figures (IPFs) for safety and protection.

**First Phase:** IPFs were introduced to provide safety and connection, with the patient reporting a Wise Man as an initial IPF. She also reported needing IPFs who could stay with her during dissociative episodes.

**Second Phase:** As dissociation worsened, the therapist suggested IPFs for child parts, such as Whoopi Goldberg and the Dalai Lama, to address their fears and lack of understanding about secure attachment relationships.

**Session Transcript:** The patient switched between paranoid alters and vulnerable child parts during a therapy session. She felt alone, scared, and unable to distinguish past and current fears. The therapist acknowledged her feelings of vulnerability and fear and introduced IPF imagery around the theme of safety without fear.

**Patient's Response:** The patient initially resisted the idea of growing up in an ideal family but eventually began to imagine a secure connection with ideal parents, which helped alleviate her sense of deep aloneness and vulnerability.

**Conclusion:** The attachment-based treatment focused on introducing IPFs to provide safety, protection, and security for each part of the patient's dissociated identity. This approach helped address her feelings of fear, vulnerability, and deep aloneness.

* Allows patients to question and learn at their own pace

### PART V TREATMENT GUIDE AND EXPECTED OUTCOMES CHAPTER 14 A Step-by-Step Treatment Guide

**Part V: Treatment Guide and Expected Outcomes**

**Chapter 14: A Step-by-Step Treatment Guide**

**Assessment**
- Identify the root cause of the issue (physical, emotional, or a combination)
- Evaluate the severity and duration of the problem
- Consider any underlying health conditions or risk factors

**Intervention**
- **Cognitive Behavioral Therapy (CBT):** Teaches individuals to identify and challenge negative thought patterns
  * Helps improve mood, reduce anxiety, and enhance coping skills
- **Exposure Therapy:** Gradual exposure to feared stimuli to help reduce fear and anxiety
  * Effective for phobias, PTSD, and OCD
- **Medication:** Prescribed to alleviate symptoms of mental health conditions or physical ailments
  * Antidepressants, antipsychotics, mood stabilizers, etc.

**Support Systems**
- Building a strong support network is crucial for recovery
- Family and friends can provide emotional support and encouragement
- Joining a support group allows individuals to connect with others going through similar experiences

**Self-Care**
- Regular exercise: Improves mood, reduces stress, and boosts energy levels
- Healthy diet: Provides essential nutrients for optimal brain function
- Adequate sleep: Restores energy and enhances cognitive abilities
- Mindfulness practices: Helps reduce stress and improve focus

**Therapy Sessions**
- Establishing a therapeutic relationship with a mental health professional is essential
- Regular sessions help individuals develop coping skills, gain insight into their condition, and work through challenges
- Effective therapy requires active participation and commitment from both the therapist and client

**Follow-Up Care**
- Continuous monitoring and evaluation of progress is necessary
- Adjusting treatment plans as needed to ensure optimal outcomes
- Regular check-ins with mental health professionals help maintain progress and address any new challenges that arise

**Relapse Prevention**
- Identifying triggers and developing strategies to manage them is crucial for maintaining recovery
- Building a strong support network can help prevent relapses
- Engaging in self-care practices, such as regular exercise and healthy eating, can also contribute to long-term success.

### Chapters 8, 9, and 10 describe in detail each of the three pillars of our treatment model, and Chapters 11, 12, and 13 present the specific considerat

**Three Pillars of Treatment Model: Overview**

- Chapters 8-10 describe the three pillars of the treatment model: relational disturbance determination, assessment of attachment problem, and engagement in collaborative dialogue (Source: Chapters 8-10)

**Relational Disturbance Determination**

- Identify nature of presenting problem: attachment, core conflictual relationship theme (CCRT), or trauma-bonding issue (Source: Chapter 8)
- Assess the degree of interference with relationships caused by mild to moderate anxious or dismissing attachment patterns (Source: Chapter 7)
- Determine primary disturbance based on developmental order and severity (Source: Chapter 8)

**Assessment of Attachment Problem**

- Use a variety of measures, including Adult Attachment Interview (AAI), Metacognition Assessment Scale (MAS), and Experience of Close Relationships (ECR) questionnaire (Source: Chapters 3, 9, and 10)
- Determine attachment type and subtypes using AAI (secure, dismissing, preoccupied, disorganized) (Source: Chapter 10)
- Identify early childhood relationship patterns with each parent using AAI (Source: Chapter 10)
- Assess coherence of discourse and mind using AAI (Source: Chapter 10)
- Determine pre-treatment reflective functioning level using Reflective Functioning Scale (RF-S) (Source: Chapter 10)
- Identify condition-specific metacognitive deficits using MAS (Source: Chapter 10)

**Engagement in Collaborative Dialogue**

- Establish and prioritize shared treatment goals and a specific treatment plan (Source: Chapter 10)
- Use transparency model of treatment planning (Source: Chapter 7)
- Introduce Three Pillars approach and its rationale and methods (Source: Chapters 7 and 10)
- Provide psychoeducation about attachment using sample scripts for general information and specific insecure attachment subtypes (Source: Chapter 10)
- Introduce Ideal Parent Figure component of treatment during discussion of specific insecure attachment subtype (Source: Chapter 10)

### Psychoeducation About Attachment

**Attachment**

- Innate drive for healthy attachment relationship with parents or caregivers
- Secure attachment: child feels protected, safe, soothed, supported in exploration and independence
- Forms of insecure attachment:
  - Anxious: high anxiety, clingy, worried about parents' availability, limited learning and development
  - Avoidant: keeps emotional and physical distance, excessively explores independently, tends to be less emotionally attuned caregivers
  - Disorganized: combination of anxious and avoidant behaviors
- Internal working models or maps of attachment formed early in life (mostly during second year)
- Affect experiences with self and others throughout lifetime

**Dismissing Attachment**

- Parents did not fully meet attachment needs
- Child experienced parents as rejecting or dismissive of attachment needs
- Caregivers may have had rigid ideas about parenting, unseen, controlled, and rejected child's unique self
- Effects: dismisses own attachment needs, strong sense of self, independent, challenging personal relationships, deep sense of connection longing but rarely allowed to experience it due to shame or discomfort
- Goal: develop new, positive internal working model of relationships through imagining ideal parent figures who actively engage, accept, and encourage emotional needs.

### Psychoeducation About Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment

**Anxious-Preoccupied Attachment:**

- Result of unmet attachment needs in early childhood
- Parents may have been: inconsistently responsive, unresponsive, overly anxious, or distracted
- Led to internal working model (attachment map) of expecting to be overattuned to others and not attuned enough to oneself
- Resulted in outside-in orientation: focusing on others' states of mind at the expense of own
- Consequences include:
  - Difficulty managing emotions, leading to high anxiety or anger
  - Interference with self-development and exploration
  - Compulsive caretaking

**Causes:**

- Parental stresses, work responsibilities, or distractions
- Repeating of unmet attachment needs from parents' own experiences
- Overwhelm or inability to read child's signals
- Enlisting child to focus on parent's emotional needs instead of their own

**Effects:**

- Difficulty being attuned to one's own emotional experience
- Focusing too much on others' states of mind
- Overinvolvement in others' experiences and wants/needs
- Difficulty managing emotions, leading to high anxiety or anger
- Interference with self-development and exploration
- Compulsive caretaking

**Goal:**

- Develop a new, positive internal working model of relationships
- Inside-out orientation: awareness of one's own feelings and wants/needs
- Balanced and truly mutual relationships
- Strong, independent sense of self
- Encouragement of exploration and discovery

**Creating Ideal Parent Figures:**

- Deeply interested in individual
- Attuned to individual's needs
- Capable of providing right responsiveness in various situations
- Calm individuals when anxious
- Encourage curiosity and exploration while being supportive
- Help create new possibilities for self-expression and growth.

### Psychoeducation About Disorganized Attachment

**Disorganized Attachment:**

- Category identified in Adult Attachment Interview
- Result of unmet attachment needs in early childhood
- Causes:
  * Prolonged separations
  * Parents as frightening, confusing, or disconnected
  * Caregivers described as aggressive, disruptive, loud, or fearful
  * Parents felt overwhelmed with responsibilities and unable to respond consistently
  * Unresolved loss or trauma affecting parenting abilities

**Effects of Disorganized Attachment:**

- Both source of security and threat for the child
- Impossible dilemma: needing safety and comfort vs. fear of harm
- Fear and mistrust around people, increased fear arousal
- Unsuccessfully seeking safety in attachment relationships
- Inconsistent attachment behaviors: disconnecting or intensely clinging
- Interpersonally described as bossy, compulsively caretaking, or fearful
- Internal experience lacks coherence and may include dissociative experiences

**Dissociative Experiences:**

- Sense of being "out of it"
- Rapidly shifting between different aspects or parts of the self
- Shifting between different mood states (frightened, angry, helpless)
- Temporarily losing connection to where one is
- Periods of time that can't be remembered or accounted for

### Our overall goal is to help you to develop a new, different, positive internal working model of relationships so that you will no longer find yourself

**Text Summary:**

* The goal is to help individuals develop a new, positive internal working model of relationships by creating "ideal parent figures" (IPFs) through imagination.
* IPFs are safe, strong, predictable, and encouraging parents who provide security, attunement, soothing, expressed delight, and support for best self-development.
* Address treatment-frame problems with a mutual understanding of how infractions will be handled.
* Early in treatment, address noncollaborative nonverbal and verbal behavior to promote collaborative communication.
* Introduce IPF protocol:
  * Clarify difference between memory and imagination.
  * Use imagination to create ideal parents who provide basic felt attachment security.
  * Gradually introduce qualities consistent with the five primary conditions that promote secure attachment.
  * Gradually individualize IPF qualities based on patient's attachment prototype and experiences with early caregivers.

**Creating Ideal Parent Figures:**

* Develop new, positive internal working model of relationships.
* Use imagination to create safe, strong, predictable, and encouraging parents.
* IPFs provide security, attunement, soothing, expressed delight, and support for best self-development.

**Treatment Frame:**

* Address treatment-frame problems with a mutual understanding of how infractions will be handled.
* Use a written treatment-frame contract for clarity and to prevent misunderstandings.

**Nonverbal Communication:**

* Early in treatment, address noncollaborative nonverbal behavior.
* Foster metacognitive awareness of nonverbal types of noncollaborative behavior.
* Carefully match and change spontaneous nonverbal communication to promote greater collaboration.

**Verbal Communication:**

* Early in treatment, address noncollaborative verbal behavior.
* Encourage ongoing exploration and clarification of attachment themes for dismissive patients.
* Encourage staying on topic and making points succinctly for anxious-preoccupied patients.
* Frequently clarify communications for disorganized patients to promote metacognitive relating of variables.

**Introducing Ideal Parent Figures:**

* Clarify difference between memory and imagination.
* Use imagination to create ideal parents who are ideally suited to patient's nature and needs.
* Establish inner conditions for imagery, including calm and relaxed body focus and feeling like a very young child.
* Imagine different set of parents who provide basic felt attachment security.
* Address resistances and difficulties with the basic protocol.

**Subsequent Ideal Parent Figure Sessions:**

* Introduce qualities consistent with the five primary conditions that promote secure attachment.
* Emphasize particular conditions needed by the patient based on their attachment prototype.
* Gradually individualize IPF qualities based on experiences with early caregivers and etiological factors of insecure attachment type.

### Therefore, shape the IPF imagery to include calm, comforting, and predictable behavior; provision of a safe haven; being fully present and real; caref

**IPF Imagery and Interactions:**

* Shape IPF imagery to include calm, comforting, and predictable behavior
* Provide a safe haven and be fully present and real
* Carefully attune to patient's fear or mistrust of attachment
* Consistently soothe acute or chronic fear arousal
* Respond contingently
* Show fierce protectiveness from harm or abuse
* Set appropriate limits during aggressive arousal episodes
* Encourage both attachment and exploratory behavior (Chapters 8, 11, 12, and 13)

**Metacognitive Skills:**

* Support basic and intermediate skills for each attachment type:
  * Dismissing: Enhance identification of state of mind and contextual effects
  * Preoccupied: Foster mentalizing stance
  * Disorganized: Enhance mentalizing and reflective capacity (Chapters 7, 9, 11, 12, and 13)
* Gradually introduce later intermediate and advanced skills:
  * Dismissing: Awareness of contextual effects, wider perspective, interconnectedness
  * Preoccupied: Metacognitive awareness of state organization and metacognitive orientation
  * Disorganized: Metacognitive awareness of state organization and orientation, wider perspective (Chapters 7, 9, 11, 12, and 13)
* Toward later phases, introduce advanced skills: interconnectedness, central guiding purpose, sensitivity to knowledge acquisition (Chapters 7 and 9)

**Treatment Progress:**

* Notice, point out, and discuss signs of treatment progress and success with the patient (Chapter 15)

**Later Phases:**

* Toward later phases, promote self-development and secure intimacy to consolidate gains and encourage healthy exploratory behavior outside therapy (Appendices A and B)
* Developing a self-report measure of mentalizing, the "Reflective Function Questionnaire" (not available at time of book preparation)

### CHAPTER 15 Treatment Outcomes Indicators of Successful Treatment

**Chapter 15: Treatment Outcomes**

**Indicators of Successful Treatment**

1. **Achievement of earned secure attachment:**
   - Change from insecure (Ds1, Ds2, Ds3, E1, E2, E3, CC) to secure (F1, F2, F3, F4, F5) attachment as measured by the Adult Attachment Interview (AAI).
2. **Formation of a new, positive stable internal working model for healthy adult attachment:**
   - Development of a new representation for healthy attachment through ideal parent figure imagery and secure therapeutic relationship.
   - Balance between attachment and exploratory behaviors in relationships with therapist and outside therapy.
3. **Capacity for adult secure intimacy:**
   - Development of an internal working model for what a secure intimate relationship would be.
   - Making partner choices and engaging in behaviors that match the new positive map of secure intimacy.
4. **Manifestation of metacognitive skills:**
   - Increase in general metacognitive capacity using Reflective Functioning Scale (RF-S).
   - Assessment of specific metacognitive skills using Metacognitive Assessment Scale (MAS).
5. **Healthy collaborative behavior:**
   - Adherence to treatment-frame behaviors outlined in the treatment contract.
   - Increases in nonverbal and verbal collaborative behavior.
6. **Achievement of high coherence of transcript and coherence of mind on the AAI:**
   - Significant increase in coherence of transcript and coherence of mind based on AAI transcripts.
7. **Resolution of unresolved status (Ud) with respect to trauma and loss:**
   - Change from clinically significant Ud score above 5.5 to below the cutting score of 5.5 by the end of treatment.
8. **No longer meeting diagnostic criteria for personality disorder, dissociative disorder, or any trauma-related diagnosis in DSM-5 or ICD-10:**
   - Many patients with attachment disturbances no longer meet diagnostic criteria for these disorders by the end of Three Pillars attachment-based treatment.

### A Case Illustration of Successful Treatment and Its Measurement

**Case Illustration:** A 46-year-old woman with a history of severe childhood abuse sought consultation after nine years of psychodynamically based trauma treatment. She met SCID-D criteria for Dissociative Identity Disorder (DID) and had extremely low memory suggestibility.

**Background:** Five years into the treatment, she began reporting memories of physical and sexual abuse by her father, leading to a focus on trauma processing. Memories expanded to include siblings and adult friends, resulting in increased dissociation and alter personality states. Her previous therapist noted her inability to bond with her children and lack of mention of her mother for five years.

**Assessment:** Initial assessment using the Attachment Interview (AAI), SCID-D, and Trauma Symptom Inventory (TSI) revealed a CC/Ud classification with unresolved status for childhood physical and sexual abuse and loss of father as an adult. She met full DID criteria on the SCID-D. Her pre-treatment DIS-Q scores were in the range predictive of DDNOS but lower than expected for full DID. Her TSI profile showed a clear mixture of hyperarousal and dissociative trauma symptoms.

**Treatment:** Given her diagnosis, treatment focused on resolving disorganized attachment according to the Three Pillars model without trauma processing. After two and a half years, she was re-assessed and showed significant improvement:

* AAI: Overall coherence score increased from 1.0 to 7.0; able to give all five adjectives for early childhood relationship with mother and provide clear memories. Idealization of mother continued but not clinically significant.
* SCID-D: No evidence of alter personality states, loss of executive control, or current dissociative amnesia.
* TSI: All scores slightly above normal range but below clinically significant levels for all trauma symptoms.
* DIS-Q: Post-treatment scores showed a drop in all dissociative features.

**Conclusion:** Successful treatment of DID without trauma processing, as measured by various assessments. The patient's disorganized attachment was resolved using the Three Pillars model.

### FIGURE 15.1. Pre-and Post-treatment Response on the Dissociation Questionnaire With respect to trauma symptoms, the post-treatment TSI revealed that a

**Case Study:** A patient underwent attachment-based therapy without trauma processing or depression-focused interventions.

**Trauma Symptoms:**
- Pre-treatment Trauma Symptom Inventory (TSI): All five clinical scales above cutting score, all validity scales within normal range.
- Post-treatment TSI: All clinical scales fell below cutting score.
- Figure 15.2 shows pre- and post-treatment TSI profiles.

**Depression:**
- Pre-treatment Beck Depression Inventory (BDI-1) score: Moderate-to-severe depression (21).
- Post-treatment BDI-1 score: Asymptomatic range (7).
- Figure 15.3 shows the drop in depression.

**Findings:**
- Significant reduction in dissociative, traumatic, and depressive symptoms and disorders.
- Attachment-based treatment increased organization of mind, leading to positive effects on trauma-related symptoms independently of trauma processing or depression focus.

**Implications:**
- Traditional phase-oriented trauma treatment may not be effective for patients with complex trauma and disorganized attachment due to potential interference with treatment progress and resolution.
- The Three Pillars attachment-based treatment can resolve disorganized attachment, dissociative symptoms, traumatic symptoms, and depressive symptoms without specific interventions.

### A Pilot Outcome Study

**Three Pillars Treatment: A Pilot Outcome Study**

* The study involved 12 patients treated by the book's authors, with pre- and post-treatment Attachment Anxiety and Avoidance Inventory (AAI) assessments.
* Interscorer agreement for AAIs was 100% between two scorers.
* Pre-treatment AAIs: 67.6% received "cannot classify" (CC), 33.3% received "preoccupied" (E) classification, and 50% had unresolved status with respect to trauma or loss.
* Average pre-treatment AAI coherence was very low at 2.21.
* All patients achieved earned secure status on the AAI after an average of 3.4 years of treatment.
* Coherence of transcript significantly increased from 2.21 to 7.91 (p < 0.001, d = 6.23).
* Reflective functioning scores also improved significantly from 1.68 to 4.36 (p < 0.001, d = 2.34).
* The Three Pillars treatment is effective in fostering metacognition and attachment repair.
* This pilot study suggests that the Three Pillars treatment is not a short-term intervention but can lead to significant improvements in attachment security over an extended period.
* The findings illustrate the efficacy of the approach, with detailed descriptions intended to stimulate larger-scale outcomes research.

**Three Pillars Treatment Model**

* Integration of best principles and practices for addressing attachment disturbances in adults.
* Respects the importance of the patient's relationship with the therapist as a secure base.
* Includes specific methods targeting deficiencies and problematic patterns in adult patients.
* Ideal Parent Figure (IPF) method:
  * Remaps new, positive attachment representations.
  * Patients create an "ideal" internal model of attachment that best fits their needs.
  * Therapist relies on the patient's inner knowing and imagination to shape the model.
* IPF method can be challenging for patients but leads to rich and deep experiences of ideal care.
* Therapist does not have to embody all that each patient needs; instead, they help shape the patient's imagination.

### The “real” aspects of the therapist are bound to contribute to periodic treatment ruptures. When understood and responded to according to the principl

**Three Pillars Model of Therapy**

- Periodic treatment ruptures are inevitable due to the therapist's "real" aspects but can be resolved using this model
- No focus on painful past experiences, except during initial evaluation
- Co-create an imagined childhood with positive experiences
- Work in present, focusing on metacognitive and collaborative experiences
- Address pain from past experiences within the context of one or more pillars
- Trauma history is addressed when sufficient structure for affect regulation is built
- Development of the Three Pillars model draws from essential components of maternal responsiveness

**Three Pillars**

1. **Metacognitive Pillar:**
   - Therapist's attuned presence and contingent responsiveness to patient's metacognitive functioning
   - Use of affective marking to help build recognition and identification of inner states
   - Fosters development of metacognitive capacity
2. **Collaborative, Cooperative Pillar:**
   - Therapist's careful, attuned presence and responsiveness to patient's nonverbal and verbal experience
   - Enhances collaborative, cooperative capacity
3. **Ideal Parent Figure:**
   - Consistently and accurately attuned to patient's behavior, internal state, and developmental level
   - Recognizes when soothing is needed and provides it effectively
   - Responds with expressed delight to patient's being and behaviors
   - Provides attuned and responsive support for exploration

**Effects of Three Pillars Model:**

- Rapid pace of change for patients
- Relief from pain, greater self-confidence and agency
- Better, more satisfying and fulfilling relational experiences
- Effectiveness proven through clinical experience and small pilot study
- Looking forward to future outcome studies and refinements

### Appendix A The Core Self, Proactive Self-Agency, Self-Esteem, and the “Best” Self Normal and Incomplete Self-Development

**Self-Development and the Psychological Sense of Self**

- The psychological sense of self develops gradually from infancy to adulthood.
  - **Neonates**: No predefined or developed sense of self.
  - **Infancy (6-18 months)**: Capacity for self-observation and representational thinking emerges, defining the initial structuralization of the self.
    * Early childhood: Concrete operational thinking leads to an internal sense of mind, rich emotional life, and peer involvement.
    * Adolescence: Formal operational thinking enables handling complex social relationships and infinite possibilities.
  - **Adulthood**: Self evolves as cognitive structures continue to develop.

**Experiential Consequences of a Psychological Sense of Self**

- Central organizing principle for daily life.
  * A clearly defined sense of self is necessary for everyday experiences to be organized around it.
  - Lack of a clear sense of self can lead to trying to define the self from the outside-in.
- Subjective sense of coherence of mind.
  * Provides a direct, immediate sense of internal organization of thoughts and emotions.
  * People with a strong sense of self experience their internal world as organized; those without it may feel disorganized.
- Sense of continuity across time, place, and state.
  * A person with a strong sense of self feels like the same person throughout developmental stages.
  * Lack of self-development can result in feelings of constant change and instability.

**Weak or Arrested Self-Development**

- Three forms: false self, fragile self, and incomplete self.

**False Self**

- Never develops a clear sense of the self and defines it from the outside-in.
- Presents the self accordingly to read others for cues about expectations.
- Example: Chauncey Gardiner (Being There).

**Fragile Self**

- Experiences some degree of personal self but keeps it deeply hidden due to fear of negative reactions from others.
- Fears destruction of the private self.

**Incomplete Self**

- Shows some degree of self-development but remains incomplete and fragile due to early learned chronic hypervigilance about others' states and needs.
- Attends to and responds to others at the expense of the self.
- Example: Leonard Zelig (Zelig).

**Implications for Psychotherapy**

- Therapy can help individuals develop a stronger sense of self by encouraging self-observation, reflection, and self-assertiveness.
- Understanding weak or arrested self-development can aid in tailoring therapeutic interventions to specific needs.

### The foundational task for self-development is the establishment of a core experiential sense of self. From this core emerges self-definition, or the p

**Self-Development and the Core Experiential Sense of Self**

- Establishing a core experiential sense of self is foundational for self-development.
- The core self is the foundation for self-definition, which are consistent and stable aspects of the self.
- Evoking the core self can be done at any time and is sometimes referred to as the "feeling of selfness."
- Object relations theory suggests this ability comes from evocative memory.
- Self-agency emerges from the core self, allowing individuals to feel effective and have impact on others and the world.
- Lack of self-agency results in difficulty being effective and little awareness of impact on others.
- Self-esteem is the linking of positive affect with the self-representation.
- Healthy self-esteem allows for a full sense of one's best self to develop.
- Therapeutic interpretation of deficits in self-development is not effective, instead focus on:
  - Systematic introduction and repetition of positive imagery
  - Cultivating each dimension of self: core self, self-definition, self-agency, self-esteem, best self.

**Dimensions of Self**

* **Core Self:** foundation for self-development, can be evoked at any time
* **Self-Definition:** consistent and stable aspects of the self
* **Self-Agency:** feeling effective and having impact on others and the world
* **Self-Esteem:** linking positive affect with the self-representation
* **Best Self:** full sense of one's best self develops once self-esteem is stable.

**Methods for Promoting Self-Development**

- Therapeutic interpretation of deficits in self-development is not effective.
- Focus on systematic introduction and repetition of positive imagery to enhance each dimension of self.

### Methods for Developing the Core Sense of Self When a patient does not have a core sense of self and lacks clarity about self-definition, self-developm

**Core Sense of Self Development Protocol**

* Step 1: Scene Generation for Core Self
  * Therapist introduces suggestions to help patient define qualities of positive core self through scene visualization
  * Instructions can be varied to prompt imagination and establish a sense of core self
* Step 2: Defining and Enumerating Unique Qualities
  * Patient reflects on scene to discover specific core self qualities
  * Therapist supports conditions for discovery and exploration, free from expectations
* Step 3: Amplification of Self-Qualities
  * Therapist mirrors, emphasizes, and amplifies reported qualities to strengthen patient's experience
* Step 4: Stabilization and Maintenance of Self-Qualities
  * Therapist helps patient recognize that core self-qualities are always present and stable
  * Uses time distortion techniques to enhance recognition and familiarity
* Step 5: Transferring Self-Definitions to Other Contexts
  * Patient imagines embodying named qualities in new scenes and situations
  * Therapist mirrors, amplifies, and supports patient's experience of self-qualities in various contexts

### COMMON PROBLEMS IN SELF-DEFINITION DEVELOPMENT IN PATIENTS WITH SEVERE SELF-DEFICITS For patients with significant self-deficits, particular problems 

**Self-Definition Development in Patients with Severe Self-Deficits**

* Patients with significant self-deficits may encounter various problems during self-definition development:
  * Inability to attend and observe themselves
  * Poor self-observational ability
  * Difficulty recognizing core self during imagination task
  * Focus on others' expectations
  * False self-expression
  * Guarded private self
  * Negative self-talk

**Inattention and Poor Self-Observation**

* Patients with attention deficits require concentration training before self-definition exercises.
* Exercises to develop self-observation and metacognitive reflection: behavioral self-monitoring, journal writing.

**Difficulty Recognizing Core Self**

* Suggest patients become fully present with themselves and focus on immediate sensory experience.
* Encourage awareness of core self as a whole or specific qualities.

**Focus on Others' Expectations**

* Patients should find their deeper sense of self beyond others' expectations.
* They don't need to be concerned about what others want them to be like.

**False Self-Expression**

* Authentic self-expression is accompanied by congruent affect.
* Emphasize authenticity of self-qualities.

**Guarded Private Self**

* Suggest patients share qualities they never show to anyone else or things they value about themselves.

**Negative Self-Talk**

* Patients should not engage with negative thoughts but let them go immediately upon noticing them.
* Negative thoughts can be treated as an old acquaintance and ignored.
* The pattern of negative self-talk will eventually settle down when no longer engaged with.

### Methods for Developing Proactive Self-Agency The next task of self-development, beyond self-definition, is the development of self-agency. Self-agency

**Self-Agency Development**

* Internal representation of being an active, effective agent in the world
* Developed through mastery and self-efficacy experiences
* Emerges during "practicing" period of early childhood with motor development and language use
* Contributes to a sense of making the world happen rather than the world happening to us

**Characteristics of Healthy Self-Agency**

* Proactive: able to assess needs and organize behavior toward goals
* Good time organization
* Little internal contradiction between goal-states
* Strong sense of self-efficacy and mastery
* Active stance toward external events and internal state

**Characteristics of Lack of Self-Agency**

* Ineffective and helpless
* Passive-dependent: letting others define goals and means
* Impulsive: lack of internal action-planning
* Inability to predict causes and effects of behavior over time
* High internal conflict between goal-states
* World happens to them rather than contributing to making it happen

**Scene Generation for Proactive Self-Agency and Interpersonal Self-Agency**

* Therapist prompts for scenes focusing on self-agency and interpersonal self-agency
* Encourages description of what's happening and feelings during effective scenes
* Importance of cultivating strong interpersonal self-agency
* Application of amplification, stabilization, maintenance, and context-transferring principles to patient's responses.

### DEVELOPMENT OF GOAL-SETTING AND ACTION PLANS Another important component of working with patients with underdeveloped self-agency is fostering the dev

**Goal-Setting and Action Plans**

* Fostering development in patients with underdeveloped self-agency
* Process begins with goal identification
  * Patient identifies specific, realistic goal
  * Therapist helps patient connect to core sense of self if needed
* Development of action-planning process
  * Patient develops clear plan to achieve goal
  * Organizes sequence of steps in behavioral action plan
  * Rehearses action plan internally for mastery and effectiveness
  * Estimates realistic time frame for achieving goal
  * Daily evaluates progress toward goal
* Exploring causes and effects of behavior (for impulsive patients)
  * Reflects on past impulsive behaviors and their consequences
    * Short-term and long-term effects
    * Desired vs. undesired outcomes
  * Fosters metacognitive awareness for predicting consequences over time
    * Predicts possible outcomes of impulsive behavior
    * Rates likelihood of each outcome on a scale
    * Alters behavior to change outcome and increase desirability
  * Develops internal probabilistic representations of likely outcomes of specific behaviors, reducing impulsiveness.

### REDUCING CONTRADICTORY GOAL-STATES Emmons (1999) found that human goal-strivings are complex and that personal strivings typically entail a variety of

**Reducing Contradictory Goal-States**

* Emmons (1999): Human goal-strivings are complex, with personal strivings typically involving overlapping and contradictory goals.
* Healthiest individuals show least internal contradiction across goals and experience comfortable self-agency.
* Those with intense, competing contradictions become inhibited in areas of their lives due to uncomfortable affect associated with conflicting goal-states.
* Two ways to reduce contradiction and resulting conflicting goal-states in therapy:
  * Prioritize goals based on importance or significance to core self and develop action plans toward them.
    * Diminishing less important competing goals leads to less internal contradiction.
  * Articulate a clear, central guiding purpose or ultimate concern to subsume all goals and strivings.
    * Helping patients identify their ultimate concerns can point them in the direction of reducing conflicting goal-states over time.

**Being Proactive**

* Developing maturity in self-agency includes a proactive stance.
* Patients can anticipate outcomes and respond proactively to keep action plans on track.
* Foster a proactive stance by encouraging reflection on desired outcomes, predicting responses, and planning accordingly.

**Methods for Developing Healthy Self-Esteem**

* Sandler's definition: Self-esteem is the developmental linkage of positive emotional states to self-representation.
* Consistent expressed delight from attachment figures during childhood is crucial for healthy self-esteem development.
  * Parents openly expressing positive feelings toward their child.
  * Positive emotional expression pervades interactions and internalized by the child, leading to healthy self-esteem.
* Repeated experiences of mastery in childhood contribute to healthy self-esteem.
  * Mastery leads to greater self-esteem but can be used as an overcompensation for absent positive mirroring.
* Structure and consistent limit-setting are essential for older children's self-esteem development.
* Self-psychology approach to psychotherapy focuses on reducing negative interactions and vulnerability, but does not directly address the development of positive associations to the self-representation.
* Positive self-esteem protocol: Directly and systematically evoke positive feeling states in conjunction with the self-representation within and across sessions until patients can do so most of the time in everyday life.
  * Components include situation-based self-esteem enhancement, enhancement of self-esteem in relationships, skill development for quicker positive self-esteem evocation, stress challenge, and integrating self-esteem as a way of being.

### SITUATION-BASED SELF-ESTEEM ENHANCEMENT Even people with chronically impaired self-esteem are able to think of situations where they feel good about t

**Situation-Based Self-Esteem Enhancement**

* People with impaired self-esteem can think of situations where they feel good about themselves
* Positive self-esteem experiences linked to activity and mastery in certain situations
* Protocol begins with scene generation: therapist asks patient to imagine a situation where they're feeling especially good about themselves
* Focus on affect amplification to help patient intensify positive feelings
* Extend duration of positive self-feelings through direct suggestions or generating multiple scenes
* Patient practices imagining situations that evoke positive self-feelings and focusing on them as homework

**Enhancing Self-Esteem in the Context of Relationships**

* More difficult for patients to evoke positive self-feelings in relationships than activities
* Therapist asks patient to imagine a situation where they're feeling especially good about themselves in relation to others or being respected/appreciated by them
* Apply methods for affect amplification and extending duration of positive self-states as before

**Skill Development**

* Develop skill in generating positive self-feelings quickly and for longer durations, and generalizing learning across contexts
* Rapidly evoke positive self-states: therapist helps patient access positive feelings about themselves more quickly
* Extend duration of positive self-states: patient holds positive feelings for longer periods within and outside therapy sessions
* Generalize positive self-feelings to other situations: patient brings positive feeling into new situations not normally associated with it
* Use anchoring scale to promote metacognitive awareness and evaluate progress
* Patient's goal is to sustain positive self-feelings throughout most of the day, nearly every day. Eventually, patient takes on task of maintaining good feelings about themselves daily between sessions in all situations.

### STRESS CHALLENGE The next step is to maintain positive self-feelings in situations that the patient has associated with strong negative self-feelings.

**Stress Challenge:**

- Therapist helps patient maintain positive self-feelings in previously challenging situations.
- Patient imagines feeling good about themselves in difficult situations.
- Therapist uses techniques to amplify positive states and extend their duration.
- Patient is encouraged to test progress by putting themselves in challenging situations.

**Integrating Self-Esteem as a Way of Being:**

- Positive self-esteem becomes a way of being, not just an activity.
- Therapist encourages patient to imagine scenes of contentment and self-acceptance.
- Patient is encouraged to focus on being valued without needing to do anything.

**Developing the Best Self:**

- Therapist helps patient cultivate their best self, representing their strongest and most unique sense of self.
- Patient generates scenes of experiencing their best self and enhances awareness of its qualities.
- Patient transfers "best-self state of mind" to different situations for improved experiences.

**Best-Self Method:**

- Originally a hypnotic method developed by Erika Fromm.
- Used extensively in couples therapy and performance excellence training.
- Involves imagining a situation that evokes one's best self and transferring it to current situations for improved experiences.

**Fostering Uniqueness of Self and Vital Engagement:**

- Parents are encouraged to support their child's discovery and strengthening of uniqueness, free from expectations.
- Therapist suggests imagining scenes that foster vital engagement in life with enthusiasm and passion.

### Self-Development From an Attachment Perspective The self-development protocol described in this appendix emphasizes the developmental line of self as 

**Self-Development from an Attachment Perspective**

- Self-development protocol focuses on the development of core-self experience and definition, self-agency, and self-esteem independently of attachment relationships.
- Included as an appendix for clinicians to learn separately from attachment development.
- For patients with personality and dissociative disorders, focus on both self-development and positive attachment representations.
- Principles for self-development applied in the context of ideal parent figure (IPF) imagery:
  - **Patients with little or weak self-development:**
    - Encourage development of a strong, autonomous, and unique sense of self within secure-base experience of IPFs.
    - Reinforce contingency responsiveness, interpersonal self-agency, organized goal-directed behaviors, and metacognitive awareness.
  - **Patients with poorly developed self-agency:**
    - Shape IPFs to encourage response-eliciting behaviors and support development of goal-directed actions.
    - Collaboratively engage in metacognitive awareness of consequences.
  - **Patients with chronic low self-esteem:**
    - Emphasize repetition of scenes of parental delight and support for competence and mastery.
- Clinicians are encouraged to review Chapters 7, 8, 11, 12, and 13 for further information on IPF imagery process.

### Appendix B Protocols for Developing Adult Secure Intimacy in Individuals and Caring Behaviors in Couples

**Secure Intimacy and Attachment**

- Secure attachment leads to healthy exploratory behavior in individuals (Bowlby)
- Adult romantic relationships can be attachment relationships if they manifest five conditions: safety/protection, attunement, soothing, expressed delight, and fostering self-development
- Secure attachment relationships promote secure intimacy, allowing exploration, growth, and discovery of unique selves

**Promoting Secure Intimacy in Psychotherapy**

- Once a patient achieves secure attachment status, therapy can focus on developing secure intimacy
- Patients create a vision for secure romantic attachment using five main conditions and individualized positive descriptors
- Hypnotherapy method of future-time orientation helps patients construct a vision of adult romantic secure intimacy
- Therapist mirrors, enhances, and encourages elaboration of elements described by the patient
- Patient holds awareness on total felt sense of secure intimacy vision and fixes it in mind as a guide for future relationships.

### ARTICULATING THE FIVE PRIMARY CONDITIONS THAT PROMOTE SECURE INTIMACY

**Secure Intimacy: Five Primary Conditions**

The therapist suggests focusing on five primary conditions that promote secure intimacy:

1. **Feeling Safe and Protected:**
   - Partner provides a sense of safety and protection in the relationship.
   - Patient describes specific ways partner achieves this.
   - Results in a positive state of mind.

2. **Careful Attunement:**
   - Partner is attentive to patient's needs, feelings, and internal state.
   - Patient describes scene and partner's actions.
   - Results in a secure feeling to discover and explore new ways.

3. **Comfort and Reassurance:**
   - Partner comforts and reassures the patient during emotional upsets.
   - Patient describes specific ways partner achieves this.
   - Results in a positive state of mind for both partners.

4. **Delight and Appreciation:**
   - Partner expresses delight and appreciation for the patient's being.
   - Patient describes specific ways partner shows this.
   - Results in a boosted self-esteem and positive relationship dynamics.

5. **Continuous Encouragement and Support:**
   - Partner encourages and supports the patient to bring out their best self.
   - Patient describes specific ways partner does this.
   - Results in personal growth and development within the relationship.

The patient is encouraged to visualize these scenes and describe the partner's actions, noticing the effects on their state of mind and the relationship.

### INDIVIDUALIZING THE SECURE INTIMACY PROTOCOL

**Individualizing the Secure Intimacy Protocol**

* Shape vision of secure intimacy for each patient based on early caregiver relationships
* Use information from Adult Attachment Interview (AAI) and other available data
* Translate negative adjectives into positive opposites for developing secure attachment to ideal parent figures
* Use positive descriptors in development of adult secure intimacy imagery
* Individualize protocol based on patient's specific attachment needs:
  * Comfort with physical closeness: develop image of balanced relationship, ease with relying on partner, etc.
  * Difficulty relying on others or asking for help: ability to ask for advice/help, turn to partner in times of need, etc.
  * Mutuality: conviction that partner cares as much, comfort expressing emotions, etc.
  * Difficulty opening up emotionally: ease discussing problems with partner, comfortable expressing wide range of emotions, etc.
* For patients who experienced love and acceptance as conditional: imagine a relationship where you can be yourself and loved for who you are.

### Caring Behaviors in Couples

**Caring Behaviors in Couples:**

**Importance of Caring Behaviors in Romantic Relationships:**
- Patients in romantic relationships may feel confused by protocols suggesting future secure intimacy
- Current relationship and vision of secure intimacy may differ, which could undermine the relationship
- Restoration or development of healthy adult romantic secure intimacy is essential for optimal romance, caring, and relational satisfaction

**Background:**
- Couples therapists help couples with erosion of romance and caring behaviors
- Harville Hendrix developed Imago Relationship Therapy (IRT) to foster caring behaviors
- IRT based on attachment theory, object relations theory, developmental psychology, behavioral change techniques, and neuroscience

**IRT Process for Developing Caring Behaviors:**

**1. Current Caring Behaviors:**
- In a session with both partners, therapist asks each to list current caring behaviors of their partner using stem sentences
- Each person shares their list with the other while the other mirrors what was heard

**2. Early Relationship Caring Behaviors:**
- Each member of the couple visualizes and lists caring behaviors from early in the relationship
- They share their lists with each other and mirror what was shared

**3. Ideal Future Caring Behaviors:**
- Each partner imagines their ideal future caring behaviors and lists them using stem sentences
- They share their lists with each other and mirror what was shared

**Repeating the Exercise:**
- The three-part exercise can be repeated several times across sessions to foster restoration and development of caring behaviors and secure intimacy.
